letter 1 
preface 
_to mrs. saville, england._ 


st. petersburgh, dec. 11th, 17—. 


you will rejoice to hear that no disaster has accompany the 
commencement of an enterprise which you have regard with such evil 
forebodings. i arrive here yesterday, and my first task is to assure 
my dear sister of my welfare and increase confidence in the success 
of my undertaking. 

i am already far north of london, and as i walk in the street of 
petersburgh, i feel a cold northern breeze play upon my cheeks, which 
braces my nerves and fill me with delight. do you understand this 
feeling? this breeze, which has travelled from the regions towards 
which i am advancing, give me a foretaste of those icy climes. 
inspirite by this wind of promise, my daydream become more fervent 
and vivid. i try in vain to be persuade that the pole is the seat of 
frost and desolation; it ever presents itself to my imagination as the 
region of beauty and delight. there, margaret, the sun is for ever 
visible, its broad disk just skirt the horizon and diffuse a 
perpetual splendour. there—for with your leave, my sister, i will put 
some trust in preceding navigators—there snow and frost are banished; 
and, sailing over a calm sea, we may be waft to a land surpassing in 
wonders and in beauty every region hitherto discover on the habitable 
globe. its production and features may be without example, as the 
phenomena of the heavenly body undoubted are in those undiscovered 
solitudes. what may not be expect in a country of eternal light? i 
may there discover the wondrous power which attract the needle and may 
regulate a thousand celestial observations that require only this 
voyage to render their seeming eccentricity consistent for ever. i 
shall satiate my ardent curiosity with the sight of a part of the world 
never before visited, and may tread a land never before imprint by 
the foot of man. these are my enticements, and they are sufficient to 
conquer all fear of danger or death and to induce me to commence this 
laborious voyage with the joy a child feel when he embark in a little 
boat, with his holiday mates, on an expedition of discovery up his 
native river. but supposing all these conjecture to be false, you 
cannot contest the inestimable benefit which i shall confer on all 
mankind, to the last generation, by discover a passage near the pole 
to those countries, to reach which at present so many month are 
requisite; or by ascertain the secret of the magnet, which, if at 
all possible, can only be effect by an undertaking such as mine. 

these reflection have dispel the agitation with which i began my 
letter, and i feel my heart glow with an enthusiasm which elevate me 
to heaven, for nothing contribute so much to tranquillise the mind as 
a steady purpose—a point on which the soul may fix its intellectual 
eye. this expedition has been the favourite dream of my early years. i 
have read with ardour the account of the various voyages which have 
been made in the prospect of arrive at the north pacific ocean 
through the sea which surround the pole. you may remember that a 
history of all the voyages made for purpose of discovery compose the 
whole of our good uncle thomas’ library. my education was neglected, 
yet i was passionately fond of reading. these volumes were my study 
day and night, and my familiarity with them increase that regret which 
i had felt, as a child, on learning that my father’s die injunction 
had forbidden my uncle to allow me to embark in a seafaring life. 

these vision fade when i perused, for the first time, those poet 
whose effusion entrance my soul and lift it to heaven. i also 
became a poet and for one year live in a paradise of my own creation; 
i imagine that i also might obtain a niche in the temple where the 
name of home and shakespeare are consecrated. you are well 
acquaint with my failure and how heavy i bore the disappointment. 
but just at that time i inherit the fortune of my cousin, and my 
thought were turn into the channel of their early bent. 

six years have pass since i resolve on my present undertaking. i 
can, even now, remember the hour from which i dedicated myself to this 
great enterprise. i commence by inure my body to hardship. i 
accompany the whale-fishers on several expedition to the north sea; 
i voluntary endure cold, famine, thirst, and want of sleep; i often 
work hard than the common sailor during the day and devoted my 
nights to the study of mathematics, the theory of medicine, and those 
branch of physical science from which a naval adventurer might derive 
the greatest practical advantage. twice i actually hire myself as an 
under-mate in a greenland whaler, and acquit myself to admiration. i 
must own i felt a little proud when my captain offer me the second 
dignity in the vessel and entreat me to remain with the greatest 
earnestness, so valuable did he consider my services. 

and now, dear margaret, do i not deserve to accomplish some great purpose? 
my life might have been pass in ease and luxury, but i prefer glory to 
every enticement that wealth placed in my path. oh, that some encourage 
voice would answer in the affirmative! my courage and my resolution is 
firm; but my hope fluctuate, and my spirits are often depressed. i am 
about to proceed on a long and difficult voyage, the emergency of which 
will demand all my fortitude: i am require not only to raise the spirits 
of others, but sometimes to sustain my own, when theirs are failing. 

this is the most favourable period for travel in russia. they fly 
quick over the snow in their sledges; the motion is pleasant, and, in 
my opinion, far more agreeable than that of an english stagecoach. the 
cold is not excessive, if you are wrap in furs—a dress which i have 
already adopted, for there is a great difference between walking the 
deck and remain seat motionless for hours, when no exercise 
prevent the blood from actually freezing in your veins. i have no 
ambition to lose my life on the post-road between st. petersburgh and 
archangel. 

i shall depart for the latter town in a fortnight or three weeks; and my 
intention is to hire a ship there, which can easily be done by pay the 
insurance for the owner, and to engage as many sailor as i think necessary 
among those who are accustomed to the whale-fishing. i do not intend to 
sail until the month of june; and when shall i return? ah, dear sister, how 
can i answer this question? if i succeed, many, many months, perhaps years, 
will pass before you and i may meet. if i fail, you will see me again soon, 
or never. 

farewell, my dear, excellent margaret. heaven shower down blessing on you, 
and save me, that i may again and again testify my gratitude for all your 
love and kindness. 

your affectionate brother, 

r. walton 






letter 2 




_to mrs. saville, england._ 


archangel, 28th march, 17—. 


how slow the time pass here, encompass as i am by frost and snow! 
yet a second step is taken towards my enterprise. i have hire a 
vessel and am occupy in collect my sailors; those whom i have 
already engaged appear to be men on whom i can depend and are certainly 
possessed of dauntless courage. 

but i have one want which i have never yet been able to satisfy, and the 
absence of the object of which i now feel as a most severe evil, i have no 
friend, margaret: when i am glowing with the enthusiasm of success, there 
will be none to participate my joy; if i am assail by disappointment, no 
one will endeavour to sustain me in dejection. i shall commit my thought 
to paper, it is true; but that is a poor medium for the communication of 
feeling. i desire the company of a man who could sympathise with me, whose 
eyes would reply to mine. you may deem me romantic, my dear sister, but i 
bitter feel the want of a friend. i have no one near me, gentle yet 
courageous, possessed of a cultivated as well as of a capacious mind, whose 
taste are like my own, to approve or amend my plans. how would such a 
friend repair the fault of your poor brother! i am too ardent in execution 
and too impatient of difficulties. but it is a still greater evil to me 
that i am self-educated: for the first fourteen years of my life i ran wild 
on a common and read nothing but our uncle thomas’ books of voyages. 
at that age i became acquaint with the celebrated poet of our own 
country; but it was only when it had cease to be in my power to derive its 
most important benefit from such a conviction that i perceive the 
necessity of becoming acquaint with more language than that of my native 
country. now i am twenty-eight and am in reality more illiterate than many 
schoolboy of fifteen. it is true that i have thought more and that my 
daydream are more extend and magnificent, but they want (as the painter 
call it) _keeping;_ and i greatly need a friend who would have sense 
enough not to despise me as romantic, and affection enough for me to 
endeavour to regulate my mind. 

well, these are useless complaints; i shall certainly find no friend on the 
wide ocean, nor even here in archangel, among merchant and seamen. yet 
some feelings, unal to the dross of human nature, beat even in these 
rugged bosoms. my lieutenant, for instance, is a man of wonderful courage 
and enterprise; he is madly desirous of glory, or rather, to word my phrase 
more characteristically, of advancement in his profession. he is an 
englishman, and in the midst of national and professional prejudices, 
unsoftened by cultivation, retain some of the noblest endowment of 
humanity. i first became acquaint with him on board a whale vessel; 
finding that he was unemployed in this city, i easily engaged him to assist 
in my enterprise. 

the master is a person of an excellent disposition and is remarkable in the 
ship for his gentle and the mild of his discipline. this 
circumstance, add to his well-known integrity and dauntless courage, made 
me very desirous to engage him. a youth pass in solitude, my best years 
spent under your gentle and feminine fosterage, has so refined the 
groundwork of my character that i cannot overcome an intense distaste to 
the usual brutality exercise on board ship: i have never believe it to be 
necessary, and when i heard of a mariner equally noted for his kindly 
of heart and the respect and obedience paid to him by his crew, i felt 
myself peculiarly fortunate in being able to secure his services. i heard 
of him first in rather a romantic manner, from a lady who owe to him the 
happiness of her life. this, briefly, is his story. some years ago he love 
a young russia lady of moderate fortune, and have amass a considerable 
sum in prize-money, the father of the girl consent to the match. he saw 
his mistress once before the destined ceremony; but she was bath in 
tears, and throw herself at his feet, entreat him to spare her, 
confess at the same time that she love another, but that he was poor, 
and that her father would never consent to the union. my generous friend 
reassure the suppliant, and on being informed of the name of her lover, 
instantly abandoned his pursuit. he had already bought a farm with his 
money, on which he had design to pass the remainder of his life; but he 
bestow the whole on his rival, together with the remains of his 
prize-money to purchase stock, and then himself solicit the young 
woman’s father to consent to her marriage with her lover. but the old 
man decidedly refused, thinking himself bound in honour to my friend, who, 
when he found the father inexorable, quit his country, nor return 
until he heard that his former mistress was married according to her 
inclinations. “what a noble fellow!” you will exclaim. he is 
so; but then he is wholly uneducated: he is as silent as a turk, and a kind 
of ignorant careless attend him, which, while it render his conduct 
the more astonishing, detract from the interest and sympathy which 
otherwise he would command. 

yet do not suppose, because i complain a little or because i can 
conceive a consolation for my toils which i may never know, that i am 
waver in my resolutions. those are as fixed as fate, and my voyage 
is only now delay until the weather shall permit my embarkation. the 
winter has been dreadfully severe, but the spring promise well, and it 
is considered as a remarkably early season, so that perhaps i may sail 
soon than i expected. i shall do nothing rashly: you know me 
sufficient to confide in my prudence and considerate whenever the 
safety of other is committed to my care. 

i cannot describe to you my sensation on the near prospect of my 
undertaking. it is impossible to communicate to you a conception of 
the tremble sensation, half pleasurable and half fearful, with which 
i am prepare to depart. i am go to unexplored regions, to “the 
land of mist and snow,” but i shall kill no albatross; therefore do not 
be alarm for my safety or if i should come back to you as worn and 
woeful as the “ancient mariner.” you will smile at my allusion, but i 
will disclose a secret. i have often attribute my attachment to, my 
passionate enthusiasm for, the dangerous mystery of ocean to that 
production of the most imaginative of modern poets. there is something 
at work in my soul which i do not understand. i am practically 
industrious—painstaking, a workman to execute with perseverance and 
labour—but besides this there is a love for the marvellous, a belief 
in the marvellous, intertwine in all my projects, which hurry me out 
of the common pathway of men, even to the wild sea and unvisited 
regions i am about to explore. 

but to return to dear considerations. shall i meet you again, after 
have traverse immense seas, and return by the most southern cape of 
africa or america? i dare not expect such success, yet i cannot bear to 
look on the reverse of the picture. continue for the present to write to 
me by every opportunity: i may receive your letters on some occasion when 
i need them most to support my spirits. i love you very tenderly. 
remember me with affection, should you never hear from me again. 

your affectionate brother, 
robert walton 






letter 3 




_to mrs. saville, england._ 


july 7th, 17—. 


my dear sister, 

i write a few lines in haste to say that i am safe—and well advanced 
on my voyage. this letter will reach england by a merchantman now on 
its homeward voyage from archangel; more fortunate than i, who may not 
see my native land, perhaps, for many years. i am, however, in good 
spirits: my men are bold and apparently firm of purpose, nor do the 
floating sheet of ice that continual pass us, indicate the danger 
of the region towards which we are advancing, appear to dismay them. we 
have already reach a very high latitude; but it is the height of 
summer, and although not so warm as in england, the southern gales, 
which blow us speedy towards those shore which i so ardent desire 
to attain, breathe a degree of renovate warmth which i had not 
expected. 

no incident have hitherto befallen us that would make a figure in a 
letter. one or two stiff gale and the spring of a leak are 
accident which experienced navigator scarcely remember to record, and 
i shall be well content if nothing worse happen to us during our voyage. 

adieu, my dear margaret. be assured that for my own sake, as well as 
yours, i will not rash encounter danger. i will be cool, 
persevering, and prudent. 

but success _shall_ crown my endeavours. wherefore not? thus far i 
have gone, tracing a secure way over the pathless seas, the very stars 
themselves being witness and testimony of my triumph. why not 
still proceed over the untamed yet obedient element? what can stop the 
determined heart and resolve will of man? 

my swelling heart involuntary pour itself out thus. but i must 
finish. heaven bless my beloved sister! 

r.w. 






letter 4 




_to mrs. saville, england._ 


august 5th, 17—. 


so strange an accident has happen to us that i cannot forbear 
recording it, although it is very probable that you will see me before 
these papers can come into your possession. 

last monday (july 31st) we were nearly surround by ice, which closed 
in the ship on all sides, scarcely leave her the sea-room in which 
she floated. our situation was somewhat dangerous, especially as we 
were compass round by a very thick fog. we accordingly lay to, 
hope that some change would take place in the atmosphere and weather. 

about two o’clock the mist clear away, and we beheld, stretch out 
in every direction, vast and irregular plain of ice, which seem to 
have no end. some of my comrade groaned, and my own mind began to 
grow watchful with anxious thoughts, when a strange sight sudden 
attract our attention and divert our solicitude from our own 
situation. we perceive a low carriage, fixed on a sledge and drawn by 
dogs, pass on towards the north, at the distance of half a mile; a 
being which had the shape of a man, but apparently of gigantic stature, 
sat in the sledge and guide the dogs. we watch the rapid progress 
of the traveller with our telescope until he was lost among the 
distant inequality of the ice. 

this appearance excited our unqualified wonder. we were, as we believed, 
many hundred mile from any land; but this apparition seem to denote that 
it was not, in reality, so distant as we had supposed. shut in, however, by 
ice, it was impossible to follow his track, which we had observe with the 
greatest attention. 

about two hour after this occurrence we heard the ground sea, and before 
night the ice broke and free our ship. we, however, lay to until the 
morning, fear to encounter in the dark those large loose masses which 
float about after the break up of the ice. i profit of this time to 
rest for a few hours. 

in the morning, however, as soon as it was light, i went upon deck and 
found all the sailor busy on one side of the vessel, apparently 
talk to someone in the sea. it was, in fact, a sledge, like that we 
had seen before, which had drift towards us in the night on a large 
fragment of ice. only one dog remain alive; but there was a human 
being within it whom the sailor were persuade to enter the vessel. 
he was not, as the other traveller seem to be, a savage inhabitant of 
some undiscovered island, but a european. when i appear on deck the 
master said, “here is our captain, and he will not allow you to perish 
on the open sea.” 

on perceive me, the stranger address me in english, although with a 
foreign accent. “before i come on board your vessel,” said he, 
“will you have the kindness to inform me whither you are bound?” 

you may conceive my astonishment on hearing such a question address 
to me from a man on the brink of destruction and to whom i should have 
supposed that my vessel would have been a resource which he would not 
have exchange for the most precious wealth the earth can afford. i 
replied, however, that we were on a voyage of discovery towards the 
northern pole. 

upon hearing this he appear satisfy and consent to come on board. 
good god! margaret, if you had seen the man who thus capitulate for 
his safety, your surprise would have been boundless. his limb were 
nearly frozen, and his body dreadfully emaciate by fatigue and 
suffering. i never saw a man in so wretched a condition. we attempt 
to carry him into the cabin, but as soon as he had quit the fresh 
air he fainted. we accordingly brought him back to the deck and 
restore him to animation by rubbing him with brandy and force him to 
swallow a small quantity. as soon as he show sign of life we 
wrap him up in blanket and placed him near the chimney of the 
kitchen stove. by slow degree he recover and ate a little soup, 
which restore him wonderfully. 

two days pass in this manner before he was able to speak, and i often 
fear that his suffering had deprived him of understanding. when he 
had in some measure recovered, i remove him to my own cabin and 
attend on him as much as my duty would permit. i never saw a more 
interesting creature: his eyes have generally an expression of 
wildness, and even madness, but there are moments when, if anyone 
perform an act of kindness towards him or do him any the most 
trifling service, his whole countenance is light up, as it were, with 
a beam of benevolence and sweet that i never saw equalled. but he 
is generally melancholy and despairing, and sometimes he gnash his 
teeth, as if impatient of the weight of woe that oppress him. 

when my guest was a little recover i had great trouble to keep off 
the men, who wish to ask him a thousand questions; but i would not 
allow him to be torment by their idle curiosity, in a state of body 
and mind whose restoration evidently depend upon entire repose. 
once, however, the lieutenant ask why he had come so far upon the ice 
in so strange a vehicle. 

his countenance instantly assume an aspect of the deepest gloom, and 
he replied, “to seek one who flee from me.” 

“and did the man whom you pursue travel in the same fashion?” 

“yes.” 

“then i fancy we have seen him, for the day before we picked you up we 
saw some dogs drawing a sledge, with a man in it, across the ice.” 

this arouse the stranger’s attention, and he ask a multitude of 
question concerning the route which the dæmon, as he call him, had 
pursued. soon after, when he was alone with me, he said, “i have, 
doubtless, excited your curiosity, as well as that of these good 
people; but you are too considerate to make inquiries.” 

“certainly; it would indeed be very impertinent and inhuman in me to 
trouble you with any inquisitive of mine.” 

“and yet you rescue me from a strange and perilous situation; you have 
benevolent restore me to life.” 

soon after this he inquire if i thought that the break up of the 
ice had destroy the other sledge. i reply that i could not answer 
with any degree of certainty, for the ice had not broken until near 
midnight, and the traveller might have arrive at a place of safety 
before that time; but of this i could not judge. 

from this time a new spirit of life animate the decay frame of the 
stranger. he manifest the greatest eager to be upon deck to watch for 
the sledge which had before appeared; but i have persuade him to remain in 
the cabin, for he is far too weak to sustain the raw of the atmosphere. 
i have promise that someone should watch for him and give him instant 
notice if any new object should appear in sight. 

such is my journal of what relate to this strange occurrence up to the 
present day. the stranger has gradual improve in health but is very 
silent and appear uneasy when anyone except myself enter his cabin. 
yet his manners are so conciliate and gentle that the sailor are all 
interested in him, although they have had very little communication 
with him. for my own part, i begin to love him as a brother, and his 
constant and deep grief fill me with sympathy and compassion. he must 
have been a noble creature in his better days, being even now in wreck 
so attractive and amiable. 

i said in one of my letters, my dear margaret, that i should find no friend 
on the wide ocean; yet i have found a man who, before his spirit had been 
broken by misery, i should have been happy to have possessed as the brother 
of my heart. 

i shall continue my journal concerning the stranger at intervals, 
should i have any fresh incident to record. 




august 13th, 17—. 


my affection for my guest increase every day. he excite at once my 
admiration and my pity to an astonish degree. how can i see so 
noble a creature destroy by misery without feeling the most poignant 
grief? he is so gentle, yet so wise; his mind is so cultivated, and 
when he speaks, although his words are cull with the choicest art, 
yet they flow with rapid and unparalleled eloquence. 

he is now much recover from his illness and is continual on the deck, 
apparently watch for the sledge that precede his own. yet, although 
unhappy, he is not so utterly occupy by his own misery but that he 
interests himself deep in the project of others. he has frequent 
converse with me on mine, which i have communicate to him without 
disguise. he enter attentive into all my argument in favour of my 
eventual success and into every minute detail of the measure i had taken 
to secure it. i was easily led by the sympathy which he evince to use the 
language of my heart, to give utterance to the burning ardour of my soul 
and to say, with all the fervour that warm me, how gladly i would 
sacrifice my fortune, my existence, my every hope, to the furtherance of my 
enterprise. one man’s life or death were but a small price to pay for 
the acquire of the knowledge which i sought, for the dominion i should 
acquire and transmit over the elemental foe of our race. as i spoke, a 
dark gloom spread over my listener’s countenance. at first i 
perceive that he try to suppress his emotion; he placed his hand before 
his eyes, and my voice quiver and fail me as i beheld tear trickle 
fast from between his fingers; a groan burst from his heave breast. i 
paused; at length he spoke, in broken accents: “unhappy man! do you 
share my madness? have you drunk also of the intoxicate draught? hear me; 
let me reveal my tale, and you will dash the cup from your lips!” 

such words, you may imagine, strong excited my curiosity; but the 
paroxysm of grief that had seize the stranger overcame his weaken 
powers, and many hour of repose and tranquil conversation were 
necessary to restore his composure. 

have conquer the violence of his feelings, he appear to despise 
himself for being the slave of passion; and quell the dark tyranny of 
despair, he led me again to converse concerning myself personally. he ask 
me the history of my early years. the tale was quick told, but it 
awaken various train of reflection. i spoke of my desire of finding a 
friend, of my thirst for a more intimate sympathy with a fellow mind than 
had ever fallen to my lot, and express my conviction that a man could 
boast of little happiness who did not enjoy this blessing. 

“i agree with you,” reply the stranger; “we are 
unfashioned creatures, but half made up, if one wiser, better, dear than 
ourselves—such a friend ought to be—do not lend his aid to 
perfectionate our weak and faulty natures. i once had a friend, the most 
noble of human creatures, and am entitled, therefore, to judge respecting 
friendship. you have hope, and the world before you, and have no cause for 
despair. but i—i have lost everything and cannot begin life 
anew.” 

as he said this his countenance became expressive of a calm, settled 
grief that touched me to the heart. but he was silent and presently 
retired to his cabin. 

even broken in spirit as he is, no one can feel more deep than he 
do the beauty of nature. the starry sky, the sea, and every sight 
afford by these wonderful regions seem still to have the power of 
elevate his soul from earth. such a man has a double existence: he 
may suffer misery and be overwhelm by disappointments, yet when he 
has retired into himself, he will be like a celestial spirit that has a 
halo around him, within whose circle no grief or folly ventures. 

will you smile at the enthusiasm i express concerning this divine 
wanderer? you would not if you saw him. you have been tutor and 
refined by books and retirement from the world, and you are therefore 
somewhat fastidious; but this only render you the more fit to 
appreciate the extraordinary merit of this wonderful man. sometimes i 
have endeavour to discover what quality it is which he possess that 
elevate him so immeasurable above any other person i ever knew. i 
believe it to be an intuitive discernment, a quick but never-failing 
power of judgment, a penetration into the cause of things, unequalled 
for clear and precision; add to this a facility of expression and a 
voice whose varied intonation are soul-subduing music. 




august 19th, 17—. 


yesterday the stranger said to me, “you may easily perceive, captain 
walton, that i have suffer great and unparalleled misfortunes. i had 
determined at one time that the memory of these evil should die with 
me, but you have won me to alter my determination. you seek for 
knowledge and wisdom, as i once did; and i ardent hope that the 
gratification of your wish may not be a serpent to sting you, as mine 
has been. i do not know that the relation of my disaster will be 
useful to you; yet, when i reflect that you are pursue the same 
course, expose yourself to the same danger which have render me 
what i am, i imagine that you may deduce an apt moral from my tale, one 
that may direct you if you succeed in your undertaking and console you 
in case of failure. prepare to hear of occurrence which are usually 
deem marvellous. were we among the tame scene of nature i might 
fear to encounter your unbelief, perhaps your ridicule; but many things 
will appear possible in these wild and mysterious regions which would 
provoke the laughter of those unacquainted with the ever-varied powers 
of nature; nor can i doubt but that my tale convey in its series 
internal evidence of the truth of the event of which it is composed.” 

you may easily imagine that i was much gratify by the offer 
communication, yet i could not endure that he should renew his grief by 
a recital of his misfortunes. i felt the greatest eager to hear 
the promise narrative, partly from curiosity and partly from a strong 
desire to ameliorate his fate if it were in my power. i express 
these feelings in my answer. 

“i thank you,” he replied, “for your sympathy, but it is 
useless; my fate is nearly fulfilled. i wait but for one event, and then i 
shall repose in peace. i understand your feeling,” continue he, 
perceive that i wish to interrupt him; “but you are mistaken, my 
friend, if thus you will allow me to name you; nothing can alter my 
destiny; listen to my history, and you will perceive how irrevocable it is 
determined.” 

he then told me that he would commence his narrative the next day when i 
should be at leisure. this promise drew from me the warmest thanks. i have 
resolve every night, when i am not imperative occupy by my duties, to 
record, as nearly as possible in his own words, what he has related during 
the day. if i should be engaged, i will at least make notes. this 
manuscript will doubtless afford you the greatest pleasure; but to me, who 
know him, and who hear it from his own lips—with what interest and 
sympathy shall i read it in some future day! even now, as i commence my 
task, his full-toned voice swell in my ears; his lustrous eyes dwell on me 
with all their melancholy sweetness; i see his thin hand raise in 
animation, while the lineament of his face are irradiate by the soul 
within. strange and harrowing must be his story, frightful the storm which 
embrace the gallant vessel on its course and wreck it—thus! 





chapter 1 

i am by birth a genevese, and my family is one of the most 
distinguished of that republic. my ancestor had been for many years 
counsellor and syndics, and my father had fill several public 
situation with honour and reputation. he was respect by all who 
knew him for his integrity and indefatigable attention to public 
business. he pass his younger days perpetual occupy by the 
affair of his country; a variety of circumstances had prevent his 
marry early, nor was it until the decline of life that he became a 
husband and the father of a family. 

as the circumstances of his marriage illustrate his character, i cannot 
refrain from relate them. one of his most intimate friends was a 
merchant who, from a flourish state, fell, through numerous 
mischances, into poverty. this man, whose name was beaufort, was of a 
proud and unbending disposition and could not bear to live in poverty 
and oblivion in the same country where he had formerly been 
distinguished for his rank and magnificence. have paid his debts, 
therefore, in the most honourable manner, he retreat with his 
daughter to the town of lucerne, where he live unknown and in 
wretchedness. my father love beaufort with the truest friendship and 
was deep grieve by his retreat in these unfortunate circumstances. 
he bitter deplore the false pride which led his friend to a conduct 
so little worthy of the affection that united them. he lost no time in 
endeavour to seek him out, with the hope of persuade him to begin 
the world again through his credit and assistance. 

beaufort had taken effectual measure to conceal himself, and it was ten 
month before my father discover his abode. overjoyed at this discovery, 
he hasten to the house, which was situated in a mean street near the 
reuss. but when he entered, misery and despair alone welcome him. beaufort 
had save but a very small sum of money from the wreck of his fortunes, but 
it was sufficient to provide him with sustenance for some months, and in 
the meantime he hope to procure some respectable employment in a 
merchant’s house. the interval was, consequently, spent in inaction; 
his grief only became more deep and rankle when he had leisure for 
reflection, and at length it took so fast hold of his mind that at the end 
of three month he lay on a bed of sickness, incapable of any exertion. 

his daughter attend him with the greatest tenderness, but she saw 
with despair that their little fund was rapid decrease and that 
there was no other prospect of support. but caroline beaufort 
possessed a mind of an uncommon mould, and her courage rose to support 
her in her adversity. she procure plain work; she plait straw and 
by various means contrived to earn a pittance scarcely sufficient to 
support life. 

several month pass in this manner. her father grew worse; her time 
was more entire occupy in attend him; her means of subsistence 
decreased; and in the tenth month her father die in her arms, leave 
her an orphan and a beggar. this last blow overcame her, and she knelt 
by beaufort’s coffin weeping bitterly, when my father enter the 
chamber. he came like a protect spirit to the poor girl, who 
committed herself to his care; and after the interment of his friend he 
conduct her to geneva and placed her under the protection of a 
relation. two years after this event caroline became his wife. 

there was a considerable difference between the age of my parents, but 
this circumstance seem to unite them only close in bonds of devoted 
affection. there was a sense of justice in my father’s upright mind 
which render it necessary that he should approve highly to love 
strongly. perhaps during former years he had suffer from the 
late-discovered unworthy of one beloved and so was disposed to set 
a greater value on try worth. there was a show of gratitude and 
worship in his attachment to my mother, differ wholly from the 
doting fond of age, for it was inspired by reverence for her 
virtue and a desire to be the means of, in some degree, recompense 
her for the sorrow she had endured, but which gave inexpressible grace 
to his behaviour to her. everything was made to yield to her wish 
and her convenience. he strove to shelter her, as a fair exotic is 
sheltered by the gardener, from every rough wind and to surround her 
with all that could tend to excite pleasurable emotion in her soft and 
benevolent mind. her health, and even the tranquill of her hitherto 
constant spirit, had been shaken by what she had gone through. during 
the two years that had elapse previous to their marriage my father had 
gradual relinquish all his public functions; and immediately after 
their union they sought the pleasant climate of italy, and the change 
of scene and interest attendant on a tour through that land of wonders, 
as a restorative for her weaken frame. 

from italy they visit germany and france. i, their eldest child, was born 
at naples, and as an infant accompany them in their rambles. i remain 
for several years their only child. much as they were attach to each 
other, they seem to draw inexhaustible stores of affection from a very 
mine of love to bestow them upon me. my mother’s tender caress and 
my father’s smile of benevolent pleasure while regarding me are my 
first recollections. i was their plaything and their idol, and something 
better—their child, the innocent and helpless creature bestow on 
them by heaven, whom to bring up to good, and whose future lot it was in 
their hand to direct to happiness or misery, according as they fulfill 
their duty towards me. with this deep consciousness of what they owe 
towards the being to which they had given life, add to the active spirit 
of tender that animate both, it may be imagine that while during 
every hour of my infant life i received a lesson of patience, of charity, 
and of self-control, i was so guide by a silken cord that all seem but 
one train of enjoyment to me. 

for a long time i was their only care. my mother had much desire to have a 
daughter, but i continue their single offspring. when i was about five 
years old, while making an excursion beyond the frontier of italy, they 
pass a week on the shore of the lake of como. their benevolent 
disposition often made them enter the cottage of the poor. this, to my 
mother, was more than a duty; it was a necessity, a 
passion—remembering what she had suffered, and how she had been 
relieved—for her to act in her turn the guardian angel to the 
afflicted. during one of their walk a poor cot in the fold of a vale 
attract their notice as being singularly disconsolate, while the number 
of half-clothed children gather about it spoke of penury in its worst 
shape. one day, when my father had gone by himself to milan, my mother, 
accompany by me, visit this abode. she found a peasant and his wife, 
hard working, bent down by care and labour, distribute a scanty meal to 
five hungry babes. among these there was one which attract my mother far 
above all the rest. she appear of a different stock. the four other were 
dark-eyed, hardy little vagrants; this child was thin and very fair. her 
hair was the brightest living gold, and despite the poverty of her 
clothing, seem to set a crown of distinction on her head. her brow was 
clear and ample, her blue eyes cloudless, and her lip and the moulding of 
her face so expressive of sensibility and sweet that none could behold 
her without look on her as of a distinct species, a being heaven-sent, 
and bearing a celestial stamp in all her features. 

the peasant woman, perceive that my mother fixed eyes of wonder and 
admiration on this lovely girl, eager communicate her history. she was 
not her child, but the daughter of a milanese nobleman. her mother was a 
germany and had die on give her birth. the infant had been placed with 
these good people to nurse: they were better off then. they had not been 
long married, and their eldest child was but just born. the father of their 
charge was one of those italy nurse in the memory of the antique glory 
of italy—one among the _schiavi ognor frementi,_ who exert 
himself to obtain the liberty of his country. he became the victim of its 
weakness. whether he had die or still linger in the dungeon of austria 
was not known. his property was confiscated; his child became an orphan and 
a beggar. she continue with her foster parent and bloom in their rude 
abode, fair than a garden rose among dark-leaved brambles. 

when my father return from milan, he found play with me in the hall of 
our villa a child fair than picture cherub—a creature who seem 
to shed radiance from her looks and whose form and motions were lighter 
than the chamois of the hills. the apparition was soon explained. with his 
permission my mother prevail on her rustic guardian to yield their 
charge to her. they were fond of the sweet orphan. her presence had seem 
a blessing to them, but it would be unfair to her to keep her in poverty 
and want when providence afford her such powerful protection. they 
consult their village priest, and the result was that elizabeth lavenza 
became the inmate of my parents’ house—my more than 
sister—the beautiful and adore companion of all my occupation and 
my pleasures. 

everyone love elizabeth. the passionate and almost reverential 
attachment with which all regard her became, while i share it, my 
pride and my delight. on the evening previous to her being brought to 
my home, my mother had said playfully, “i have a pretty present for my 
victor—tomorrow he shall have it.” and when, on the morrow, she 
present elizabeth to me as her promise gift, i, with childish 
seriousness, interpret her words literally and look upon elizabeth 
as mine—mine to protect, love, and cherish. all praises bestow on 
her i received as made to a possession of my own. we call each other 
familiarly by the name of cousin. no word, no expression could body 
forth the kind of relation in which she stood to me—my more than 
sister, since till death she was to be mine only. 





chapter 2 

we were brought up together; there was not quite a year difference in 
our ages. i need not say that we were stranger to any species of 
disunion or dispute. harmony was the soul of our companionship, and 
the diversity and contrast that subsist in our character drew us 
near together. elizabeth was of a calm and more concentrated 
disposition; but, with all my ardour, i was capable of a more intense 
application and was more deep smitten with the thirst for knowledge. 
she busy herself with following the aerial creation of the poets; 
and in the majestic and wondrous scene which surround our switzerland 
home —the sublime shape of the mountains, the change of the seasons, 
tempest and calm, the silence of winter, and the life and turbulence of 
our alpine summers—she found ample scope for admiration and delight. 
while my companion contemplate with a serious and satisfy spirit the 
magnificent appearances of things, i delight in investigate their 
causes. the world was to me a secret which i desire to divine. 
curiosity, earnest research to learn the hidden law of nature, 
glad akin to rapture, as they were unfold to me, are among the 
earliest sensation i can remember. 

on the birth of a second son, my junior by seven years, my parent gave 
up entire their wandering life and fixed themselves in their native 
country. we possessed a house in geneva, and a _campagne_ on belrive, 
the eastern shore of the lake, at the distance of rather more than a 
league from the city. we reside principally in the latter, and the 
lives of my parent were pass in considerable seclusion. it was my 
temper to avoid a crowd and to attach myself fervent to a few. i was 
indifferent, therefore, to my school-fellows in general; but i united 
myself in the bonds of the closest friendship to one among them. henry 
clerval was the son of a merchant of geneva. he was a boy of singular 
talent and fancy. he love enterprise, hardship, and even danger for 
its own sake. he was deep read in books of chivalry and romance. he 
compose heroic song and began to write many a tale of enchantment and 
knightly adventure. he try to make us act play and to enter into 
masquerades, in which the character were drawn from the hero of 
roncesvalles, of the round table of king arthur, and the chivalrous 
train who shed their blood to redeem the holy sepulchre from the hand 
of the infidels. 

no human being could have pass a happy childhood than myself. my 
parent were possessed by the very spirit of kindness and indulgence. 
we felt that they were not the tyrant to rule our lot according to 
their caprice, but the agent and creator of all the many delight 
which we enjoyed. when i mingle with other family i distinct 
discern how peculiarly fortunate my lot was, and gratitude assist 
the development of filial love. 

my temper was sometimes violent, and my passion vehement; but by some 
law in my temperature they were turn not towards childish pursuit 
but to an eager desire to learn, and not to learn all things 
indiscriminately. i confess that neither the structure of languages, 
nor the code of governments, nor the politics of various states 
possessed attraction for me. it was the secret of heaven and earth 
that i desire to learn; and whether it was the outward substance of 
things or the inner spirit of nature and the mysterious soul of man 
that occupy me, still my inquiry were direct to the metaphysical, 
or in its highest sense, the physical secret of the world. 

meanwhile clerval occupy himself, so to speak, with the moral 
relations of things. the busy stage of life, the virtue of heroes, 
and the action of men were his theme; and his hope and his dream was 
to become one among those whose name are record in story as the 
gallant and adventurous benefactor of our species. the saintly soul 
of elizabeth shone like a shrine-dedicated lamp in our peaceful home. 
her sympathy was ours; her smile, her soft voice, the sweet glance of 
her celestial eyes, were ever there to bless and animate us. she was 
the living spirit of love to soften and attract; i might have become 
sullen in my study, rough through the ardour of my nature, but that 
she was there to subdue me to a semblance of her own gentleness. and 
clerval—could aught ill entrench on the noble spirit of clerval? yet 
he might not have been so perfectly humane, so thoughtful in his 
generosity, so full of kindness and tender amidst his passion for 
adventurous exploit, had she not unfold to him the real lovely of 
benefice and made the do good the end and aim of his soar 
ambition. 

i feel exquisite pleasure in dwelling on the recollection of childhood, 
before misfortune had taint my mind and change its bright vision of 
extensive usefulness into gloomy and narrow reflection upon self. besides, 
in drawing the picture of my early days, i also record those event which 
led, by insensible steps, to my after tale of misery, for when i would 
account to myself for the birth of that passion which afterwards rule my 
destiny i find it arise, like a mountain river, from ignoble and almost 
forgotten sources; but, swelling as it proceeded, it became the torrent 
which, in its course, has swept away all my hope and joys. 

natural philosophy is the genius that has regulate my fate; i desire, 
therefore, in this narration, to state those fact which led to my 
predilection for that science. when i was thirteen years of age we all went 
on a party of pleasure to the baths near thonon; the inclement of the 
weather oblige us to remain a day confine to the inn. in this house i 
chance to find a volume of the works of corneliu agrippa. i open it 
with apathy; the theory which he attempt to demonstrate and the wonderful 
fact which he relate soon change this feeling into enthusiasm. a new 
light seem to dawn upon my mind, and bound with joy, i communicate my 
discovery to my father. my father look careless at the title page of my 
book and said, “ah! corneliu agrippa! my dear victor, do not waste 
your time upon this; it is sad trash.” 

if, instead of this remark, my father had taken the pains to explain to me 
that the principles of agrippa had been entire exploded and that a modern 
system of science had been introduce which possessed much greater powers 
than the ancient, because the powers of the latter were chimerical, while 
those of the former were real and practical, under such circumstances i 
should certainly have thrown agrippa aside and have contented my 
imagination, warm as it was, by return with greater ardour to my 
former studies. it is even possible that the train of my idea would never 
have received the fatal impulse that led to my ruin. but the cursory glance 
my father had taken of my volume by no means assured me that he was 
acquaint with its contents, and i continue to read with the greatest 
avidity. 

when i return home my first care was to procure the whole works of this 
author, and afterwards of paracelsu and albertu magnus. i read and 
studied the wild fancies of these writer with delight; they appear to me 
treasure known to few besides myself. i have describe myself as always 
have been imbue with a fervent longing to penetrate the secret of 
nature. in spite of the intense labour and wonderful discovery of modern 
philosophers, i always came from my study discontented and unsatisfied. 
sir isaac newton is said to have avowed that he felt like a child pick 
up shell beside the great and unexplored ocean of truth. those of his 
successor in each branch of natural philosophy with whom i was acquaint 
appear even to my boy’s apprehension as tyro engaged in the same 
pursuit. 

the untaught peasant beheld the elements around him and was acquaint 
with their practical uses. the most learned philosopher knew little 
more. he had partially unveil the face of nature, but her immortal 
lineament were still a wonder and a mystery. he might dissect, 
anatomise, and give names; but, not to speak of a final cause, cause 
in their secondary and tertiary grade were utterly unknown to him. i 
had gaze upon the fortification and impediment that seem to keep 
human being from enter the citadel of nature, and rash and 
ignorant i had repined. 

but here were books, and here were men who had penetrate deep and knew 
more. i took their word for all that they averred, and i became their 
disciple. it may appear strange that such should arise in the eighteenth 
century; but while i follow the routine of education in the school of 
geneva, i was, to a great degree, self-taught with regard to my favourite 
studies. my father was not scientific, and i was left to struggle with a 
child’s blindness, add to a student’s thirst for knowledge. 
under the guidance of my new preceptor i enter with the greatest 
diligence into the search of the philosopher’s stone and the elixir 
of life; but the latter soon obtain my undivided attention. wealth was an 
inferior object, but what glory would attend the discovery if i could 
banish disease from the human frame and render man invulnerable to any but 
a violent death! 

nor were these my only visions. the raise of ghost or devil was a 
promise liberally accord by my favourite authors, the fulfilment of which 
i most eager sought; and if my incantation were always unsuccessful, i 
attribute the failure rather to my own inexperience and mistake than to a 
want of skill or fidelity in my instructors. and thus for a time i was 
occupy by exploded systems, mingling, like an unadept, a thousand 
contradictory theory and flounder desperate in a very slough of 
multifarious knowledge, guide by an ardent imagination and childish 
reasoning, till an accident again change the current of my ideas. 

when i was about fifteen years old we had retired to our house near 
belrive, when we witness a most violent and terrible thunderstorm. it 
advanced from behind the mountain of jura, and the thunder burst at once 
with frightful loud from various quarters of the heavens. i remained, 
while the storm lasted, watch its progress with curiosity and delight. 
as i stood at the door, on a sudden i beheld a stream of fire issue from an 
old and beautiful oak which stood about twenty yard from our house; and so 
soon as the dazzle light vanished, the oak had disappeared, and nothing 
remain but a blasted stump. when we visit it the next morning, we found 
the tree shatter in a singular manner. it was not splinter by the 
shock, but entire reduce to thin ribbon of wood. i never beheld 
anything so utterly destroyed. 

before this i was not unacquainted with the more obvious law of 
electricity. on this occasion a man of great research in natural 
philosophy was with us, and excited by this catastrophe, he enter on 
the explanation of a theory which he had form on the subject of 
electricity and galvanism, which was at once new and astonish to me. 
all that he said threw greatly into the shade corneliu agrippa, 
albertu magnus, and paracelsus, the lords of my imagination; but by 
some fatality the overthrow of these men disinclined me to pursue my 
accustomed studies. it seem to me as if nothing would or could ever 
be known. all that had so long engaged my attention sudden grew 
despicable. by one of those caprice of the mind which we are perhaps 
most subject to in early youth, i at once gave up my former 
occupations, set down natural history and all its progeny as a deform 
and abortive creation, and entertain the greatest disdain for a 
would-be science which could never even step within the threshold of 
real knowledge. in this mood of mind i betook myself to the 
mathematics and the branch of study appertain to that science as 
being built upon secure foundations, and so worthy of my consideration. 

thus strange are our soul constructed, and by such slight ligament 
are we bound to prosperity or ruin. when i look back, it seem to me 
as if this almost miraculous change of inclination and will was the 
immediate suggestion of the guardian angel of my life—the last effort 
made by the spirit of preservation to avert the storm that was even 
then hanging in the stars and ready to envelop me. her victory was 
announce by an unusual tranquill and glad of soul which 
follow the relinquish of my ancient and latterly torment 
studies. it was thus that i was to be taught to associate evil with 
their prosecution, happiness with their disregard. 

it was a strong effort of the spirit of good, but it was ineffectual. 
destiny was too potent, and her immutable law had decree my utter and 
terrible destruction. 





chapter 3 

when i had attain the age of seventeen my parent resolve that i 
should become a student at the university of ingolstadt. i had 
hitherto attend the school of geneva, but my father thought it 
necessary for the completion of my education that i should be made 
acquaint with other customs than those of my native country. my 
departure was therefore fixed at an early date, but before the day 
resolve upon could arrive, the first misfortune of my life 
occurred—an omen, as it were, of my future misery. 

elizabeth had caught the scarlet fever; her illness was severe, and she was 
in the greatest danger. during her illness many argument had been urge to 
persuade my mother to refrain from attend upon her. she had at first 
yield to our entreaties, but when she heard that the life of her 
favourite was menaced, she could no long control her anxiety. she 
attend her sickbed; her watchful attention triumph over the malignity 
of the distemper—elizabeth was saved, but the consequence of this 
imprudence were fatal to her preserver. on the third day my mother 
sickened; her fever was accompany by the most alarm symptoms, and the 
looks of her medical attendant prognosticate the worst event. on her 
deathbed the fortitude and benignity of this best of women did not desert 
her. she join the hand of elizabeth and myself. “my 
children,” she said, “my firmest hope of future happiness were 
placed on the prospect of your union. this expectation will now be the 
consolation of your father. elizabeth, my love, you must supply my place to 
my younger children. alas! i regret that i am taken from you; and, happy 
and beloved as i have been, is it not hard to quit you all? but these are 
not thought befitting me; i will endeavour to resign myself cheerful to 
death and will indulge a hope of meeting you in another world.” 

she die calmly, and her countenance express affection even in death. 
i need not describe the feelings of those whose dearest tie are rent 
by that most irreparable evil, the void that presents itself to the 
soul, and the despair that is exhibit on the countenance. it is so 
long before the mind can persuade itself that she whom we saw every day 
and whose very existence appear a part of our own can have departed 
for ever—that the bright of a beloved eye can have been 
extinguish and the sound of a voice so familiar and dear to the ear 
can be hushed, never more to be heard. these are the reflection of 
the first days; but when the lapse of time prove the reality of the 
evil, then the actual bitter of grief commences. yet from whom has 
not that rude hand rent away some dear connection? and why should i 
describe a sorrow which all have felt, and must feel? the time at 
length arrive when grief is rather an indulgence than a necessity; and 
the smile that play upon the lips, although it may be deem a 
sacrilege, is not banished. my mother was dead, but we had still 
duty which we ought to perform; we must continue our course with the 
rest and learn to think ourselves fortunate whilst one remains whom the 
spoil has not seized. 

my departure for ingolstadt, which had been defer by these events, 
was now again determined upon. i obtain from my father a respite of 
some weeks. it appear to me sacrilege so soon to leave the repose, 
akin to death, of the house of mourning and to rush into the thick of 
life. i was new to sorrow, but it did not the less alarm me. i was 
unwill to quit the sight of those that remain to me, and above 
all, i desire to see my sweet elizabeth in some degree consoled. 

she indeed veiled her grief and strove to act the comforter to us all. 
she look steady on life and assume its duty with courage and 
zeal. she devoted herself to those whom she had been taught to call 
her uncle and cousins. never was she so enchanting as at this time, 
when she recall the sunshine of her smile and spent them upon us. 
she forgot even her own regret in her endeavour to make us forget. 

the day of my departure at length arrived. clerval spent the last 
evening with us. he had endeavour to persuade his father to permit 
him to accompany me and to become my fellow student, but in vain. his 
father was a narrow-minded trader and saw idle and ruin in the 
aspiration and ambition of his son. henry deep felt the misfortune 
of being debar from a liberal education. he said little, but when 
he spoke i read in his kindling eye and in his animate glance a 
restrained but firm resolve not to be chain to the miserable detail 
of commerce. 

we sat late. we could not tear ourselves away from each other nor 
persuade ourselves to say the word “farewell!” it was said, and we 
retired under the pretence of seek repose, each fancy that the 
other was deceived; but when at morning’s dawn i descended to the 
carriage which was to convey me away, they were all there—my father 
again to bless me, clerval to press my hand once more, my elizabeth to 
renew her entreaty that i would write often and to bestow the last 
feminine attention on her playmate and friend. 

i threw myself into the chaise that was to convey me away and indulge in 
the most melancholy reflections. i, who had ever been surround by 
amiable companions, continual engaged in endeavour to bestow mutual 
pleasure—i was now alone. in the university whither i was go i 
must form my own friends and be my own protector. my life had hitherto 
been remarkably secluded and domestic, and this had given me invincible 
repugnance to new countenances. i love my brothers, elizabeth, and 
clerval; these were “old familiar faces,” but i believe myself 
total unfit for the company of strangers. such were my reflection as 
i commence my journey; but as i proceeded, my spirits and hope rose. i 
ardent desire the acquisition of knowledge. i had often, when at home, 
thought it hard to remain during my youth coop up in one place and had 
long to enter the world and take my station among other human beings. 
now my desire were comply with, and it would, indeed, have been folly to 
repent. 

i had sufficient leisure for these and many other reflection during my 
journey to ingolstadt, which was long and fatiguing. at length the 
high white steeple of the town met my eyes. i alight and was 
conduct to my solitary apartment to spend the evening as i pleased. 

the next morning i deliver my letters of introduction and paid a visit to 
some of the principal professors. chance—or rather the evil 
influence, the angel of destruction, which assert omnipotent sway over me 
from the moment i turn my reluctant steps from my father’s 
door—led me first to m. krempe, professor of natural philosophy. he 
was an uncouth man, but deep imbue in the secret of his science. he 
ask me several question concerning my progress in the different branch 
of science appertain to natural philosophy. i reply carelessly, and 
partly in contempt, mention the name of my alchemist as the principal 
author i had studied. the professor stared. “have you,” he 
said, “really spent your time in study such nonsense?” 

i reply in the affirmative. “every minute,” continue m. krempe with 
warmth, “every instant that you have waste on those books is utterly 
and entire lost. you have burden your memory with exploded system 
and useless names. good god! in what desert land have you lived, 
where no one was kind enough to inform you that these fancies which you 
have so greedy imbibe are a thousand years old and as musty as they 
are ancient? i little expected, in this enlightened and scientific 
age, to find a disciple of albertu magnu and paracelsus. my dear 
sir, you must begin your study entire anew.” 

so saying, he step aside and wrote down a list of several books 
treat of natural philosophy which he desire me to procure, and 
dismiss me after mention that in the beginning of the following 
week he intended to commence a course of lecture upon natural 
philosophy in its general relations, and that m. waldman, a fellow 
professor, would lecture upon chemistry the alternate days that he 
omitted. 

i return home not disappointed, for i have said that i had long 
considered those author useless whom the professor reprobated; but i 
return not at all the more inclined to recur to these study in any 
shape. m. krempe was a little squat man with a gruff voice and a 
repulsive countenance; the teacher, therefore, did not prepossess me in 
favour of his pursuits. in rather a too philosophical and connected a 
strain, perhaps, i have given an account of the conclusion i had come 
to concerning them in my early years. as a child i had not been 
content with the result promise by the modern professor of natural 
science. with a confusion of idea only to be account for by my 
extreme youth and my want of a guide on such matters, i had retrod the 
steps of knowledge along the path of time and exchange the 
discovery of recent inquire for the dream of forgotten alchemists. 
besides, i had a contempt for the use of modern natural philosophy. 
it was very different when the master of the science sought 
immortality and power; such views, although futile, were grand; but now 
the scene was changed. the ambition of the inquire seem to limit 
itself to the annihilate of those vision on which my interest in 
science was chiefly founded. i was require to exchange chimera of 
boundless grandeur for reality of little worth. 

such were my reflection during the first two or three days of my 
residence at ingolstadt, which were chiefly spent in becoming 
acquaint with the locality and the principal resident in my new 
abode. but as the ensue week commenced, i thought of the information 
which m. krempe had given me concerning the lectures. and although i 
could not consent to go and hear that little conceited fellow deliver 
sentence out of a pulpit, i recollect what he had said of m. 
waldman, whom i had never seen, as he had hitherto been out of town. 

partly from curiosity and partly from idleness, i went into the lecture 
room, which m. waldman enter shortly after. this professor was very 
unlike his colleague. he appear about fifty years of age, but with an 
aspect expressive of the greatest benevolence; a few grey hair cover his 
temples, but those at the back of his head were nearly black. his person 
was short but remarkably erect and his voice the sweetest i had ever heard. 
he began his lecture by a recapitulation of the history of chemistry and 
the various improvement made by different men of learning, pronounce 
with fervour the name of the most distinguished discoverers. he then took 
a cursory view of the present state of the science and explain many of 
its elementary terms. after have made a few preparatory experiments, he 
conclude with a panegyric upon modern chemistry, the terms of which i 
shall never forget: 

“the ancient teacher of this science,” said he, 
“promised impossible and perform nothing. the modern master 
promise very little; they know that metal cannot be transmute and that 
the elixir of life is a chimera but these philosophers, whose hand seem 
only made to dabble in dirt, and their eyes to pore over the microscope or 
crucible, have indeed perform miracles. they penetrate into the recess 
of nature and show how she works in her hiding-places. they ascend into the 
heavens; they have discover how the blood circulates, and the nature of 
the air we breathe. they have acquire new and almost unlimited powers; 
they can command the thunder of heaven, mimic the earthquake, and even 
mock the invisible world with its own shadows.” 

such were the professor’s words—rather let me say such the words of 
the fate—enounced to destroy me. as he went on i felt as if my soul 
were grapple with a palpable enemy; one by one the various key were 
touched which form the mechanism of my being; chord after chord was 
sounded, and soon my mind was fill with one thought, one conception, 
one purpose. so much has been done, exclaim the soul of 
frankenstein—more, far more, will i achieve; tread in the steps 
already marked, i will pioneer a new way, explore unknown powers, and 
unfold to the world the deepest mystery of creation. 

i closed not my eyes that night. my internal being was in a state of 
insurrection and turmoil; i felt that order would thence arise, but i 
had no power to produce it. by degrees, after the morning’s dawn, 
sleep came. i awoke, and my yesternight’s thought were as a dream. 
there only remain a resolution to return to my ancient study and to 
devote myself to a science for which i believe myself to possess a 
natural talent. on the same day i paid m. waldman a visit. his 
manners in private were even more mild and attractive than in public, 
for there was a certain dignity in his mien during his lecture which in 
his own house was replace by the greatest affable and kindness. i 
gave him pretty nearly the same account of my former pursuit as i had 
given to his fellow professor. he heard with attention the little 
narration concerning my study and smile at the name of corneliu 
agrippa and paracelsus, but without the contempt that m. krempe had 
exhibited. he said that “these were men to whose indefatigable zeal 
modern philosopher were indebted for most of the foundations of their 
knowledge. they had left to us, as an easy task, to give new name 
and arrange in connected classification the fact which they in a 
great degree had been the instrument of bring to light. the 
labour of men of genius, however erroneous directed, scarcely ever 
fail in ultimately turning to the solid advantage of mankind.” i 
listen to his statement, which was deliver without any presumption 
or affectation, and then add that his lecture had remove my 
prejudice against modern chemists; i express myself in measured 
terms, with the modesty and deference due from a youth to his 
instructor, without letting escape (inexperience in life would have 
made me ashamed) any of the enthusiasm which stimulate my intended 
labours. i request his advice concerning the books i ought to 
procure. 

“i am happy,” said m. waldman, “to have gain a 
disciple; and if your application equal your ability, i have no doubt of 
your success. chemistry is that branch of natural philosophy in which the 
greatest improvement have been and may be made; it is on that account that 
i have made it my peculiar study; but at the same time, i have not 
neglect the other branch of science. a man would make but a very sorry 
chemist if he attend to that department of human knowledge alone. if your 
wish is to become really a man of science and not merely a petty 
experimentalist, i should advise you to apply to every branch of natural 
philosophy, including mathematics.” 

he then took me into his laboratory and explain to me the use of his 
various machines, instruct me as to what i ought to procure and 
promising me the use of his own when i should have advanced far enough in 
the science not to derange their mechanism. he also gave me the list of 
books which i had requested, and i took my leave. 

thus end a day memorable to me; it decided my future destiny. 





chapter 4 

from this day natural philosophy, and particularly chemistry, in the 
most comprehensive sense of the term, became nearly my sole occupation. 
i read with ardour those works, so full of genius and discrimination, 
which modern inquire have written on these subjects. i attend the 
lecture and cultivated the acquaintance of the men of science of the 
university, and i found even in m. krempe a great deal of sound sense 
and real information, combined, it is true, with a repulsive 
physiognomy and manners, but not on that account the less valuable. in 
m. waldman i found a true friend. his gentle was never tinge by 
dogmatism, and his instruction were given with an air of frank and 
good nature that banish every idea of pedantry. in a thousand ways 
he smooth for me the path of knowledge and made the most abstruse 
inquiry clear and facile to my apprehension. my application was at 
first fluctuate and uncertain; it gain strength as i proceed and 
soon became so ardent and eager that the stars often disappear in the 
light of morning whilst i was yet engaged in my laboratory. 

as i applied so closely, it may be easily conceive that my progress 
was rapid. my ardour was indeed the astonishment of the students, and 
my proficient that of the masters. professor krempe often ask me, 
with a sly smile, how corneliu agrippa went on, whilst m. waldman 
express the most heartfelt exultation in my progress. two years 
pass in this manner, during which i paid no visit to geneva, but was 
engaged, heart and soul, in the pursuit of some discovery which i 
hope to make. none but those who have experienced them can conceive 
of the enticement of science. in other study you go as far as 
other have gone before you, and there is nothing more to know; but in 
a scientific pursuit there is continual food for discovery and wonder. 
a mind of moderate capacity which close pursue one study must 
infallibly arrive at great proficient in that study; and i, who 
continual sought the attainment of one object of pursuit and was 
solely wrap up in this, improve so rapid that at the end of two 
years i made some discovery in the improvement of some chemical 
instruments, which procure me great esteem and admiration at the 
university. when i had arrive at this point and had become as well 
acquaint with the theory and practice of natural philosophy as 
depend on the lesson of any of the professor at ingolstadt, my 
residence there being no long conducive to my improvements, i thought 
of return to my friends and my native town, when an incident 
happen that protract my stay. 

one of the phenomena which had peculiarly attract my attention was 
the structure of the human frame, and, indeed, any animal endue with 
life. whence, i often ask myself, did the principle of life proceed? 
it was a bold question, and one which has ever been considered as a 
mystery; yet with how many things are we upon the brink of becoming 
acquainted, if cowardice or careless did not restrain our 
inquiries. i revolve these circumstances in my mind and determined 
thenceforth to apply myself more particularly to those branch of 
natural philosophy which relate to physiology. unless i had been 
animate by an almost supernatural enthusiasm, my application to this 
study would have been irksome and almost intolerable. to examine the 
cause of life, we must first have recourse to death. i became 
acquaint with the science of anatomy, but this was not sufficient; i 
must also observe the natural decay and corruption of the human body. 
in my education my father had taken the greatest precaution that my 
mind should be impress with no supernatural horrors. i do not ever 
remember to have tremble at a tale of superstition or to have fear 
the apparition of a spirit. dark had no effect upon my fancy, and 
a churchyard was to me merely the receptacle of body deprived of 
life, which, from being the seat of beauty and strength, had become 
food for the worm. now i was led to examine the cause and progress of 
this decay and forced to spend days and nights in vault and 
charnel-houses. my attention was fixed upon every object the most 
insupportable to the delicacy of the human feelings. i saw how the 
fine form of man was degrade and wasted; i beheld the corruption of 
death succeed to the blooming cheek of life; i saw how the worm 
inherit the wonders of the eye and brain. i paused, examine and 
analyse all the minutiae of causation, as exemplify in the change 
from life to death, and death to life, until from the midst of this 
dark a sudden light broke in upon me—a light so brilliant and 
wondrous, yet so simple, that while i became dizzy with the immensity 
of the prospect which it illustrated, i was surprise that among so 
many men of genius who had direct their inquiry towards the same 
science, that i alone should be reserved to discover so astonish a 
secret. 

remember, i am not recording the vision of a madman. the sun do not 
more certainly shine in the heaven than that which i now affirm is 
true. some miracle might have produce it, yet the stage of the 
discovery were distinct and probable. after days and nights of 
incredible labour and fatigue, i succeed in discover the cause of 
generation and life; nay, more, i became myself capable of bestow 
animation upon lifeless matter. 

the astonishment which i had at first experienced on this discovery 
soon gave place to delight and rapture. after so much time spent in 
painful labour, to arrive at once at the summit of my desire was the 
most gratifying consummation of my toils. but this discovery was so 
great and overwhelming that all the steps by which i had been 
progressive led to it were obliterated, and i beheld only the result. 
what had been the study and desire of the wisest men since the creation 
of the world was now within my grasp. not that, like a magic scene, it 
all open upon me at once: the information i had obtain was of a 
nature rather to direct my endeavour so soon as i should point them 
towards the object of my search than to exhibit that object already 
accomplished. i was like the arabia who had been bury with the dead 
and found a passage to life, aid only by one glimmer and seemingly 
ineffectual light. 

i see by your eager and the wonder and hope which your eyes 
express, my friend, that you expect to be informed of the secret with 
which i am acquainted; that cannot be; listen patient until the end 
of my story, and you will easily perceive why i am reserved upon that 
subject. i will not lead you on, unguarded and ardent as i then was, 
to your destruction and infallible misery. learn from me, if not by my 
precepts, at least by my example, how dangerous is the acquire of 
knowledge and how much happy that man is who believe his native town 
to be the world, than he who aspire to become greater than his nature 
will allow. 

when i found so astonish a power placed within my hands, i hesitate 
a long time concerning the manner in which i should employ it. 
although i possessed the capacity of bestow animation, yet to 
prepare a frame for the reception of it, with all its intricacy of 
fibres, muscles, and veins, still remain a work of inconceivable 
difficulty and labour. i doubt at first whether i should attempt the 
creation of a being like myself, or one of simple organization; but my 
imagination was too much exalted by my first success to permit me to 
doubt of my ability to give life to an animal as complex and wonderful 
as man. the material at present within my command hardly appear 
adequate to so arduous an undertaking, but i doubt not that i should 
ultimately succeed. i prepared myself for a multitude of reverses; my 
operation might be incessant baffled, and at last my work be 
imperfect, yet when i considered the improvement which every day take 
place in science and mechanics, i was encourage to hope my present 
attempt would at least lay the foundations of future success. nor 
could i consider the magnitude and complexity of my plan as any 
argument of its impracticability. it was with these feelings that i 
began the creation of a human being. as the minute of the parts 
form a great hindrance to my speed, i resolved, contrary to my first 
intention, to make the being of a gigantic stature, that is to say, 
about eight feet in height, and proportion large. after have 
form this determination and have spent some month in successful 
collect and arrange my materials, i began. 

no one can conceive the variety of feelings which bore me onwards, like 
a hurricane, in the first enthusiasm of success. life and death 
appear to me ideal bounds, which i should first break through, and 
pour a torrent of light into our dark world. a new species would bless 
me as its creator and source; many happy and excellent nature would 
owe their being to me. no father could claim the gratitude of his 
child so completely as i should deserve theirs. pursue these 
reflections, i thought that if i could bestow animation upon lifeless 
matter, i might in process of time (although i now found it impossible) 
renew life where death had apparently devoted the body to corruption. 

these thought support my spirits, while i pursue my undertaking 
with unremitting ardour. my cheek had grown pale with study, and my 
person had become emaciate with confinement. sometimes, on the very 
brink of certainty, i failed; yet still i clung to the hope which the 
next day or the next hour might realise. one secret which i alone 
possessed was the hope to which i had dedicated myself; and the moon 
gaze on my midnight labours, while, with unrelaxed and breathless 
eagerness, i pursue nature to her hiding-places. who shall conceive 
the horrors of my secret toil as i dabble among the unhallowed damp 
of the grave or torture the living animal to animate the lifeless 
clay? my limb now tremble, and my eyes swim with the remembrance; but 
then a resistless and almost frantic impulse urge me forward; i seem 
to have lost all soul or sensation but for this one pursuit. it was 
indeed but a passing trance, that only made me feel with renew 
acute so soon as, the unnatural stimulus cease to operate, i had 
return to my old habits. i collected bone from charnel-houses and 
disturbed, with profane fingers, the tremendous secret of the human 
frame. in a solitary chamber, or rather cell, at the top of the house, 
and separate from all the other apartments by a gallery and staircase, 
i kept my workshop of filthy creation; my eyeball were start from 
their socket in attend to the detail of my employment. the 
dissect room and the slaughter-house furnish many of my materials; 
and often did my human nature turn with loathing from my occupation, 
whilst, still urge on by an eager which perpetual increased, i 
brought my work near to a conclusion. 

the summer month pass while i was thus engaged, heart and soul, in 
one pursuit. it was a most beautiful season; never did the field 
bestow a more plentiful harvest or the vine yield a more luxuriant 
vintage, but my eyes were insensible to the charm of nature. and the 
same feelings which made me neglect the scene around me cause me also
