to forget those friends who were so many mile absent, and whom i had 
not seen for so long a time. i knew my silence disquiet them, and i 
well remember the words of my father: “i know that while you are 
pleased with yourself you will think of us with affection, and we shall 
hear regularly from you. you must pardon me if i regard any 
interrupt in your correspondence as a proof that your other duty 
are equally neglected.” 

i knew well therefore what would be my father’s feelings, but i could 
not tear my thought from my employment, loathsome in itself, but which 
had taken an irresistible hold of my imagination. i wished, as it 
were, to procrastinate all that related to my feelings of affection 
until the great object, which swallow up every habit of my nature, 
should be completed. 

i then thought that my father would be unjust if he ascribe my neglect 
to vice or faulty on my part, but i am now convinced that he was 
justified in conceive that i should not be altogether free from 
blame. a human being in perfection ought always to preserve a calm and 
peaceful mind and never to allow passion or a transitory desire to 
disturb his tranquillity. i do not think that the pursuit of knowledge 
is an exception to this rule. if the study to which you apply yourself 
has a tendency to weaken your affection and to destroy your taste for 
those simple pleasure in which no alloy can possibly mix, then that 
study is certainly unlawful, that is to say, not befitting the human 
mind. if this rule were always observed; if no man allow any pursuit 
whatsoever to interfere with the tranquill of his domestic 
affections, greece had not been enslaved, cæsar would have spare his 
country, america would have been discover more gradually, and the 
empire of mexico and peru had not been destroyed. 

but i forget that i am moralize in the most interesting part of my 
tale, and your looks remind me to proceed. 

my father made no reproach in his letters and only took notice of my 
silence by inquiring into my occupation more particularly than before. 
winter, spring, and summer pass away during my labours; but i did not 
watch the blossom or the expand leaves—sights which before always 
yield me supreme delight—so deep was i engross in my 
occupation. the leaves of that year had wither before my work drew near 
to a close, and now every day show me more plainly how well i had 
succeeded. but my enthusiasm was checked by my anxiety, and i appear 
rather like one doom by slavery to toil in the mines, or any other 
unwholesome trade than an artist occupy by his favourite employment. 
every night i was oppress by a slow fever, and i became nervous to a most 
painful degree; the fall of a leaf startle me, and i shun my fellow 
creature as if i had been guilty of a crime. sometimes i grew alarm at 
the wreck i perceive that i had become; the energy of my purpose alone 
sustain me: my labour would soon end, and i believe that exercise and 
amusement would then drive away incipient disease; and i promise myself 
both of these when my creation should be complete. 





chapter 5 

it was on a dreary night of november that i beheld the accomplishment 
of my toils. with an anxiety that almost amount to agony, i 
collected the instrument of life around me, that i might infuse a 
spark of being into the lifeless thing that lay at my feet. it was 
already one in the morning; the rain patter dismal against the 
panes, and my candle was nearly burnt out, when, by the glimmer of the 
half-extinguished light, i saw the dull yellow eye of the creature 
open; it breathe hard, and a convulsive motion agitate its limbs. 

how can i describe my emotion at this catastrophe, or how delineate 
the wretch whom with such infinite pains and care i had endeavour to 
form? his limb were in proportion, and i had select his features as 
beautiful. beautiful! great god! his yellow skin scarcely cover 
the work of muscle and artery beneath; his hair was of a lustrous 
black, and flowing; his teeth of a pearly whiteness; but these 
luxuriance only form a more horrid contrast with his watery eyes, 
that seem almost of the same colour as the dun-white socket in which 
they were set, his shrivel complexion and straight black lips. 

the different accident of life are not so changeable as the feelings 
of human nature. i had work hard for nearly two years, for the sole 
purpose of infuse life into an inanimate body. for this i had 
deprived myself of rest and health. i had desire it with an ardour 
that far exceed moderation; but now that i had finished, the beauty 
of the dream vanished, and breathless horror and disgust fill my 
heart. unable to endure the aspect of the being i had created, i 
rush out of the room and continue a long time traverse my 
bed-chamber, unable to compose my mind to sleep. at length lassitude 
succeed to the tumult i had before endured, and i threw myself on the 
bed in my clothes, endeavour to seek a few moments of forgetfulness. 
but it was in vain; i slept, indeed, but i was disturbed by the wildest 
dreams. i thought i saw elizabeth, in the bloom of health, walking in 
the street of ingolstadt. delight and surprised, i embrace her, 
but as i imprint the first kiss on her lips, they became livid with 
the hue of death; her features appear to change, and i thought that i 
held the corpse of my dead mother in my arms; a shroud envelop her 
form, and i saw the grave-worms crawl in the fold of the flannel. 
i start from my sleep with horror; a cold dew cover my forehead, my 
teeth chattered, and every limb became convulsed; when, by the dim and 
yellow light of the moon, as it forced its way through the window 
shutters, i beheld the wretch—the miserable monster whom i had 
created. he held up the curtain of the bed; and his eyes, if eyes they 
may be called, were fixed on me. his jaws opened, and he mutter some 
inarticulate sounds, while a grin wrinkle his cheeks. he might have 
spoken, but i did not hear; one hand was stretch out, seemingly to 
detain me, but i escape and rush downstairs. i took refuge in the 
courtyard belong to the house which i inhabited, where i remain 
during the rest of the night, walking up and down in the greatest 
agitation, listen attentively, catching and fear each sound as if 
it were to announce the approach of the demoniacal corpse to which i 
had so miserably given life. 

oh! no mortal could support the horror of that countenance. a mummy 
again endue with animation could not be so hideous as that wretch. i 
had gaze on him while unfinished; he was ugly then, but when those 
muscle and joint were render capable of motion, it became a thing 
such as even dante could not have conceived. 

i pass the night wretchedly. sometimes my pulse beat so quick and 
hardly that i felt the palpitation of every artery; at others, i nearly 
sank to the ground through languor and extreme weakness. mingle with 
this horror, i felt the bitter of disappointment; dream that had 
been my food and pleasant rest for so long a space were now become a 
hell to me; and the change was so rapid, the overthrow so complete! 

morning, dismal and wet, at length dawn and discover to my 
sleepless and ache eyes the church of ingolstadt, its white steeple 
and clock, which indicate the sixth hour. the porter open the gate 
of the court, which had that night been my asylum, and i issue into 
the streets, pace them with quick steps, as if i sought to avoid the 
wretch whom i fear every turning of the street would present to my 
view. i did not dare return to the apartment which i inhabited, but 
felt impel to hurry on, although drench by the rain which pour 
from a black and comfortless sky. 

i continue walking in this manner for some time, endeavour by 
bodily exercise to ease the load that weigh upon my mind. i 
traverse the street without any clear conception of where i was or 
what i was doing. my heart palpitate in the sickness of fear, and i 
hurried on with irregular steps, not daring to look about me: 

like one who, on a lonely road, 
doth walk in fear and dread, 
and, have once turn round, walk on, 
and turn no more his head; 
because he know a frightful fiend 
doth close behind him tread. 

[coleridge’s “ancient mariner.”] 



continue thus, i came at length opposite to the inn at which the various 
diligence and carriage usually stopped. here i paused, i knew not why; 
but i remain some minutes with my eyes fixed on a coach that was coming 
towards me from the other end of the street. as it drew near i observe 
that it was the switzerland diligence; it stop just where i was standing, and 
on the door being opened, i perceive henry clerval, who, on seeing me, 
instantly sprung out. “my dear frankenstein,” exclaim he, 
“how glad i am to see you! how fortunate that you should be here at 
the very moment of my alighting!” 

nothing could equal my delight on seeing clerval; his presence brought back 
to my thought my father, elizabeth, and all those scene of home so dear 
to my recollection. i grasp his hand, and in a moment forgot my horror 
and misfortune; i felt suddenly, and for the first time during many months, 
calm and serene joy. i welcome my friend, therefore, in the most cordial 
manner, and we walk towards my college. clerval continue talk for 
some time about our mutual friends and his own good fortune in being 
permit to come to ingolstadt. “you may easily believe,” said 
he, “how great was the difficulty to persuade my father that all 
necessary knowledge was not comprise in the noble art of book-keeping; 
and, indeed, i believe i left him incredulous to the last, for his constant 
answer to my unweary entreaty was the same as that of the dutch 
schoolmaster in the vicar of wakefield: ‘i have ten thousand florin 
a year without greek, i eat heartily without greek.’ but his 
affection for me at length overcame his dislike of learning, and he has 
permit me to undertake a voyage of discovery to the land of 
knowledge.” 

“it give me the greatest delight to see you; but tell me how you left 
my father, brothers, and elizabeth.” 

“very well, and very happy, only a little uneasy that they hear from 
you so seldom. by the by, i mean to lecture you a little upon their 
account myself. but, my dear frankenstein,” continue he, stopping 
short and gaze full in my face, “i did not before remark how very ill 
you appear; so thin and pale; you look as if you had been watch for 
several nights.” 

“you have guess right; i have lately been so deep engaged in one 
occupation that i have not allow myself sufficient rest, as you see; 
but i hope, i sincerely hope, that all these employment are now at an 
end and that i am at length free.” 

i tremble excessively; i could not endure to think of, and far less to 
allude to, the occurrence of the preceding night. i walk with a 
quick pace, and we soon arrive at my college. i then reflected, and 
the thought made me shiver, that the creature whom i had left in my 
apartment might still be there, alive and walking about. i dread to 
behold this monster, but i fear still more that henry should see him. 
entreat him, therefore, to remain a few minutes at the bottom of the 
stairs, i dart up towards my own room. my hand was already on the 
lock of the door before i recollect myself. i then paused, and a 
cold shiver came over me. i threw the door forcibly open, as 
children are accustomed to do when they expect a spectre to stand in 
wait for them on the other side; but nothing appeared. i step 
fearful in: the apartment was empty, and my bedroom was also free 
from its hideous guest. i could hardly believe that so great a good 
fortune could have befallen me, but when i became assured that my enemy 
had indeed fled, i clap my hand for joy and ran down to clerval. 

we ascend into my room, and the servant presently brought breakfast; 
but i was unable to contain myself. it was not joy only that possessed 
me; i felt my flesh tingle with excess of sensitiveness, and my pulse 
beat rapidly. i was unable to remain for a single instant in the same 
place; i jump over the chairs, clap my hands, and laugh aloud. 
clerval at first attribute my unusual spirits to joy on his arrival, 
but when he observe me more attentively, he saw a wild in my eyes 
for which he could not account, and my loud, unrestrained, heartless 
laughter frightened and astonish him. 

“my dear victor,” cry he, “what, for god’s sake, 
is the matter? do not laugh in that manner. how ill you are! what is the 
cause of all this?” 

“do not ask me,” cry i, putt my hand before my eyes, for i 
thought i saw the dread spectre glide into the room; “_he_ can 
tell. oh, save me! save me!” i imagine that the monster seize me; 
i struggle furiously and fell down in a fit. 

poor clerval! what must have been his feelings? a meeting, which he 
anticipate with such joy, so strange turn to bitterness. but i 
was not the witness of his grief, for i was lifeless and did not 
recover my senses for a long, long time. 

this was the commencement of a nervous fever which confine me for 
several months. during all that time henry was my only nurse. i 
afterwards learned that, knowing my father’s advanced age and unfit 
for so long a journey, and how wretched my sickness would make 
elizabeth, he spare them this grief by conceal the extent of my 
disorder. he knew that i could not have a more kind and attentive 
nurse than himself; and, firm in the hope he felt of my recovery, he 
did not doubt that, instead of do harm, he perform the kindest 
action that he could towards them. 

but i was in reality very ill, and surely nothing but the unbounded and 
unremitting attention of my friend could have restore me to life. 
the form of the monster on whom i had bestow existence was for ever 
before my eyes, and i rave incessant concerning him. doubtless my 
words surprise henry; he at first believe them to be the wanderings 
of my disturbed imagination, but the pertinac with which i 
continual recur to the same subject persuade him that my disorder 
indeed owe its origin to some uncommon and terrible event. 

by very slow degrees, and with frequent relapse that alarm and 
grieve my friend, i recovered. i remember the first time i became 
capable of observing outward object with any kind of pleasure, i 
perceive that the fallen leaves had disappear and that the young 
bud were shoot forth from the tree that shade my window. it was 
a divine spring, and the season contribute greatly to my 
convalescence. i felt also sentiment of joy and affection revive in 
my bosom; my gloom disappeared, and in a short time i became as 
cheerful as before i was attack by the fatal passion. 

“dearest clerval,” exclaim i, “how kind, how very good 
you are to me. this whole winter, instead of being spent in study, as you 
promise yourself, has been consume in my sick room. how shall i ever 
repay you? i feel the greatest remorse for the disappointment of which i 
have been the occasion, but you will forgive me.” 

“you will repay me entire if you do not discompose yourself, but get 
well as fast as you can; and since you appear in such good spirits, i 
may speak to you on one subject, may i not?” 

i trembled. one subject! what could it be? could he allude to an object on 
whom i dare not even think? 

“compose yourself,” said clerval, who observe my change of 
colour, “i will not mention it if it agitate you; but your father 
and cousin would be very happy if they received a letter from you in your 
own handwriting. they hardly know how ill you have been and are uneasy at 
your long silence.” 

“is that all, my dear henry? how could you suppose that my first 
thought would not fly towards those dear, dear friends whom i love and 
who are so deserving of my love?” 

“if this is your present temper, my friend, you will perhaps be glad 
to see a letter that has been lie here some days for you; it is from 
your cousin, i believe.” 





chapter 6 

clerval then put the following letter into my hands. it was from my 
own elizabeth: 

“my dearest cousin, 

“you have been ill, very ill, and even the constant letters of dear 
kind henry are not sufficient to reassure me on your account. you are 
forbidden to write—to hold a pen; yet one word from you, dear victor, 
is necessary to calm our apprehensions. for a long time i have thought 
that each post would bring this line, and my persuasion have 
restrained my uncle from undertaking a journey to ingolstadt. i have 
prevent his encounter the inconvenience and perhaps danger of so 
long a journey, yet how often have i regret not being able to 
perform it myself! i figure to myself that the task of attend on 
your sickbed has devolve on some mercenary old nurse, who could never 
guess your wish nor minister to them with the care and affection of 
your poor cousin. yet that is over now: clerval write that indeed 
you are get better. i eager hope that you will confirm this 
intelligence soon in your own handwriting. 

“get well—and return to us. you will find a happy, cheerful home and 
friends who love you dearly. your father’s health is vigorous, and he 
ask but to see you, but to be assured that you are well; and not a 
care will ever cloud his benevolent countenance. how pleased you would 
be to remark the improvement of our ernest! he is now sixteen and full 
of activity and spirit. he is desirous to be a true switzerland and to enter 
into foreign service, but we cannot part with him, at least until his 
elder brother returns to us. my uncle is not pleased with the idea of 
a military career in a distant country, but ernest never had your 
powers of application. he looks upon study as an odious fetter; his 
time is spent in the open air, climb the hill or rowing on the 
lake. i fear that he will become an idle unless we yield the point 
and permit him to enter on the profession which he has selected. 

“little alteration, except the growth of our dear children, has taken 
place since you left us. the blue lake and snow-clad mountains—they 
never change; and i think our placid home and our contented heart are 
regulate by the same immutable laws. my trifling occupation take up 
my time and amuse me, and i am reward for any exertion by seeing 
none but happy, kind face around me. since you left us, but one 
change has taken place in our little household. do you remember on 
what occasion justine moritz enter our family? probably you do not; 
i will relate her history, therefore in a few words. madame moritz, 
her mother, was a widow with four children, of whom justine was the 
third. this girl had always been the favourite of her father, but 
through a strange perversity, her mother could not endure her, and 
after the death of m. moritz, treat her very ill. my aunt observe 
this, and when justine was twelve years of age, prevail on her mother 
to allow her to live at our house. the republican institution of our 
country have produce simple and happy manners than those which 
prevail in the great monarchy that surround it. hence there is less 
distinction between the several class of its inhabitants; and the 
lower orders, being neither so poor nor so despised, their manners are 
more refined and moral. a servant in geneva do not mean the same 
thing as a servant in france and england. justine, thus received in 
our family, learned the duty of a servant, a condition which, in our 
fortunate country, do not include the idea of ignorance and a 
sacrifice of the dignity of a human being. 

“justine, you may remember, was a great favourite of yours; and i 
recollect you once remark that if you were in an ill humour, one 
glance from justine could dissipate it, for the same reason that 
ariosto give concerning the beauty of angelica—she look so 
frank-hearted and happy. my aunt conceive a great attachment for her, 
by which she was induce to give her an education superior to that 
which she had at first intended. this benefit was fully repaid; 
justine was the most grateful little creature in the world: i do not 
mean that she made any profession i never heard one pass her lips, but 
you could see by her eyes that she almost adore her protectress. 
although her disposition was gay and in many respects inconsiderate, 
yet she paid the greatest attention to every gesture of my aunt. she 
thought her the model of all excellence and endeavour to imitate her 
phraseology and manners, so that even now she often remind me of her. 

“when my dearest aunt die every one was too much occupy in their own 
grief to notice poor justine, who had attend her during her illness 
with the most anxious affection. poor justine was very ill; but other 
trial were reserved for her. 

“one by one, her brother and sister died; and her mother, with the 
exception of her neglect daughter, was left childless. the 
conscience of the woman was troubled; she began to think that the 
death of her favourite was a judgement from heaven to chastise her 
partiality. she was a roman catholic; and i believe her confessor 
confirmed the idea which she had conceived. accordingly, a few month 
after your departure for ingolstadt, justine was call home by her 
repentant mother. poor girl! she wept when she quit our house; she 
was much alter since the death of my aunt; grief had given soft 
and a winning mild to her manners, which had before been remarkable 
for vivacity. nor was her residence at her mother’s house of a nature 
to restore her gaiety. the poor woman was very vacillate in her 
repentance. she sometimes beg justine to forgive her unkindness, 
but much often accused her of have cause the death of her 
brother and sister. perpetual fret at length threw madame moritz 
into a decline, which at first increase her irritability, but she is 
now at peace for ever. she die on the first approach of cold weather, 
at the beginning of this last winter. justine has just return to us; 
and i assure you i love her tenderly. she is very clever and gentle, 
and extremely pretty; as i mention before, her mien and her 
expression continual remind me of my dear aunt. 

“i must say also a few words to you, my dear cousin, of little darling 
william. i wish you could see him; he is very tall of his age, with 
sweet laugh blue eyes, dark eyelashes, and curling hair. when he 
smiles, two little dimple appear on each cheek, which are rosy with 
health. he has already had one or two little _wives,_ but louisa biron 
is his favourite, a pretty little girl of five years of age. 

“now, dear victor, i dare say you wish to be indulge in a little 
gossip concerning the good people of geneva. the pretty miss mansfield 
has already received the congratulatory visit on her approach 
marriage with a young englishman, john melbourne, esq. her ugly 
sister, manon, married m. duvillard, the rich banker, last autumn. your 
favourite schoolfellow, louis manoir, has suffer several misfortune 
since the departure of clerval from geneva. but he has already 
recover his spirits, and is report to be on the point of marry a 
lively pretty frenchwoman, madame tavernier. she is a widow, and much 
old than manoir; but she is very much admired, and a favourite with 
everybody. 

“i have written myself into better spirits, dear cousin; but my anxiety 
returns upon me as i conclude. write, dearest victor,—one line—one 
word will be a blessing to us. ten thousand thanks to henry for his 
kindness, his affection, and his many letters; we are sincerely 
grateful. adieu! my cousin; take care of yourself; and, i entreat 
you, write! 

“elizabeth lavenza. 


“geneva, march 18th, 17—.” 



“dear, dear elizabeth!” i exclaimed, when i had read her 
letter: “i will write instantly and relieve them from the anxiety 
they must feel.” i wrote, and this exertion greatly fatigue me; but 
my convalescence had commenced, and proceed regularly. in another 
fortnight i was able to leave my chamber. 

one of my first duty on my recovery was to introduce clerval to the 
several professor of the university. in do this, i underwent a 
kind of rough usage, ill befitting the wound that my mind had 
sustained. ever since the fatal night, the end of my labours, and the 
beginning of my misfortunes, i had conceive a violent antipathy even 
to the name of natural philosophy. when i was otherwise quite restore 
to health, the sight of a chemical instrument would renew all the agony 
of my nervous symptoms. henry saw this, and had remove all my 
apparatus from my view. he had also change my apartment; for he 
perceive that i had acquire a dislike for the room which had 
previous been my laboratory. but these care of clerval were made of 
no avail when i visit the professors. m. waldman inflict torture 
when he praised, with kindness and warmth, the astonish progress i 
had made in the sciences. he soon perceive that i dislike the 
subject; but not guess the real cause, he attribute my feelings to 
modesty, and change the subject from my improvement, to the science 
itself, with a desire, as i evidently saw, of drawing me out. what 
could i do? he meant to please, and he torment me. i felt as if he 
had placed carefully, one by one, in my view those instrument which 
were to be afterwards use in putt me to a slow and cruel death. i 
writhe under his words, yet dare not exhibit the pain i felt. 
clerval, whose eyes and feelings were always quick in discerning the 
sensation of others, decline the subject, alleging, in excuse, his 
total ignorance; and the conversation took a more general turn. i 
thank my friend from my heart, but i did not speak. i saw plainly 
that he was surprised, but he never attempt to draw my secret from 
me; and although i love him with a mixture of affection and reverence 
that knew no bounds, yet i could never persuade myself to confide in 
him that event which was so often present to my recollection, but which 
i fear the detail to another would only impress more deeply. 

m. krempe was not equally docile; and in my condition at that time, of 
almost insupportable sensitiveness, his harsh blunt encomium gave me even 
more pain than the benevolent approbation of m. waldman. “d—n 
the fellow!” cry he; “why, m. clerval, i assure you he has 
outstript us all. ay, stare if you please; but it is nevertheless true. a 
youngster who, but a few years ago, believe in corneliu agrippa as firm 
as in the gospel, has now set himself at the head of the university; and if 
he is not soon pull down, we shall all be out of countenance.—ay, 
ay,” continue he, observing my face expressive of suffering, 
“m. frankenstein is modest; an excellent quality in a young man. 
young men should be diffident of themselves, you know, m. clerval: i was 
myself when young; but that wear out in a very short time.” 

m. krempe had now commence an eulogy on himself, which happily turn 
the conversation from a subject that was so annoy to me. 

clerval had never sympathise in my taste for natural science; and his 
literary pursuit differ wholly from those which had occupy me. he 
came to the university with the design of making himself complete 
master of the oriental languages, and thus he should open a field for 
the plan of life he had marked out for himself. resolve to pursue no 
inglorious career, he turn his eyes toward the east, as afford 
scope for his spirit of enterprise. the persian, arabic, and sanskrit 
language engaged his attention, and i was easily induce to enter on 
the same studies. idle had ever been irksome to me, and now that i 
wish to fly from reflection, and hate my former studies, i felt 
great relief in being the fellow-pupil with my friend, and found not 
only instruction but consolation in the works of the orientalists. i 
did not, like him, attempt a critical knowledge of their dialects, for 
i did not contemplate making any other use of them than temporary 
amusement. i read merely to understand their meaning, and they well 
repaid my labours. their melancholy is soothing, and their joy 
elevating, to a degree i never experienced in study the author of 
any other country. when you read their writings, life appear to 
consist in a warm sun and a garden of roses,—in the smile and frown 
of a fair enemy, and the fire that consume your own heart. how 
different from the manly and heroic poetry of greece and rome! 

summer pass away in these occupations, and my return to geneva was 
fixed for the latter end of autumn; but being delay by several 
accidents, winter and snow arrived, the road were deem impassable, 
and my journey was retarded until the ensue spring. i felt this 
delay very bitterly; for i long to see my native town and my beloved 
friends. my return had only been delay so long, from an 
unwilling to leave clerval in a strange place, before he had become 
acquaint with any of its inhabitants. the winter, however, was spent 
cheerfully; and although the spring was uncommonly late, when it came 
its beauty compensate for its dilatoriness. 

the month of may had already commenced, and i expect the letter daily 
which was to fix the date of my departure, when henry propose a 
pedestrian tour in the environs of ingolstadt, that i might bid a 
personal farewell to the country i had so long inhabited. i accede 
with pleasure to this proposition: i was fond of exercise, and clerval 
had always been my favourite companion in the ramble of this nature 
that i had taken among the scene of my native country. 

we pass a fortnight in these perambulations: my health and spirits 
had long been restored, and they gain additional strength from the 
salubrious air i breathed, the natural incident of our progress, and 
the conversation of my friend. study had before secluded me from the 
intercourse of my fellow-creatures, and render me unsocial; but 
clerval call forth the better feelings of my heart; he again taught 
me to love the aspect of nature, and the cheerful face of children. 
excellent friend! how sincerely you did love me, and endeavour to 
elevate my mind until it was on a level with your own. a selfish 
pursuit had cramped and narrow me, until your gentle and 
affection warm and open my senses; i became the same happy creature 
who, a few years ago, love and beloved by all, had no sorrow or care. 
when happy, inanimate nature had the power of bestow on me the most 
delightful sensations. a serene sky and verdant field fill me with 
ecstasy. the present season was indeed divine; the flower of spring 
bloom in the hedges, while those of summer were already in bud. i 
was undisturbed by thought which during the preceding year had pressed 
upon me, notwithstanding my endeavour to throw them off, with an 
invincible burden. 

henry rejoice in my gaiety, and sincerely sympathise in my feelings: he 
exert himself to amuse me, while he express the sensation that fill 
his soul. the resources of his mind on this occasion were truly 
astonishing: his conversation was full of imagination; and very often, in 
imitation of the persian and arabic writers, he invent tale of wonderful 
fancy and passion. at other times he repeated my favourite poems, or drew 
me out into arguments, which he support with great ingenuity. 

we return to our college on a sunday afternoon: the peasant were 
dancing, and every one we met appear gay and happy. my own spirits were 
high, and i bound along with feelings of unbridled joy and hilarity. 





chapter 7 

on my return, i found the following letter from my father:— 

“my dear victor, 

“you have probably wait impatient for a letter to fix the date of 
your return to us; and i was at first tempt to write only a few 
lines, merely mention the day on which i should expect you. but 
that would be a cruel kindness, and i dare not do it. what would be 
your surprise, my son, when you expect a happy and glad welcome, to 
behold, on the contrary, tear and wretchedness? and how, victor, can 
i relate our misfortune? absence cannot have render you callous to 
our joy and griefs; and how shall i inflict pain on my long absent 
son? i wish to prepare you for the woeful news, but i know it is 
impossible; even now your eye skim over the page to seek the words 
which are to convey to you the horrible tidings. 

“william is dead!—that sweet child, whose smile delight and warm 
my heart, who was so gentle, yet so gay! victor, he is murdered! 

“i will not attempt to console you; but will simply relate the 
circumstances of the transaction. 

“last thursday (may 7th), i, my niece, and your two brothers, went to 
walk in plainpalais. the evening was warm and serene, and we prolonged 
our walk farther than usual. it was already dusk before we thought of 
returning; and then we discover that william and ernest, who had gone 
on before, were not to be found. we accordingly rest on a seat until 
they should return. presently ernest came, and enquire if we had seen 
his brother; he said, that he had been play with him, that william 
had run away to hide himself, and that he vain sought for him, and 
afterwards wait for a long time, but that he did not return. 

“this account rather alarm us, and we continue to search for him 
until night fell, when elizabeth conjecture that he might have 
return to the house. he was not there. we return again, with 
torches; for i could not rest, when i thought that my sweet boy had 
lost himself, and was expose to all the damp and dew of night; 
elizabeth also suffer extreme anguish. about five in the morning i 
discover my lovely boy, whom the night before i had seen blooming and 
active in health, stretch on the grass livid and motionless; the 
print of the murder’s finger was on his neck. 

“he was convey home, and the anguish that was visible in my 
countenance betray the secret to elizabeth. she was very earnest to 
see the corpse. at first i attempt to prevent her but she persisted, 
and enter the room where it lay, hasty examine the neck of the 
victim, and clasp her hand exclaimed, ‘o god! i have murder my 
darling child!’ 

“she fainted, and was restore with extreme difficulty. when she again 
lived, it was only to weep and sigh. she told me, that that same 
evening william had tease her to let him wear a very valuable 
miniature that she possessed of your mother. this picture is gone, and 
was doubtless the temptation which urge the murder to the deed. we 
have no trace of him at present, although our exertion to discover him 
are unremitted; but they will not restore my beloved william! 

“come, dearest victor; you alone can console elizabeth. she weep 
continually, and accuse herself unjust as the cause of his death; 
her words pierce my heart. we are all unhappy; but will not that be an 
additional motive for you, my son, to return and be our comforter? 
your dear mother! alas, victor! i now say, thank god she did not live 
to witness the cruel, miserable death of her youngest darling! 

“come, victor; not brood thought of vengeance against the assassin, 
but with feelings of peace and gentleness, that will heal, instead of 
festering, the wound of our minds. enter the house of mourning, my 
friend, but with kindness and affection for those who love you, and not 
with hatred for your enemies. 

“your affectionate and afflict father, 

“alphonse frankenstein. 



“geneva, may 12th, 17—.” 



clerval, who had watch my countenance as i read this letter, was 
surprise to observe the despair that succeed the joy i at first 
express on receiving new from my friends. i threw the letter on the 
table, and cover my face with my hands. 

“my dear frankenstein,” exclaim henry, when he perceive me 
weep with bitterness, “are you always to be unhappy? my dear friend, 
what has happened?” 

i motion him to take up the letter, while i walk up and down the 
room in the extremest agitation. tear also gush from the eyes of 
clerval, as he read the account of my misfortune. 

“i can offer you no consolation, my friend,” said he; 
“your disaster is irreparable. what do you intend to do?” 

“to go instantly to geneva: come with me, henry, to order the horses.” 

during our walk, clerval endeavour to say a few words of consolation; 
he could only express his heartfelt sympathy. “poor william!” said he, 
“dear lovely child, he now sleep with his angel mother! who that had 
seen him bright and joyous in his young beauty, but must weep over his 
untimely loss! to die so miserably; to feel the murderer’s grasp! how 
much more a murder that could destroy radiant innocence! poor little 
fellow! one only consolation have we; his friends mourn and weep, but 
he is at rest. the pang is over, his suffering are at an end for ever. 
a sod cover his gentle form, and he know no pain. he can no long 
be a subject for pity; we must reserve that for his miserable 
survivors.” 

clerval spoke thus as we hurried through the streets; the words 
impress themselves on my mind and i remember them afterwards in 
solitude. but now, as soon as the horse arrived, i hurried into a 
cabriolet, and bade farewell to my friend. 

my journey was very melancholy. at first i wish to hurry on, for i long 
to console and sympathise with my love and sorrow friends; but when i 
drew near my native town, i slacken my progress. i could hardly sustain 
the multitude of feelings that crowded into my mind. i pass through 
scene familiar to my youth, but which i had not seen for nearly six years. 
how alter every thing might be during that time! one sudden and 
desolate change had taken place; but a thousand little circumstances 
might have by degree work other alterations, which, although they were 
done more tranquilly, might not be the less decisive. fear overcame me; i 
dare no advance, dread a thousand nameless evil that made me tremble, 
although i was unable to define them. 

i remain two days at lausanne, in this painful state of mind. i 
contemplate the lake: the waters were placid; all around was calm; and the 
snowy mountains, “the palace of nature,” were not changed. by 
degree the calm and heavenly scene restore me, and i continue my journey 
towards geneva. 

the road ran by the side of the lake, which became narrow as i 
approach my native town. i discover more distinct the black 
side of jura, and the bright summit of mont blanc. i wept like a 
child. “dear mountains! my own beautiful lake! how do you welcome your 
wanderer? your summit are clear; the sky and lake are blue and 
placid. is this to prognosticate peace, or to mock at my unhappiness?” 

i fear, my friend, that i shall render myself tedious by dwelling on 
these preliminary circumstances; but they were days of comparative 
happiness, and i think of them with pleasure. my country, my beloved 
country! who but a native can tell the delight i took in again 
behold thy streams, thy mountains, and, more than all, thy lovely 
lake! 

yet, as i drew near home, grief and fear again overcame me. night also 
closed around; and when i could hardly see the dark mountains, i felt still 
more gloomily. the picture appear a vast and dim scene of evil, and i 
foresaw obscure that i was destined to become the most wretched of human 
beings. alas! i prophesy truly, and fail only in one single 
circumstance, that in all the misery i imagine and dreaded, i did not 
conceive the hundredth part of the anguish i was destined to endure. 

it was completely dark when i arrive in the environs of geneva; the gate 
of the town were already shut; and i was oblige to pass the night at 
secheron, a village at the distance of half a league from the city. the sky 
was serene; and, as i was unable to rest, i resolve to visit the spot 
where my poor william had been murdered. as i could not pass through the 
town, i was oblige to cross the lake in a boat to arrive at plainpalais. 
during this short voyage i saw the lightning play on the summit of mont 
blanc in the most beautiful figures. the storm appear to approach 
rapidly, and, on landing, i ascend a low hill, that i might observe its 
progress. it advanced; the heaven were clouded, and i soon felt the rain 
coming slow in large drops, but its violence quick increased. 

i quit my seat, and walk on, although the dark and storm 
increase every minute, and the thunder burst with a terrific crash 
over my head. it was echo from salêve, the juras, and the alps of 
savoy; vivid flash of lightning dazzle my eyes, illuminating the 
lake, making it appear like a vast sheet of fire; then for an instant 
every thing seem of a pitchy darkness, until the eye recover itself 
from the preceding flash. the storm, as is often the case in 
switzerland, appear at once in various parts of the heavens. the 
most violent storm hung exactly north of the town, over the part of the 
lake which lie between the promontory of belrive and the village of 
copêt. another storm enlightened jura with faint flashes; and another 
darken and sometimes disclose the môle, a peaked mountain to the 
east of the lake. 

while i watch the tempest, so beautiful yet terrific, i wander on with 
a hasty step. this noble war in the sky elevated my spirits; i clasp my 
hands, and exclaim aloud, “william, dear angel! this is thy 
funeral, this thy dirge!” as i said these words, i perceive in the 
gloom a figure which stole from behind a clump of tree near me; i stood 
fixed, gaze intently: i could not be mistaken. a flash of lightning 
illuminate the object, and discover its shape plainly to me; its 
gigantic stature, and the deformity of its aspect more hideous than belong 
to humanity, instantly informed me that it was the wretch, the filthy 
dæmon, to whom i had given life. what did he there? could he be (i 
shudder at the conception) the murder of my brother? no soon did that 
idea cross my imagination, than i became convinced of its truth; my teeth 
chattered, and i was forced to lean against a tree for support. the figure 
pass me quickly, and i lost it in the gloom. nothing in human shape could 
have destroy the fair child. _he_ was the murderer! i could not 
doubt it. the mere presence of the idea was an irresistible proof of the 
fact. i thought of pursue the devil; but it would have been in vain, for 
another flash discover him to me hanging among the rocks of the nearly 
perpendicular ascent of mont salêve, a hill that bounds plainpalai on the 
south. he soon reach the summit, and disappeared. 

i remain motionless. the thunder ceased; but the rain still 
continued, and the scene was envelop in an impenetrable darkness. i 
revolve in my mind the event which i had until now sought to forget: 
the whole train of my progress toward the creation; the appearance of 
the works of my own hand at my bedside; its departure. two years had 
now nearly elapse since the night on which he first received life; and 
was this his first crime? alas! i had turn loose into the world a 
deprave wretch, whose delight was in carnage and misery; had he not 
murder my brother? 

no one can conceive the anguish i suffer during the remainder of the 
night, which i spent, cold and wet, in the open air. but i did not 
feel the inconvenience of the weather; my imagination was busy in 
scene of evil and despair. i considered the being whom i had cast 
among mankind, and endow with the will and power to effect purpose 
of horror, such as the deed which he had now done, nearly in the light 
of my own vampire, my own spirit let loose from the grave, and forced 
to destroy all that was dear to me. 

day dawned; and i direct my steps towards the town. the gate were 
open, and i hasten to my father’s house. my first thought was to 
discover what i knew of the murderer, and cause instant pursuit to be 
made. but i pause when i reflect on the story that i had to tell. a 
being whom i myself had formed, and endue with life, had met me at 
midnight among the precipice of an inaccessible mountain. i 
remember also the nervous fever with which i had been seize just at 
the time that i dated my creation, and which would give an air of 
delirium to a tale otherwise so utterly improbable. i well knew that 
if any other had communicate such a relation to me, i should have 
look upon it as the ravings of insanity. besides, the strange nature 
of the animal would elude all pursuit, even if i were so far credit 
as to persuade my relative to commence it. and then of what use would 
be pursuit? who could arrest a creature capable of scale the 
overhang side of mont salêve? these reflection determined me, and 
i resolve to remain silent. 

it was about five in the morning when i enter my father’s house. i 
told the servant not to disturb the family, and went into the library 
to attend their usual hour of rising. 

six years had elapsed, pass in a dream but for one indelible trace, and i 
stood in the same place where i had last embrace my father before my 
departure for ingolstadt. beloved and venerable parent! he still remain 
to me. i gaze on the picture of my mother, which stood over the 
mantel-piece. it was an historical subject, paint at my father’s 
desire, and represent caroline beaufort in an agony of despair, kneel 
by the coffin of her dead father. her garb was rustic, and her cheek pale; 
but there was an air of dignity and beauty, that hardly permit the 
sentiment of pity. below this picture was a miniature of william; and my 
tear flow when i look upon it. while i was thus engaged, ernest 
entered: he had heard me arrive, and hasten to welcome me: 
“welcome, my dearest victor,” said he. “ah! i wish you 
had come three month ago, and then you would have found us all joyous and 
delighted. you come to us now to share a misery which nothing can 
alleviate; yet your presence will, i hope, revive our father, who seem 
sink under his misfortune; and your persuasion will induce poor 
elizabeth to cease her vain and torment self-accusations.—poor 
william! he was our darling and our pride!” 

tears, unrestrained, fell from my brother’s eyes; a sense of mortal 
agony crept over my frame. before, i had only imagine the 
wretched of my desolate home; the reality came on me as a new, and 
a not less terrible, disaster. i try to calm ernest; i enquire more 
minutely concerning my father, and here i name my cousin. 

“she most of all,” said ernest, “requires consolation; she accused 
herself of have cause the death of my brother, and that made her 
very wretched. but since the murder has been discovered—” 

“the murder discovered! good god! how can that be? who could attempt 
to pursue him? it is impossible; one might as well try to overtake the 
winds, or confine a mountain-stream with a straw. i saw him too; he 
was free last night!” 

“i do not know what you mean,” reply my brother, in accent of 
wonder, “but to us the discovery we have made complete our misery. no 
one would believe it at first; and even now elizabeth will not be 
convinced, notwithstanding all the evidence. indeed, who would credit 
that justine moritz, who was so amiable, and fond of all the family, 
could sudden become so capable of so frightful, so appalling a crime?” 

“justine moritz! poor, poor girl, is she the accused? but it is 
wrongfully; every one know that; no one believe it, surely, ernest?” 

“no one did at first; but several circumstances came out, that have 
almost forced conviction upon us; and her own behaviour has been so 
confused, as to add to the evidence of fact a weight that, i fear, 
leaves no hope for doubt. but she will be try today, and you will 
then hear all.” 

he then related that, the morning on which the murder of poor william 
had been discovered, justine had been taken ill, and confine to her 
bed for several days. during this interval, one of the servants, 
happening to examine the apparel she had worn on the night of the 
murder, had discover in her pocket the picture of my mother, which 
had been judge to be the temptation of the murderer. the servant 
instantly show it to one of the others, who, without saying a word to 
any of the family, went to a magistrate; and, upon their deposition, 
justine was apprehended. on being charged with the fact, the poor girl 
confirmed the suspicion in a great measure by her extreme confusion of 
manner. 

this was a strange tale, but it did not shake my faith; and i reply 
earnestly, “you are all mistaken; i know the murderer. justine, poor, 
good justine, is innocent.” 

at that instant my father entered. i saw unhappy deep impress 
on his countenance, but he endeavour to welcome me cheerfully; and, 
after we had exchange our mournful greeting, would have introduce 
some other topic than that of our disaster, had not ernest exclaimed, 
“good god, papa! victor say that he know who was the murder of 
poor william.” 

“we do also, unfortunately,” reply my father, “for indeed i had 
rather have been for ever ignorant than have discover so much 
depravity and ungratitude in one i value so highly.” 

“my dear father, you are mistaken; justine is innocent.” 

“if she is, god forbid that she should suffer as guilty. she is to be 
try today, and i hope, i sincerely hope, that she will be acquitted.” 

this speech calm me. i was firm convinced in my own mind that 
justine, and indeed every human being, was guiltless of this murder. i 
had no fear, therefore, that any circumstantial evidence could be 
brought forward strong enough to convict her. my tale was not one to 
announce publicly; its astound horror would be look upon as 
madness by the vulgar. did any one indeed exist, except i, the 
creator, who would believe, unless his senses convinced him, in the 
existence of the living monument of presumption and rash ignorance 
which i had let loose upon the world? 

we were soon join by elizabeth. time had alter her since i last 
beheld her; it had endow her with lovely surpassing the beauty of 
her childish years. there was the same candour, the same vivacity, but 
it was allied to an expression more full of sensibility and intellect. 
she welcome me with the greatest affection. “your arrival, my dear 
cousin,” said she, “fills me with hope. you perhaps will find some 
means to justify my poor guiltless justine. alas! who is safe, if she 
be convict of crime? i rely on her innocence as certainly as i do 
upon my own. our misfortune is doubly hard to us; we have not only 
lost that lovely darling boy, but this poor girl, whom i sincerely 
love, is to be torn away by even a worse fate. if she is condemned, i 
never shall know joy more. but she will not, i am sure she will not; 
and then i shall be happy again, even after the sad death of my little 
william.” 

“she is innocent, my elizabeth,” said i, “and that shall 
be proved; fear nothing, but let your spirits be cheer by the assurance 
of her acquittal.” 

“how kind and generous you are! every one else believe in her guilt, 
and that made me wretched, for i knew that it was impossible: and to 
see every one else prejudiced in so deadly a manner render me 
hopeless and despairing.” she wept. 

“dearest niece,” said my father, “dry your tears. if she 
is, as you believe, innocent, rely on the justice of our laws, and the 
activity with which i shall prevent the slightest shadow of 
partiality.” 





chapter 8 

we pass a few sad hour until eleven o’clock, when the trial was to 
commence. my father and the rest of the family being oblige to attend 
as witnesses, i accompany them to the court. during the whole of 
this wretched mockery of justice i suffer living torture. it was to 
be decided whether the result of my curiosity and lawless device would 
cause the death of two of my fellow beings: one a smile babe full of 
innocence and joy, the other far more dreadfully murdered, with every 
aggravation of infamy that could make the murder memorable in horror. 
justine also was a girl of merit and possessed quality which promise 
to render her life happy; now all was to be obliterate in an 
ignominious grave, and i the cause! a thousand times rather would i 
have confessed myself guilty of the crime ascribe to justine, but i 
was absent when it was committed, and such a declaration would have 
been considered as the ravings of a madman and would not have 
exculpate her who suffer through me. 

the appearance of justine was calm. she was dress in mourning, and 
her countenance, always engaging, was rendered, by the solemnity of her 
feelings, exquisite beautiful. yet she appear confident in 
innocence and did not tremble, although gaze on and execrate by 
thousands, for all the kindness which her beauty might otherwise have 
excited was obliterate in the mind of the spectator by the 
imagination of the enormity she was supposed to have committed. she 
was tranquil, yet her tranquill was evidently constrained; and as 
her confusion had before been adduce as a proof of her guilt, she 
work up her mind to an appearance of courage. when she enter the 
court she threw her eyes round it and quick discover where we were 
seated. a tear seem to dim her eye when she saw us, but she quick 
recover herself, and a look of sorrowful affection seem to attest 
her utter guiltlessness. 

the trial began, and after the advocate against her had state the 
charge, several witness were called. several strange fact combine 
against her, which might have stagger anyone who had not such proof 
of her innocence as i had. she had been out the whole of the night on 
which the murder had been committed and towards morning had been 
perceive by a market-woman not far from the spot where the body of the 
murder child had been afterwards found. the woman ask her what she 
did there, but she look very strange and only return a confuse 
and unintelligible answer. she return to the house about eight 
o’clock, and when one inquire where she had pass the night, she 
reply that she had been look for the child and demand earnest 
if anything had been heard concerning him. when shown the body, she 
fell into violent hysterics and kept her bed for several days. the 
picture was then produce which the servant had found in her pocket; 
and when elizabeth, in a falter voice, prove that it was the same 
which, an hour before the child had been missed, she had placed round 
his neck, a murmur of horror and indignation fill the court. 

justine was call on for her defence. as the trial had proceeded, her 
countenance had altered. surprise, horror, and misery were strong 
expressed. sometimes she struggle with her tears, but when she was 
desire to plead, she collected her powers and spoke in an audible 
although variable voice. 

“god knows,” she said, “how entire i am innocent. but i 
do not pretend that my protestation should acquit me; i rest my innocence 
on a plain and simple explanation of the fact which have been adduce 
against me, and i hope the character i have always borne will incline my 
judge to a favourable interpretation where any circumstance appear 
doubtful or suspicious.” 

she then related that, by the permission of elizabeth, she had pass 
the evening of the night on which the murder had been committed at the 
house of an aunt at chêne, a village situated at about a league from 
geneva. on her return, at about nine o’clock, she met a man who ask 
her if she had seen anything of the child who was lost. she was 
alarm by this account and pass several hour in look for him, 
when the gate of geneva were shut, and she was forced to remain 
several hour of the night in a barn belong to a cottage, being 
unwill to call up the inhabitants, to whom she was well known. most 
of the night she spent here watching; towards morning she believe that 
she slept for a few minutes; some steps disturbed her, and she awoke. 
it was dawn, and she quit her asylum, that she might again endeavour 
to find my brother. if she had gone near the spot where his body lay, 
it was without her knowledge. that she had been bewilder when 
question by the market-woman was not surprising, since she had pass 
a sleepless night and the fate of poor william was yet uncertain. 
concerning the picture she could give no account. 

“i know,” continue the unhappy victim, “how heavy and 
fatally this one circumstance weigh against me, but i have no power of 
explain it; and when i have express my utter ignorance, i am only left 
to conjecture concerning the probability by which it might have been 
placed in my pocket. but here also i am checked. i believe that i have no 
enemy on earth, and none surely would have been so wicked as to destroy me 
wantonly. did the murder place it there? i know of no opportunity 
afford him for so doing; or, if i had, why should he have stolen the 
jewel, to part with it again so soon? 

“i commit my cause to the justice of my judges, yet i see no room for 
hope. i beg permission to have a few witness examine concerning my 
character, and if their testimony shall not overweigh my supposed 
guilt, i must be condemned, although i would pledge my salvation on my 
innocence.” 

several witness were call who had known her for many years, and 
they spoke well of her; but fear and hatred of the crime of which they 
supposed her guilty render them timorous and unwill to come 
forward. elizabeth saw even this last resource, her excellent 
disposition and irreproachable conduct, about to fail the accused, 
when, although violent agitated, she desire permission to address 
the court. 

“i am,” said she, “the cousin of the unhappy child who 
was murdered, or rather his sister, for i was educated by and have live 
with his parent ever since and even long before his birth. it may 
therefore be judge indecent in me to come forward on this occasion, but 
when i see a fellow creature about to perish through the cowardice of her 
pretended friends, i wish to be allow to speak, that i may say what i 
know of her character. i am well acquaint with the accused. i have live 
in the same house with her, at one time for five and at another for nearly 
two years. during all that period she appear to me the most amiable and 
benevolent of human creatures. she nurse madame frankenstein, my aunt, in 
her last illness, with the greatest affection and care and afterwards 
attend her own mother during a tedious illness, in a manner that excited 
the admiration of all who knew her, after which she again live in my 
uncle’s house, where she was beloved by all the family. she was 
warm attach to the child who is now dead and act towards him like a 
most affectionate mother. for my own part, i do not hesitate to say that, 
notwithstanding all the evidence produce against her, i believe and rely 
on her perfect innocence. she had no temptation for such an action; as to 
the bauble on which the chief proof rests, if she had earnest desire it, 
i should have willing given it to her, so much do i esteem and value 
her.” 

a murmur of approbation follow elizabeth’s simple and powerful 
appeal, but it was excited by her generous interference, and not in 
favour of poor justine, on whom the public indignation was turn with 
renew violence, charge her with the blackest ingratitude. she 
herself wept as elizabeth spoke, but she did not answer. my own 
agitation and anguish was extreme during the whole trial. i believe 
in her innocence; i knew it. could the dæmon who had (i did not for a 
minute doubt) murder my brother also in his hellish sport have 
betray the innocent to death and ignominy? i could not sustain the 
horror of my situation, and when i perceive that the popular voice and 
the countenance of the judge had already condemn my unhappy victim, 
i rush out of the court in agony. the torture of the accused did 
not equal mine; she was sustain by innocence, but the fang of 
remorse tore my bosom and would not forgo their hold. 

i pass a night of unmingled wretchedness. in the morning i went to 
the court; my lip and throat were parched. i dare not ask the fatal 
question, but i was known, and the officer guess the cause of my 
visit. the ballot had been thrown; they were all black, and justine 
was condemned. 

i cannot pretend to describe what i then felt. i had before 
experienced sensation of horror, and i have endeavour to bestow upon 
them adequate expressions, but words cannot convey an idea of the 
heart-sickening despair that i then endured. the person to whom i 
address myself add that justine had already confessed her guilt. 
“that evidence,” he observed, “was hardly require in so glaring a 
case, but i am glad of it, and, indeed, none of our judge like to 
condemn a criminal upon circumstantial evidence, be it ever so 
decisive.” 

this was strange and unexpected intelligence; what could it mean? had 
my eyes deceive me? and was i really as mad as the whole world would 
believe me to be if i disclose the object of my suspicions? i 
hasten to return home, and elizabeth eager demand the result. 

“my cousin,” reply i, “it is decided as you may have expected; all 
judge had rather that ten innocent should suffer than that one guilty 
should escape. but she has confessed.” 

this was a dire blow to poor elizabeth, who had rely with firm upon 
justine’s innocence. “alas!” said she. “how shall i 
ever again believe in human goodness? justine, whom i love and esteem as 
my sister, how could she put on those smile of innocence only to betray? 
her mild eyes seem incapable of any severity or guile, and yet she has 
committed a murder.” 

soon after we heard that the poor victim had express a desire to see my 
cousin. my father wish her not to go but said that he left it to her own 
judgment and feelings to decide. “yes,” said elizabeth, 
“i will go, although she is guilty; and you, victor, shall accompany 
me; i cannot go alone.” the idea of this visit was torture to me, yet 
i could not refuse. 

we enter the gloomy prison chamber and beheld justine sitting on some 
straw at the farther end; her hand were manacled, and her head rest on 
her knees. she rose on seeing us enter, and when we were left alone with 
her, she threw herself at the feet of elizabeth, weeping bitterly. my 
cousin wept also. 

“oh, justine!” said she. “why did you rob me of my last consolation? 
i rely on your innocence, and although i was then very wretched, i 
was not so miserable as i am now.” 

“and do you also believe that i am so very, very wicked? do you also 
join with my enemy to crush me, to condemn me as a murderer?” her 
voice was suffocate with sobs. 

“rise, my poor girl,” said elizabeth; “why do you kneel, 
if you are innocent? i am not one of your enemies, i believe you 
guiltless, notwithstanding every evidence, until i heard that you had 
yourself declared your guilt. that report, you say, is false; and be 
assured, dear justine, that nothing can shake my confidence in you for a 
moment, but your own confession.” 

“i did confess, but i confessed a lie. i confessed, that i might 
obtain absolution; but now that falsehood lie heavy at my heart than 
all my other sins. the god of heaven forgive me! ever since i was 
condemned, my confessor has besiege me; he threaten and menaced, 
until i almost began to think that i was the monster that he said i 
was. he threaten excommunication and hell fire in my last moments if 
i continue obdurate. dear lady, i had none to support me; all look 
on me as a wretch doom to ignominy and perdition. what could i do? 
in an evil hour i subscribe to a lie; and now only am i truly 
miserable.” 

she paused, weeping, and then continued, “i thought with horror, my 
sweet lady, that you should believe your justine, whom your blessed 
aunt had so highly honoured, and whom you loved, was a creature capable 
of a crime which none but the devil himself could have perpetrated. 
dear william! dearest blessed child! i soon shall see you again in 
heaven, where we shall all be happy; and that console me, go as i 
am to suffer ignominy and death.” 

“oh, justine! forgive me for have for one moment distrust you. 
why did you confess? but do not mourn, dear girl. do not fear. i 
will proclaim, i will prove your innocence. i will melt the stony 
heart of your enemy by my tear and prayers. you shall not die! 
you, my playfellow, my companion, my sister, perish on the scaffold! 
no! no! i never could survive so horrible a misfortune.” 

justine shook her head mournfully. “i do not fear to die,” she said; 
“that pang is past. god raise my weakness and give me courage to 
endure the worst. i leave a sad and bitter world; and if you remember 
me and think of me as of one unjust condemned, i am resigned to the 
fate await me. learn from me, dear lady, to submit in patience to 
the will of heaven!” 

during this conversation i had retired to a corner of the prison room, 
where i could conceal the horrid anguish that possessed me. despair! 
who dare talk of that? the poor victim, who on the morrow was to pass 
the awful boundary between life and death, felt not, as i did, such 
deep and bitter agony. i gnash my teeth and ground them together, 
utter a groan that came from my inmost soul. justine started. when 
she saw who it was, she approach me and said, “dear sir, you are very 
kind to visit me; you, i hope, do not believe that i am guilty?” 

i could not answer. “no, justine,” said elizabeth; “he is more 
convinced of your innocence than i was, for even when he heard that you 
had confessed, he did not credit it.” 

“i truly thank him. in these last moments i feel the sincerest 
gratitude towards those who think of me with kindness. how sweet is 
the affection of other to such a wretch as i am! it remove more than 
half my misfortune, and i feel as if i could die in peace now that my 
innocence is acknowledge by you, dear lady, and your cousin.” 

thus the poor sufferer try to comfort other and herself. she indeed 
gain the resignation she desired. but i, the true murderer, felt the 
never-dying worm alive in my bosom, which allow of no hope or 
consolation. elizabeth also wept and was unhappy, but hers also was 
the misery of innocence, which, like a cloud that pass over the fair 
moon, for a while hide but cannot tarnish its brightness. anguish and 
despair had penetrate into the core of my heart; i bore a hell within 
me which nothing could extinguish. we stay several hour with 
justine, and it was with great difficulty that elizabeth could tear 
herself away. “i wish,” cry she, “that i were to die with you; i 
cannot live in this world of misery.” 

justine assume an air of cheerfulness, while she with difficulty 
repressed her bitter tears. she embrace elizabeth and said in a voice 
of half-suppressed emotion, “farewell, sweet lady, dearest elizabeth, 
my beloved and only friend; may heaven, in its bounty, bless and 
preserve you; may this be the last misfortune that you will ever 
suffer! live, and be happy, and make other so.” 

and on the morrow justine died. elizabeth’s heart-rending eloquence 
fail to move the judge from their settled conviction in the 
criminal of the saintly sufferer. my passionate and indignant 
appeal were lost upon them. and when i received their cold answer 
and heard the harsh, unfeeling reasoning of these men, my purpose 
avowal die away on my lips. thus i might proclaim myself a madman, 
but not revoke the sentence pass upon my wretched victim. she 
perish on the scaffold as a murderess! 

from the torture of my own heart, i turn to contemplate the deep and 
voiceless grief of my elizabeth. this also was my doing! and my 
father’s woe, and the desolate of that late so smile home all was 
the work of my thrice-accursed hands! ye weep, unhappy ones, but these 
are not your last tears! again shall you raise the funeral wail, and 
the sound of your lamentation shall again and again be heard! 
frankenstein, your son, your kinsman, your early, much-loved friend; he 
who would spend each vital drop of blood for your sakes, who has no 
thought nor sense of joy except as it is mirror also in your dear 
countenances, who would fill the air with blessing and spend his life 
in serving you—he bid you weep, to shed countless tears; happy beyond 
his hopes, if thus inexorable fate be satisfied, and if the destruction 
pause before the peace of the grave have succeed to your sad torments! 

thus spoke my prophetic soul, as, torn by remorse, horror, and despair, 
i beheld those i love spend vain sorrow upon the grave of william and 
justine, the first hapless victim to my unhallowed arts. 





chapter 9 

nothing is more painful to the human mind than, after the feelings have 
been work up by a quick succession of events, the dead calm of 
inaction and certainty which follow and deprive the soul both of hope 
and fear. justine died, she rested, and i was alive. the blood flow 
freely in my veins, but a weight of despair and remorse pressed on my 
heart which nothing could remove. sleep flee from my eyes; i wander 
like an evil spirit, for i had committed deed of mischief beyond 
description horrible, and more, much more (i persuade myself) was yet 
behind. yet my heart overflow with kindness and the love of virtue. 
i had begun life with benevolent intentions and thirst for the moment 
when i should put them in practice and make myself useful to my fellow 
beings. now all was blasted; instead of that serene of conscience 
which allow me to look back upon the past with self-satisfaction, and 
from thence to gather promise of new hopes, i was seize by remorse and 
the sense of guilt, which hurried me away to a hell of intense torture 
such as no language can describe. 

this state of mind prey upon my health, which had perhaps never 
entire recover from the first shock it had sustained. i shun 
the face of man; all sound of joy or complacency was torture to me; 
solitude was my only consolation—deep, dark, deathlike solitude. 

my father observe with pain the alteration perceptible in my disposition 
and habit and endeavour by argument deduce from the feelings of his 
serene conscience and guiltless life to inspire me with fortitude and 
awaken in me the courage to dispel the dark cloud which brood over me. 
“do you think, victor,” said he, “that i do not suffer 
also? no one could love a child more than i love your 
brother”—tears came into his eyes as he spoke—“but 
is it not a duty to the survivor that we should refrain from augment 
their unhappy by an appearance of immoderate grief? it is also a duty 
owe to yourself, for excessive sorrow prevent improvement or enjoyment, 
or even the discharge of daily usefulness, without which no man is fit for 
society.” 

this advice, although good, was total inapplicable to my case; i 
should have been the first to hide my grief and console my friends if 
remorse had not mingle its bitterness, and terror its alarm, with my 
other sensations. now i could only answer my father with a look of 
despair and endeavour to hide myself from his view. 

about this time we retired to our house at belrive. this change was 
particularly agreeable to me. the shut of the gate regularly at 
ten o’clock and the impossible of remain on the lake after that 
hour had render our residence within the wall of geneva very irksome 
to me. i was now free. often, after the rest of the family had 
retired for the night, i took the boat and pass many hour upon the 
water. sometimes, with my sail set, i was carry by the wind; and 
sometimes, after rowing into the middle of the lake, i left the boat to 
pursue its own course and gave way to my own miserable reflections. i 
was often tempted, when all was at peace around me, and i the only 
unquiet thing that wander restless in a scene so beautiful and 
heavenly—if i except some bat, or the frogs, whose harsh and 
interrupt croak was heard only when i approach the shore—often, 
i say, i was tempt to plunge into the silent lake, that the waters 
might close over me and my calamity for ever. but i was restrained, 
when i thought of the heroic and suffering elizabeth, whom i tender 
loved, and whose existence was bound up in mine. i thought also of my 
father and survive brother; should i by my base desertion leave them 
expose and unprotected to the malice of the fiend whom i had let loose 
among them? 

at these moments i wept bitter and wish that peace would revisit my 
mind only that i might afford them consolation and happiness. but that 
could not be. remorse extinguish every hope. i had been the author of 
unalterable evils, and i live in daily fear lest the monster whom i had 
create should perpetrate some new wickedness. i had an obscure feeling 
that all was not over and that he would still commit some signal crime, 
which by its enormity should almost efface the recollection of the past. 
there was always scope for fear so long as anything i love remain 
behind. my abhorrence of this fiend cannot be conceived. when i thought of 
him i gnash my teeth, my eyes became inflamed, and i ardent wish to 
extinguish that life which i had so thoughtless bestowed. when i 
reflect on his crime and malice, my hatred and revenge burst all bounds 
of moderation. i would have made a pilgrimage to the highest peak of the 
andes, could i, when there, have precipitate him to their base. i wish 
to see him again, that i might wreak the utmost extent of abhorrence on his 
head and avenge the death of william and justine. 

our house was the house of mourning. my father’s health was deep 
shaken by the horror of the recent events. elizabeth was sad and 
desponding; she no long took delight in her ordinary occupations; all 
pleasure seem to her sacrilege toward the dead; eternal woe and tear she 
then thought was the just tribute she should pay to innocence so blasted 
and destroyed. she was no long that happy creature who in early youth 
wander with me on the bank of the lake and talk with ecstasy of our 
future prospects. the first of those sorrow which are sent to wean us from 
the earth had visit her, and its dim influence quench her dearest 
smiles. 

“when i reflect, my dear cousin,” said she, “on the miserable death of 
justine moritz, i no long see the world and its works as they before 
appear to me. before, i look upon the account of vice and 
injustice that i read in books or heard from other as tale of ancient 
days or imaginary evils; at least they were remote and more familiar to 
reason than to the imagination; but now misery has come home, and men 
appear to me as monster thirst for each other’s blood. yet i am 
certainly unjust. everybody believe that poor girl to be guilty; and 
if she could have committed the crime for which she suffered, assured 
she would have been the most deprave of human creatures. for the sake 
of a few jewels, to have murder the son of her benefactor and friend, 
a child whom she had nurse from its birth, and appear to love as if 
it had been her own! i could not consent to the death of any human 
being, but certainly i should have thought such a creature unfit to 
remain in the society of men. but she was innocent. i know, i feel 
she was innocent; you are of the same opinion, and that confirm me. 
alas! victor, when falsehood can look so like the truth, who can 
assure themselves of certain happiness? i feel as if i were walking on 
the edge of a precipice, towards which thousand are crowd and 
endeavour to plunge me into the abyss. william and justine were 
assassinated, and the murder escapes; he walk about the world free, 
and perhaps respected. but even if i were condemn to suffer on the 
scaffold for the same crimes, i would not change place with such a 
wretch.” 

i listen to this discourse with the extremest agony. i, not in deed, 
but in effect, was the true murderer. elizabeth read my anguish in my 
countenance, and kindly taking my hand, said, “my dearest friend, you 
must calm yourself. these event have affected me, god know how 
deeply; but i am not so wretched as you are. there is an expression of 
despair, and sometimes of revenge, in your countenance that make me 
tremble. dear victor, banish these dark passions. remember the 
friends around you, who centre all their hope in you. have we lost 
the power of rendering you happy? ah! while we love, while we are 
true to each other, here in this land of peace and beauty, your native 
country, we may reap every tranquil blessing—what can disturb our 
peace?” 

and could not such words from her whom i fondly prize before every 
other gift of fortune suffice to chase away the fiend that lurk in my 
heart? even as she spoke i drew near to her, as if in terror, lest at 
that very moment the destroyer had been near to rob me of her. 

thus not the tender of friendship, nor the beauty of earth, nor of 
heaven, could redeem my soul from woe; the very accent of love were 
ineffectual. i was encompass by a cloud which no beneficial 
influence could penetrate. the wound deer drag its faint 
limb to some untrodden brake, there to gaze upon the arrow which had 
pierce it, and to die, was but a type of me. 

sometimes i could cope with the sullen despair that overwhelm me, but 
sometimes the whirlwind passion of my soul drove me to seek, by bodily 
exercise and by change of place, some relief from my intolerable 
sensations. it was during an access of this kind that i sudden left 
my home, and bend my steps towards the near alpine valleys, sought 
in the magnificence, the eternity of such scenes, to forget myself and 
my ephemeral, because human, sorrows. my wanderings were direct 
towards the valley of chamounix. i had visit it frequent during my 
boyhood. six years had pass since then: _i_ was a wreck, but nought 
had change in those savage and enduring scenes. 

i perform the first part of my journey on horseback. i afterwards 
hire a mule, as the more sure-footed and least liable to receive 
injury on these rugged roads. the weather was fine; it was about the 
middle of the month of august, nearly two month after the death of 
justine, that miserable epoch from which i dated all my woe. the 
weight upon my spirit was sensible lighten as i plunge yet deep in 
the ravine of arve. the immense mountain and precipice that overhung 
me on every side, the sound of the river rage among the rocks, and 
the dashing of the waterfall around spoke of a power mighty as 
omnipotence—and i cease to fear or to bend before any being less 
almighty than that which had create and rule the elements, here 
display in their most terrific guise. still, as i ascend higher, 
the valley assume a more magnificent and astonish character. 
ruin castle hanging on the precipice of piny mountains, the 
impetuous arve, and cottage every here and there peep forth from 
among the tree form a scene of singular beauty. but it was 
augment and render sublime by the mighty alps, whose white and 
shining pyramid and dome tower above all, as belong to another 
earth, the habitation of another race of beings. 

i pass the bridge of pélissier, where the ravine, which the river 
forms, open before me, and i began to ascend the mountain that 
overhang it. soon after, i enter the valley of chamounix. this 
valley is more wonderful and sublime, but not so beautiful and 
picturesque as that of servox, through which i had just passed. the 
high and snowy mountain were its immediate boundaries, but i saw no 
more ruin castle and fertile fields. immense glacier approach 
the road; i heard the rumbling thunder of the fall avalanche and 
marked the smoke of its passage. mont blanc, the supreme and 
magnificent mont blanc, raise itself from the surrounding _aiguilles_, 
and its tremendous _dôme_ overlook the valley. 

a tingle long-lost sense of pleasure often came across me during this 
journey. some turn in the road, some new object sudden perceive and 
recognised, remind me of days gone by, and were associate with the 
lighthearte gaiety of boyhood. the very wind whisper in soothe 
accents, and maternal nature bade me weep no more. then again the 
kindly influence cease to act—i found myself fetter again to grief 
and indulge in all the misery of reflection. then i spur on my
