animal, strive so to forget the world, my fears, and more than all, 
myself—or, in a more desperate fashion, i alight and threw myself on 
the grass, weigh down by horror and despair. 

at length i arrive at the village of chamounix. exhaustion succeed 
to the extreme fatigue both of body and of mind which i had endured. 
for a short space of time i remain at the window watch the pallid 
lightning that play above mont blanc and listen to the rush of 
the arve, which pursue its noisy way beneath. the same lull sound 
act as a lullaby to my too keen sensations; when i placed my head 
upon my pillow, sleep crept over me; i felt it as it came and blessed 
the give of oblivion. 





chapter 10 

i spent the following day roam through the valley. i stood beside 
the source of the arveiron, which take their rise in a glacier, that 
with slow pace is advance down from the summit of the hill to 
barricade the valley. the abrupt side of vast mountain were before 
me; the icy wall of the glacier overhung me; a few shatter pine were 
scattered around; and the solemn silence of this glorious 
presence-chamber of imperial nature was broken only by the brawl 
wave or the fall of some vast fragment, the thunder sound of the 
avalanche or the cracking, reverberate along the mountains, of the 
accumulate ice, which, through the silent working of immutable laws, 
was ever and anon rent and torn, as if it had been but a plaything in 
their hands. these sublime and magnificent scene afford me the 
greatest consolation that i was capable of receiving. they elevated me 
from all little of feeling, and although they did not remove my 
grief, they subdued and tranquillise it. in some degree, also, they 
divert my mind from the thought over which it had brood for the 
last month. i retired to rest at night; my slumbers, as it were, 
wait on and minister to by the assemble of grand shape which i 
had contemplate during the day. they congregate round me; the 
unstained snowy mountain-top, the glittering pinnacle, the pine woods, 
and ragged bare ravine, the eagle, soar amidst the clouds—they all 
gather round me and bade me be at peace. 

where had they flee when the next morning i awoke? all of 
soul-inspiriting flee with sleep, and dark melancholy cloud every 
thought. the rain was pour in torrents, and thick mists hid the 
summit of the mountains, so that i even saw not the face of those 
mighty friends. still i would penetrate their misty veil and seek them 
in their cloudy retreats. what were rain and storm to me? my mule was 
brought to the door, and i resolve to ascend to the summit of 
montanvert. i remember the effect that the view of the tremendous 
and ever-moving glacier had produce upon my mind when i first saw it. 
it had then fill me with a sublime ecstasy that gave wings to the 
soul and allow it to soar from the obscure world to light and joy. 
the sight of the awful and majestic in nature had indeed always the 
effect of solemnise my mind and cause me to forget the passing 
care of life. i determined to go without a guide, for i was well 
acquaint with the path, and the presence of another would destroy the 
solitary grandeur of the scene. 

the ascent is precipitous, but the path is cut into continual and short 
windings, which enable you to surmount the perpendicular of the 
mountain. it is a scene terrifically desolate. in a thousand spot 
the trace of the winter avalanche may be perceived, where tree lie 
broken and strew on the ground, some entire destroyed, other bent, 
leaning upon the jut rocks of the mountain or transverse upon 
other trees. the path, as you ascend higher, is intersect by ravine 
of snow, down which stone continual roll from above; one of them is 
particularly dangerous, as the slightest sound, such as even speak 
in a loud voice, produce a concussion of air sufficient to draw 
destruction upon the head of the speaker. the pine are not tall or 
luxuriant, but they are sombre and add an air of severity to the scene. 
i look on the valley beneath; vast mists were rising from the river 
which ran through it and curling in thick wreath around the opposite 
mountains, whose summit were hid in the uniform clouds, while rain 
pour from the dark sky and add to the melancholy impression i 
received from the object around me. alas! why do man boast of 
sensibility superior to those apparent in the brute; it only render 
them more necessary beings. if our impulse were confine to hunger, 
thirst, and desire, we might be nearly free; but now we are move by 
every wind that blow and a chance word or scene that that word may 
convey to us. 

we rest; a dream has power to poison sleep. 
we rise; one wand’ring thought pollute the day. 
we feel, conceive, or reason; laugh or weep, 
embrace fond woe, or cast our care away; 
it is the same: for, be it joy or sorrow, 
the path of its departure still is free. 
man’s yesterday may ne’er be like his morrow; 
nought may endure but mutability! 



it was nearly noon when i arrive at the top of the ascent. for some 
time i sat upon the rock that overlook the sea of ice. a mist cover 
both that and the surrounding mountains. presently a breeze dissipated 
the cloud, and i descended upon the glacier. the surface is very 
uneven, rising like the wave of a trouble sea, descend low, and 
intersperse by rift that sink deep. the field of ice is almost a 
league in width, but i spent nearly two hour in crossing it. the 
opposite mountain is a bare perpendicular rock. from the side where i 
now stood montanvert was exactly opposite, at the distance of a league; 
and above it rose mont blanc, in awful majesty. i remain in a recess 
of the rock, gaze on this wonderful and stupendous scene. the sea, 
or rather the vast river of ice, wound among its dependent mountains, 
whose aerial summit hung over its recesses. their icy and glittering 
peak shone in the sunlight over the clouds. my heart, which was 
before sorrowful, now swell with something like joy; i exclaimed, 
“wandering spirits, if indeed ye wander, and do not rest in your narrow 
beds, allow me this faint happiness, or take me, as your companion, 
away from the joy of life.” 

as i said this i sudden beheld the figure of a man, at some distance, 
advance towards me with superhuman speed. he bound over the 
crevice in the ice, among which i had walk with caution; his 
stature, also, as he approached, seem to exceed that of man. i was 
troubled; a mist came over my eyes, and i felt a faint seize me, 
but i was quick restore by the cold gale of the mountains. i 
perceived, as the shape came near (sight tremendous and abhorred!) 
that it was the wretch whom i had created. i tremble with rage and 
horror, resolve to wait his approach and then close with him in 
mortal combat. he approached; his countenance bespoke bitter anguish, 
combine with disdain and malignity, while its unearthly ugly 
render it almost too horrible for human eyes. but i scarcely 
observe this; rage and hatred had at first deprived me of utterance, 
and i recover only to overwhelm him with words expressive of furious 
detest and contempt. 

“devil,” i exclaimed, “do you dare approach me? and do 
not you fear the fierce vengeance of my arm wreak on your miserable head? 
begone, vile insect! or rather, stay, that i may trample you to dust! and, 
oh! that i could, with the extinction of your miserable existence, restore 
those victim whom you have so diabolical murdered!” 

“i expect this reception,” said the dæmon. “all men hate the 
wretched; how, then, must i be hated, who am miserable beyond all 
living things! yet you, my creator, detest and spurn me, thy creature, 
to whom thou art bound by tie only dissoluble by the annihilate of 
one of us. you purpose to kill me. how dare you sport thus with life? 
do your duty towards me, and i will do mine towards you and the rest of 
mankind. if you will comply with my conditions, i will leave them and 
you at peace; but if you refuse, i will glut the maw of death, until it 
be satiate with the blood of your remain friends.” 

“abhorred monster! fiend that thou art! the torture of hell are too 
mild a vengeance for thy crimes. wretched devil! you reproach me with 
your creation, come on, then, that i may extinguish the spark which i 
so negligent bestowed.” 

my rage was without bounds; i sprang on him, impel by all the 
feelings which can arm one being against the existence of another. 

he easily elude me and said, 

“be calm! i entreat you to hear me before you give vent to your hatred 
on my devoted head. have i not suffer enough, that you seek to 
increase my misery? life, although it may only be an accumulation of 
anguish, is dear to me, and i will defend it. remember, thou hast made 
me more powerful than thyself; my height is superior to thine, my 
joint more supple. but i will not be tempt to set myself in 
opposition to thee. i am thy creature, and i will be even mild and 
docile to my natural lord and king if thou wilt also perform thy part, 
the which thou owest me. oh, frankenstein, be not equitable to every 
other and trample upon me alone, to whom thy justice, and even thy 
clemency and affection, is most due. remember that i am thy creature; 
i ought to be thy adam, but i am rather the fallen angel, whom thou 
drivest from joy for no misdeed. everywhere i see bliss, from which i 
alone am irrevocable excluded. i was benevolent and good; misery made 
me a fiend. make me happy, and i shall again be virtuous.” 

“begone! i will not hear you. there can be no community between you 
and me; we are enemies. begone, or let us try our strength in a fight, 
in which one must fall.” 

“how can i move thee? will no entreaty cause thee to turn a 
favourable eye upon thy creature, who implore thy goodness and 
compassion? believe me, frankenstein, i was benevolent; my soul glow 
with love and humanity; but am i not alone, miserably alone? you, my 
creator, abhor me; what hope can i gather from your fellow creatures, 
who owe me nothing? they spurn and hate me. the desert mountain and 
dreary glacier are my refuge. i have wander here many days; the 
cave of ice, which i only do not fear, are a dwelling to me, and the 
only one which man do not grudge. these bleak sky i hail, for they 
are kind to me than your fellow beings. if the multitude of mankind 
knew of my existence, they would do as you do, and arm themselves for 
my destruction. shall i not then hate them who abhor me? i will keep 
no terms with my enemies. i am miserable, and they shall share my 
wretchedness. yet it is in your power to recompense me, and deliver 
them from an evil which it only remains for you to make so great, that 
not only you and your family, but thousand of others, shall be 
swallow up in the whirlwind of its rage. let your compassion be 
moved, and do not disdain me. listen to my tale; when you have heard 
that, abandon or commiserate me, as you shall judge that i deserve. 
but hear me. the guilty are allowed, by human laws, bloody as they 
are, to speak in their own defence before they are condemned. listen 
to me, frankenstein. you accuse me of murder, and yet you would, with 
a satisfy conscience, destroy your own creature. oh, praise the 
eternal justice of man! yet i ask you not to spare me; listen to me, 
and then, if you can, and if you will, destroy the work of your hands.” 

“why do you call to my remembrance,” i rejoined, “circumstances of 
which i shudder to reflect, that i have been the miserable origin and 
author? cursed be the day, abhor devil, in which you first saw 
light! cursed (although i curse myself) be the hand that form you! 
you have made me wretched beyond expression. you have left me no power 
to consider whether i am just to you or not. begone! relieve me from 
the sight of your detest form.” 

“thus i relieve thee, my creator,” he said, and placed his hate hand 
before my eyes, which i flung from me with violence; “thus i take from 
thee a sight which you abhor. still thou canst listen to me and grant 
me thy compassion. by the virtue that i once possessed, i demand this 
from you. hear my tale; it is long and strange, and the temperature of 
this place is not fitting to your fine sensations; come to the hut upon 
the mountain. the sun is yet high in the heavens; before it descend 
to hide itself behind your snowy precipice and illuminate another 
world, you will have heard my story and can decide. on you it rests, 
whether i quit for ever the neighbourhood of man and lead a harmless 
life, or become the scourge of your fellow creature and the author of 
your own speedy ruin.” 

as he said this he led the way across the ice; i followed. my heart 
was full, and i did not answer him, but as i proceeded, i weigh the 
various argument that he had use and determined at least to listen to 
his tale. i was partly urge by curiosity, and compassion confirmed my 
resolution. i had hitherto supposed him to be the murder of my 
brother, and i eager sought a confirmation or denial of this opinion. 
for the first time, also, i felt what the duty of a creator towards 
his creature were, and that i ought to render him happy before i 
complain of his wickedness. these motive urge me to comply with 
his demand. we cross the ice, therefore, and ascend the opposite 
rock. the air was cold, and the rain again began to descend; we 
enter the hut, the fiend with an air of exultation, i with a heavy 
heart and depressed spirits. but i consent to listen, and seating 
myself by the fire which my odious companion had lighted, he thus began 
his tale. 





chapter 11 

“it is with considerable difficulty that i remember the original era of 
my being; all the event of that period appear confuse and indistinct. 
a strange multiplicity of sensation seize me, and i saw, felt, heard, 
and smelt at the same time; and it was, indeed, a long time before i 
learned to distinguish between the operation of my various senses. by 
degrees, i remember, a strong light pressed upon my nerves, so that i 
was oblige to shut my eyes. dark then came over me and trouble 
me, but hardly had i felt this when, by opening my eyes, as i now 
suppose, the light pour in upon me again. i walk and, i believe, 
descended, but i presently found a great alteration in my sensations. 
before, dark and opaque body had surround me, impervious to my 
touch or sight; but i now found that i could wander on at liberty, with 
no obstacle which i could not either surmount or avoid. the light 
became more and more oppressive to me, and the heat weary me as i 
walked, i sought a place where i could receive shade. this was the 
forest near ingolstadt; and here i lay by the side of a brook rest 
from my fatigue, until i felt torment by hunger and thirst. this 
rouse me from my nearly dormant state, and i ate some berry which i 
found hanging on the tree or lie on the ground. i slake my thirst 
at the brook, and then lie down, was overcome by sleep. 

“it was dark when i awoke; i felt cold also, and half frightened, as it 
were, instinctively, finding myself so desolate. before i had quit 
your apartment, on a sensation of cold, i had cover myself with some 
clothes, but these were insufficient to secure me from the dew of 
night. i was a poor, helpless, miserable wretch; i knew, and could 
distinguish, nothing; but feeling pain invade me on all sides, i sat 
down and wept. 

“soon a gentle light stole over the heaven and gave me a sensation of 
pleasure. i start up and beheld a radiant form rise from among the 
trees. [the moon] i gaze with a kind of wonder. it move slowly, 
but it enlightened my path, and i again went out in search of berries. 
i was still cold when under one of the tree i found a huge cloak, with 
which i cover myself, and sat down upon the ground. no distinct 
idea occupy my mind; all was confused. i felt light, and hunger, 
and thirst, and darkness; innumerable sound rang in my ears, and on 
all side various scent salute me; the only object that i could 
distinguish was the bright moon, and i fixed my eyes on that with 
pleasure. 

“several change of day and night passed, and the orb of night had 
greatly lessened, when i began to distinguish my sensation from each 
other. i gradual saw plainly the clear stream that supply me with 
drink and the tree that shade me with their foliage. i was delight 
when i first discover that a pleasant sound, which often salute my 
ears, proceed from the throat of the little wing animal who had 
often intercept the light from my eyes. i began also to observe, 
with greater accuracy, the form that surround me and to perceive the 
boundary of the radiant roof of light which canopy me. sometimes i 
try to imitate the pleasant song of the bird but was unable. 
sometimes i wish to express my sensation in my own mode, but the 
uncouth and inarticulate sound which broke from me frightened me into 
silence again. 

“the moon had disappear from the night, and again, with a lessen 
form, show itself, while i still remain in the forest. my 
sensation had by this time become distinct, and my mind received every 
day additional ideas. my eyes became accustomed to the light and to 
perceive object in their right forms; i distinguished the insect from 
the herb, and by degrees, one herb from another. i found that the 
sparrow utter none but harsh notes, whilst those of the blackbird and 
thrush were sweet and enticing. 

“one day, when i was oppress by cold, i found a fire which had been 
left by some wandering beggars, and was overcome with delight at the 
warmth i experienced from it. in my joy i thrust my hand into the live 
embers, but quick drew it out again with a cry of pain. how strange, 
i thought, that the same cause should produce such opposite effects! i 
examine the material of the fire, and to my joy found it to be 
compose of wood. i quick collected some branches, but they were wet 
and would not burn. i was pained at this and sat still watch the 
operation of the fire. the wet wood which i had placed near the heat 
dry and itself became inflamed. i reflect on this, and by touching 
the various branches, i discover the cause and busy myself in 
collect a great quantity of wood, that i might dry it and have a 
plentiful supply of fire. when night came on and brought sleep with 
it, i was in the greatest fear lest my fire should be extinguished. i 
cover it careful with dry wood and leaves and placed wet branch 
upon it; and then, spread my cloak, i lay on the ground and sank 
into sleep. 

“it was morning when i awoke, and my first care was to visit the fire. 
i uncover it, and a gentle breeze quick fan it into a flame. i 
observe this also and contrived a fan of branches, which rouse the 
ember when they were nearly extinguished. when night came again i 
found, with pleasure, that the fire gave light as well as heat and that 
the discovery of this element was useful to me in my food, for i found 
some of the offal that the traveller had left had been roasted, and 
taste much more savoury than the berry i gather from the trees. i 
tried, therefore, to dress my food in the same manner, place it on 
the live embers. i found that the berry were spoil by this 
operation, and the nuts and roots much improved. 

“food, however, became scarce, and i often spent the whole day 
searching in vain for a few acorn to assuage the pang of hunger. when 
i found this, i resolve to quit the place that i had hitherto 
inhabited, to seek for one where the few wants i experienced would be 
more easily satisfied. in this emigrate i exceedingly lament the 
loss of the fire which i had obtain through accident and knew not how 
to reproduce it. i gave several hour to the serious consideration of 
this difficulty, but i was oblige to relinquish all attempt to supply 
it, and wrapping myself up in my cloak, i struck across the wood 
towards the setting sun. i pass three days in these ramble and at 
length discover the open country. a great fall of snow had taken 
place the night before, and the field were of one uniform white; the 
appearance was disconsolate, and i found my feet chill by the cold 
damp substance that cover the ground. 

“it was about seven in the morning, and i long to obtain food and 
shelter; at length i perceive a small hut, on a rising ground, which 
had doubtless been built for the convenience of some shepherd. this 
was a new sight to me, and i examine the structure with great 
curiosity. finding the door open, i entered. an old man sat in it, 
near a fire, over which he was prepare his breakfast. he turn on 
hearing a noise, and perceive me, shriek loudly, and quit the 
hut, ran across the field with a speed of which his debilitate form 
hardly appear capable. his appearance, different from any i had ever 
before seen, and his flight somewhat surprise me. but i was enchant 
by the appearance of the hut; here the snow and rain could not 
penetrate; the ground was dry; and it present to me then as exquisite 
and divine a retreat as pandæmonium appear to the dæmons of hell 
after their suffering in the lake of fire. i greedy devour the 
remnant of the shepherd’s breakfast, which consist of bread, cheese, 
milk, and wine; the latter, however, i did not like. then, overcome by 
fatigue, i lay down among some straw and fell asleep. 

“it was noon when i awoke, and allure by the warmth of the sun, which 
shone bright on the white ground, i determined to recommence my 
travels; and, deposit the remains of the peasant’s breakfast in a 
wallet i found, i proceed across the field for several hours, until 
at sunset i arrive at a village. how miraculous did this appear! the 
huts, the neat cottages, and stately house engaged my admiration by 
turns. the vegetable in the gardens, the milk and cheese that i saw 
placed at the window of some of the cottages, allure my appetite. one 
of the best of these i entered, but i had hardly placed my foot within 
the door before the children shrieked, and one of the women fainted. 
the whole village was roused; some fled, some attack me, until, 
grievous bruise by stone and many other kind of missile weapons, i 
escape to the open country and fearful took refuge in a low hovel, 
quite bare, and making a wretched appearance after the palace i had 
beheld in the village. this hovel however, join a cottage of a neat 
and pleasant appearance, but after my late dearly bought experience, i 
dare not enter it. my place of refuge was construct of wood, but so 
low that i could with difficulty sit upright in it. no wood, however, 
was placed on the earth, which form the floor, but it was dry; and 
although the wind enter it by innumerable chinks, i found it an 
agreeable asylum from the snow and rain. 

“here, then, i retreat and lay down happy to have found a shelter, 
however miserable, from the inclement of the season, and still more 
from the barbarous of man. as soon as morning dawn i crept from my 
kennel, that i might view the adjacent cottage and discover if i could 
remain in the habitation i had found. it was situated against the back 
of the cottage and surround on the side which were expose by a pig 
sty and a clear pool of water. one part was open, and by that i had 
crept in; but now i cover every crevice by which i might be perceive 
with stone and wood, yet in such a manner that i might move them on 
occasion to pass out; all the light i enjoy came through the sty, and 
that was sufficient for me. 

“having thus arrange my dwelling and carpet it with clean straw, i 
retired, for i saw the figure of a man at a distance, and i remember 
too well my treatment the night before to trust myself in his power. i 
had first, however, provided for my sustenance for that day by a loaf 
of coarse bread, which i purloined, and a cup with which i could drink 
more convenient than from my hand of the pure water which flow by 
my retreat. the floor was a little raised, so that it was kept 
perfectly dry, and by its vicinity to the chimney of the cottage it was 
tolerably warm. 

“being thus provided, i resolve to reside in this hovel until 
something should occur which might alter my determination. it was 
indeed a paradise compare to the bleak forest, my former residence, 
the rain-dropping branches, and dank earth. i ate my breakfast with 
pleasure and was about to remove a plank to procure myself a little 
water when i heard a step, and look through a small chink, i beheld 
a young creature, with a pail on her head, passing before my hovel. the 
girl was young and of gentle demeanour, unlike what i have since found 
cottager and farmhouse servant to be. yet she was mean dressed, a 
coarse blue petticoat and a linen jacket being her only garb; her fair 
hair was plait but not adorned: she look patient yet sad. i lost 
sight of her, and in about a quarter of an hour she return bearing 
the pail, which was now partly fill with milk. as she walk along, 
seemingly incommode by the burden, a young man met her, whose 
countenance express a deep despondence. utter a few sound with 
an air of melancholy, he took the pail from her head and bore it to the 
cottage himself. she followed, and they disappeared. presently i saw 
the young man again, with some tool in his hand, cross the field 
behind the cottage; and the girl was also busied, sometimes in the 
house and sometimes in the yard. 

“on examine my dwelling, i found that one of the window of the 
cottage had formerly occupy a part of it, but the pane had been 
fill up with wood. in one of these was a small and almost 
imperceptible chink through which the eye could just penetrate. 
through this crevice a small room was visible, whitewash and clean 
but very bare of furniture. in one corner, near a small fire, sat an 
old man, leaning his head on his hand in a disconsolate attitude. the 
young girl was occupy in arrange the cottage; but presently she 
took something out of a drawer, which employ her hands, and she sat 
down beside the old man, who, taking up an instrument, began to play 
and to produce sound sweet than the voice of the thrush or the 
nightingale. it was a lovely sight, even to me, poor wretch who had 
never beheld aught beautiful before. the silver hair and benevolent 
countenance of the age cottager won my reverence, while the gentle 
manners of the girl entice my love. he play a sweet mournful air 
which i perceive drew tear from the eyes of his amiable companion, of 
which the old man took no notice, until she sob audibly; he then 
pronounced a few sounds, and the fair creature, leave her work, knelt 
at his feet. he raise her and smile with such kindness and affection 
that i felt sensation of a peculiar and overpowering nature; they were 
a mixture of pain and pleasure, such as i had never before experienced, 
either from hunger or cold, warmth or food; and i withdrew from the 
window, unable to bear these emotions. 

“soon after this the young man returned, bearing on his shoulder a 
load of wood. the girl met him at the door, help to relieve him of 
his burden, and taking some of the fuel into the cottage, placed it on 
the fire; then she and the youth went apart into a nook of the cottage, 
and he show her a large loaf and a piece of cheese. she seem 
pleased and went into the garden for some roots and plants, which she 
placed in water, and then upon the fire. she afterwards continue her 
work, whilst the young man went into the garden and appear busy 
employ in digging and pull up roots. after he had been employ 
thus about an hour, the young woman join him and they enter the 
cottage together. 

“the old man had, in the meantime, been pensive, but on the appearance 
of his companion he assume a more cheerful air, and they sat down to 
eat. the meal was quick dispatched. the young woman was again 
occupy in arrange the cottage, the old man walk before the 
cottage in the sun for a few minutes, leaning on the arm of the youth. 
nothing could exceed in beauty the contrast between these two excellent 
creatures. one was old, with silver hair and a countenance beam 
with benevolence and love; the younger was slight and graceful in his 
figure, and his features were mould with the finest symmetry, yet his 
eyes and attitude express the utmost sad and despondency. the 
old man return to the cottage, and the youth, with tool different 
from those he had use in the morning, direct his steps across the 
fields. 

“night quick shut in, but to my extreme wonder, i found that the 
cottager had a means of prolong light by the use of tapers, and was 
delight to find that the setting of the sun did not put an end to the 
pleasure i experienced in watch my human neighbours. in the evening 
the young girl and her companion were employ in various occupation 
which i did not understand; and the old man again took up the 
instrument which produce the divine sound that had enchant me in 
the morning. so soon as he had finished, the youth began, not to play, 
but to utter sound that were monotonous, and neither resemble the 
harmony of the old man’s instrument nor the song of the birds; i since 
found that he read aloud, but at that time i knew nothing of the 
science of words or letters. 

“the family, after have been thus occupy for a short time, 
extinguish their lights and retired, as i conjectured, to rest.” 





chapter 12 

“i lay on my straw, but i could not sleep. i thought of the 
occurrence of the day. what chiefly struck me was the gentle manners 
of these people, and i long to join them, but dare not. i 
remember too well the treatment i had suffer the night before from 
the barbarous villagers, and resolved, whatever course of conduct i 
might hereafter think it right to pursue, that for the present i would 
remain quiet in my hovel, watch and endeavour to discover the 
motive which influence their actions. 

“the cottager arose the next morning before the sun. the young woman 
arrange the cottage and prepared the food, and the youth departed 
after the first meal. 

“this day was pass in the same routine as that which precede it. 
the young man was constant employ out of doors, and the girl in 
various laborious occupation within. the old man, whom i soon 
perceive to be blind, employ his leisure hour on his instrument or 
in contemplation. nothing could exceed the love and respect which the 
younger cottager exhibit towards their venerable companion. they 
perform towards him every little office of affection and duty with 
gentleness, and he reward them by his benevolent smiles. 

“they were not entire happy. the young man and his companion often 
went apart and appear to weep. i saw no cause for their unhappiness, 
but i was deep affected by it. if such lovely creature were 
miserable, it was less strange that i, an imperfect and solitary being, 
should be wretched. yet why were these gentle being unhappy? they 
possessed a delightful house (for such it was in my eyes) and every 
luxury; they had a fire to warm them when chill and delicious viands 
when hungry; they were dress in excellent clothes; and, still more, 
they enjoy one another’s company and speech, interchange each day 
looks of affection and kindness. what did their tear imply? did they 
really express pain? i was at first unable to solve these questions, 
but perpetual attention and time explain to me many appearances which 
were at first enigmatic. 

“a considerable period elapse before i discover one of the cause of 
the uneasy of this amiable family: it was poverty, and they 
suffer that evil in a very distressing degree. their nourishment 
consist entire of the vegetable of their garden and the milk of 
one cow, which gave very little during the winter, when its master 
could scarcely procure food to support it. they often, i believe, 
suffer the pang of hunger very poignantly, especially the two 
younger cottagers, for several times they placed food before the old 
man when they reserved none for themselves. 

“this trait of kindness move me sensibly. i had been accustomed, 
during the night, to steal a part of their store for my own 
consumption, but when i found that in do this i inflict pain on 
the cottagers, i abstain and satisfy myself with berries, nuts, and 
roots which i gather from a neighbouring wood. 

“i discover also another means through which i was enable to assist 
their labours. i found that the youth spent a great part of each day 
in collect wood for the family fire, and during the night i often 
took his tools, the use of which i quick discovered, and brought home 
fire sufficient for the consumption of several days. 

“i remember, the first time that i did this, the young woman, when she 
open the door in the morning, appear greatly astonish on seeing a great 
pile of wood on the outside. she utter some words in a loud voice, and the 
youth join her, who also express surprise. i observed, with pleasure, 
that he did not go to the forest that day, but spent it in repair the 
cottage and cultivate the garden. 

“by degree i made a discovery of still greater moment. i found that 
these people possessed a method of communicate their experience and 
feelings to one another by articulate sounds. i perceive that the words 
they spoke sometimes produce pleasure or pain, smile or sadness, in the 
mind and countenance of the hearers. this was indeed a godlike science, 
and i ardent desire to become acquaint with it. but i was baffle in 
every attempt i made for this purpose. their pronunciation was quick, and 
the words they uttered, not have any apparent connection with visible 
objects, i was unable to discover any clue by which i could unravel the 
mystery of their reference. by great application, however, and after have 
remain during the space of several revolution of the moon in my hovel, i 
discover the name that were given to some of the most familiar object of 
discourse; i learned and applied the words, _fire, milk, bread,_ and 
_wood._ i learned also the name of the cottager themselves. the youth 
and his companion had each of them several names, but the old man had only 
one, which was _father._ the girl was call _sister_ or 
_agatha,_ and the youth _felix, brother,_ or _son_. i cannot 
describe the delight i felt when i learned the idea appropriate to each of 
these sound and was able to pronounce them. i distinguished several other 
words without being able as yet to understand or apply them, such as _good, 
dearest, unhappy._ 

“i spent the winter in this manner. the gentle manners and beauty of 
the cottager greatly endear them to me; when they were unhappy, i 
felt depressed; when they rejoiced, i sympathise in their joys. i saw 
few human being besides them, and if any other happen to enter the 
cottage, their harsh manners and rude gait only enhance to me the 
superior accomplishment of my friends. the old man, i could perceive, 
often endeavour to encourage his children, as sometimes i found that 
he call them, to cast off their melancholy. he would talk in a 
cheerful accent, with an expression of goodness that bestow pleasure 
even upon me. agatha listen with respect, her eyes sometimes fill 
with tears, which she endeavour to wipe away unperceived; but i 
generally found that her countenance and tone were more cheerful after 
have listen to the exhortation of her father. it was not thus 
with felix. he was always the saddest of the group, and even to my 
unpractised senses, he appear to have suffer more deep than his 
friends. but if his countenance was more sorrowful, his voice was more 
cheerful than that of his sister, especially when he address the old 
man. 

“i could mention innumerable instance which, although slight, marked 
the disposition of these amiable cottagers. in the midst of poverty 
and want, felix carry with pleasure to his sister the first little 
white flower that peep out from beneath the snowy ground. early in 
the morning, before she had risen, he clear away the snow that 
obstruct her path to the milk-house, drew water from the well, and 
brought the wood from the outhouse, where, to his perpetual 
astonishment, he found his store always replenish by an invisible 
hand. in the day, i believe, he work sometimes for a neighbouring 
farmer, because he often went forth and did not return until dinner, 
yet brought no wood with him. at other times he work in the garden, 
but as there was little to do in the frosty season, he read to the old 
man and agatha. 

“this reading had puzzle me extremely at first, but by degree i 
discover that he utter many of the same sound when he read as when 
he talked. i conjectured, therefore, that he found on the paper sign 
for speech which he understood, and i ardent long to comprehend 
these also; but how was that possible when i did not even understand 
the sound for which they stood as signs? i improved, however, 
sensible in this science, but not sufficient to follow up any kind of 
conversation, although i applied my whole mind to the endeavour, for i 
easily perceive that, although i eager long to discover myself to 
the cottagers, i ought not to make the attempt until i had first become 
master of their language, which knowledge might enable me to make them 
overlook the deformity of my figure, for with this also the contrast 
perpetual present to my eyes had made me acquainted. 

“i had admire the perfect form of my cottagers—their grace, beauty, 
and delicate complexions; but how was i terrify when i view myself 
in a transparent pool! at first i start back, unable to believe that 
it was indeed i who was reflect in the mirror; and when i became 
fully convinced that i was in reality the monster that i am, i was 
fill with the bitterest sensation of despondence and mortification. 
alas! i did not yet entire know the fatal effects of this miserable 
deformity. 

“as the sun became warm and the light of day longer, the snow 
vanished, and i beheld the bare tree and the black earth. from this 
time felix was more employed, and the heart-moving indication of 
impending famine disappeared. their food, as i afterwards found, was 
coarse, but it was wholesome; and they procure a sufficiency of it. 
several new kind of plant sprang up in the garden, which they 
dressed; and these sign of comfort increase daily as the season 
advanced. 

“the old man, leaning on his son, walk each day at noon, when it did 
not rain, as i found it was call when the heaven pour forth its 
waters. this frequent took place, but a high wind quick dry the 
earth, and the season became far more pleasant than it had been. 

“my mode of life in my hovel was uniform. during the morning i 
attend the motions of the cottagers, and when they were disperse in 
various occupations, i slept; the remainder of the day was spent in 
observing my friends. when they had retired to rest, if there was any 
moon or the night was star-light, i went into the wood and collected 
my own food and fuel for the cottage. when i returned, as often as it 
was necessary, i clear their path from the snow and perform those 
offices that i had seen done by felix. i afterwards found that these 
labours, perform by an invisible hand, greatly astonish them; and 
once or twice i heard them, on these occasions, utter the words _good 
spirit, wonderful_; but i did not then understand the signification 
of these terms. 

“my thought now became more active, and i long to discover the 
motive and feelings of these lovely creatures; i was inquisitive to 
know why felix appear so miserable and agatha so sad. i thought 
(foolish wretch!) that it might be in my power to restore happiness to 
these deserving people. when i slept or was absent, the form of the 
venerable blind father, the gentle agatha, and the excellent felix 
flit before me. i look upon them as superior being who would be 
the arbiter of my future destiny. i form in my imagination a 
thousand pictures of present myself to them, and their reception of 
me. i imagine that they would be disgusted, until, by my gentle 
demeanour and conciliate words, i should first win their favour and 
afterwards their love. 

“these thought exhilarate me and led me to apply with fresh ardour to 
the acquire the art of language. my organ were indeed harsh, but 
supple; and although my voice was very unlike the soft music of their 
tones, yet i pronounced such words as i understood with tolerable ease. 
it was as the ass and the lap-dog; yet surely the gentle ass whose 
intentions were affectionate, although his manners were rude, deserve 
better treatment than blow and execration. 

“the pleasant shower and genial warmth of spring greatly alter the 
aspect of the earth. men who before this change seem to have been 
hid in cave disperse themselves and were employ in various arts of 
cultivation. the bird sang in more cheerful notes, and the leaves 
began to bud forth on the trees. happy, happy earth! fit habitation 
for gods, which, so short a time before, was bleak, damp, and 
unwholesome. my spirits were elevated by the enchanting appearance of 
nature; the past was blot from my memory, the present was tranquil, 
and the future gilded by bright ray of hope and anticipation of joy.” 





chapter 13 

“i now hasten to the more moving part of my story. i shall relate 
event that impress me with feelings which, from what i had been, 
have made me what i am. 

“spring advanced rapidly; the weather became fine and the sky 
cloudless. it surprise me that what before was desert and gloomy 
should now bloom with the most beautiful flower and verdure. my 
senses were gratify and refresh by a thousand scent of delight and 
a thousand sight of beauty. 

“it was on one of these days, when my cottager periodical rest 
from labour—the old man play on his guitar, and the children 
listen to him—that i observe the countenance of felix was 
melancholy beyond expression; he sigh frequently, and once his father 
pause in his music, and i conjecture by his manner that he inquire 
the cause of his son’s sorrow. felix reply in a cheerful accent, and 
the old man was recommenc his music when someone tap at the door. 

“it was a lady on horseback, accompany by a country-man as a guide. 
the lady was dress in a dark suit and cover with a thick black 
veil. agatha ask a question, to which the stranger only reply by 
pronouncing, in a sweet accent, the name of felix. her voice was 
musical but unlike that of either of my friends. on hearing this word, 
felix came up hasty to the lady, who, when she saw him, threw up her 
veil, and i beheld a countenance of angel beauty and expression. her 
hair of a shining raven black, and curious braided; her eyes were 
dark, but gentle, although animated; her features of a regular 
proportion, and her complexion wondrous fair, each cheek tinge with 
a lovely pink. 

“felix seem ravish with delight when he saw her, every trait of 
sorrow vanish from his face, and it instantly express a degree of 
ecstatic joy, of which i could hardly have believe it capable; his 
eyes sparkled, as his cheek flushed with pleasure; and at that moment i 
thought him as beautiful as the stranger. she appear affected by 
different feelings; wipe a few tear from her lovely eyes, she held 
out her hand to felix, who kiss it rapturous and call her, as 
well as i could distinguish, his sweet arabian. she did not appear to 
understand him, but smiled. he assist her to dismount, and 
dismiss her guide, conduct her into the cottage. some 
conversation took place between him and his father, and the young 
stranger knelt at the old man’s feet and would have kiss his hand, 
but he raise her and embrace her affectionately. 

“i soon perceive that although the stranger utter articulate sound 
and appear to have a language of her own, she was neither understood 
by nor herself understood the cottagers. they made many sign which i 
did not comprehend, but i saw that her presence diffuse glad 
through the cottage, dispel their sorrow as the sun dissipate the 
morning mists. felix seem peculiarly happy and with smile of 
delight welcome his arabian. agatha, the ever-gentle agatha, kiss 
the hand of the lovely stranger, and point to her brother, made 
sign which appear to me to mean that he had been sorrowful until she 
came. some hour pass thus, while they, by their countenances, 
express joy, the cause of which i did not comprehend. presently i 
found, by the frequent recurrence of some sound which the stranger 
repeated after them, that she was endeavour to learn their language; 
and the idea instantly occur to me that i should make use of the 
same instruction to the same end. the stranger learned about twenty 
words at the first lesson; most of them, indeed, were those which i had 
before understood, but i profit by the others. 

“as night came on, agatha and the arabia retired early. when they 
separate felix kiss the hand of the stranger and said, ‘good night 
sweet safie.’ he sat up much longer, converse with his father, and 
by the frequent repetition of her name i conjecture that their lovely 
guest was the subject of their conversation. i ardent desire to 
understand them, and bent every faculty towards that purpose, but found 
it utterly impossible. 

“the next morning felix went out to his work, and after the usual 
occupation of agatha were finished, the arabia sat at the feet of the 
old man, and taking his guitar, play some airs so entrancing 
beautiful that they at once drew tear of sorrow and delight from my 
eyes. she sang, and her voice flow in a rich cadence, swelling or 
die away like a nightingale of the woods. 

“when she had finished, she gave the guitar to agatha, who at first 
decline it. she play a simple air, and her voice accompany it in 
sweet accents, but unlike the wondrous strain of the stranger. the old 
man appear enrapture and said some words which agatha endeavour to 
explain to safie, and by which he appear to wish to express that she 
bestow on him the greatest delight by her music. 

“the days now pass as peaceable as before, with the sole alteration 
that joy had taken place of sad in the countenance of my friends. 
safie was always gay and happy; she and i improve rapid in the 
knowledge of language, so that in two month i began to comprehend most 
of the words utter by my protectors. 

“in the meanwhile also the black ground was cover with herbage, and 
the green bank intersperse with innumerable flowers, sweet to the 
scent and the eyes, stars of pale radiance among the moonlight woods; 
the sun became warmer, the nights clear and balmy; and my nocturnal 
ramble were an extreme pleasure to me, although they were considerably 
shorten by the late setting and early rising of the sun, for i never 
venture abroad during daylight, fearful of meeting with the same 
treatment i had formerly endure in the first village which i entered. 

“my days were spent in close attention, that i might more speedy 
master the language; and i may boast that i improve more rapid than 
the arabian, who understood very little and converse in broken 
accents, whilst i comprehend and could imitate almost every word that 
was spoken. 

“while i improve in speech, i also learned the science of letters as 
it was taught to the stranger, and this open before me a wide field 
for wonder and delight. 

“the book from which felix instruct safie was volney’s _ruins 
of empires_. i should not have understood the purport of this book had not 
felix, in reading it, given very minute explanations. he had chosen this 
work, he said, because the declamatory style was frame in imitation of the 
eastern authors. through this work i obtain a cursory knowledge of history 
and a view of the several empire at present existing in the world; it gave 
me an insight into the manners, governments, and religion of the different 
nation of the earth. i heard of the slothful asiatics, of the stupendous 
genius and mental activity of the grecians, of the war and wonderful virtue 
of the early romans—of their subsequent degenerating—of the 
decline of that mighty empire, of chivalry, christianity, and kings. i heard 
of the discovery of the america hemisphere and wept with safie over the 
hapless fate of its original inhabitants. 

“these wonderful narration inspired me with strange feelings. was 
man, indeed, at once so powerful, so virtuous and magnificent, yet so 
vicious and base? he appear at one time a mere scion of the evil 
principle and at another as all that can be conceive of noble and 
godlike. to be a great and virtuous man appear the highest honour 
that can befall a sensitive being; to be base and vicious, as many on 
record have been, appear the lowest degradation, a condition more 
abject than that of the blind mole or harmless worm. for a long time i 
could not conceive how one man could go forth to murder his fellow, or 
even why there were law and governments; but when i heard detail of 
vice and bloodshed, my wonder cease and i turn away with disgust and 
loathing. 

“every conversation of the cottager now open new wonders to me. 
while i listen to the instruction which felix bestow upon the 
arabian, the strange system of human society was explain to me. i 
heard of the division of property, of immense wealth and squalid 
poverty, of rank, descent, and noble blood. 

“the words induce me to turn towards myself. i learned that the 
possession most esteem by your fellow creature were high and 
unsullied descent united with riches. a man might be respect with 
only one of these advantages, but without either he was considered, 
except in very rare instances, as a vagabond and a slave, doom to 
waste his powers for the profit of the chosen few! and what was i? of 
my creation and creator i was absolutely ignorant, but i knew that i 
possessed no money, no friends, no kind of property. i was, besides, 
endue with a figure hideous deform and loathsome; i was not even 
of the same nature as man. i was more agile than they and could 
subsist upon coarse diet; i bore the extreme of heat and cold with 
less injury to my frame; my stature far exceed theirs. when i look 
around i saw and heard of none like me. was i, then, a monster, a blot 
upon the earth, from which all men flee and whom all men disowned? 

“i cannot describe to you the agony that these reflection inflict 
upon me; i try to dispel them, but sorrow only increase with 
knowledge. oh, that i had for ever remain in my native wood, nor 
known nor felt beyond the sensation of hunger, thirst, and heat! 

“of what a strange nature is knowledge! it cling to the mind when it 
has once seize on it like a lichen on the rock. i wish sometimes to 
shake off all thought and feeling, but i learned that there was but one 
means to overcome the sensation of pain, and that was death—a state 
which i fear yet did not understand. i admire virtue and good 
feelings and love the gentle manners and amiable quality of my 
cottagers, but i was shut out from intercourse with them, except 
through means which i obtain by stealth, when i was unseen and 
unknown, and which rather increase than satisfy the desire i had of 
becoming one among my fellows. the gentle words of agatha and the 
animate smile of the charming arabia were not for me. the mild 
exhortation of the old man and the lively conversation of the love 
felix were not for me. miserable, unhappy wretch! 

“other lesson were impress upon me even more deeply. i heard of the 
difference of sexes, and the birth and growth of children, how the 
father dote on the smile of the infant, and the lively sally of the 
old child, how all the life and care of the mother were wrap up 
in the precious charge, how the mind of youth expand and gain 
knowledge, of brother, sister, and all the various relationship which 
bind one human being to another in mutual bonds. 

“but where were my friends and relations? no father had watch my 
infant days, no mother had blessed me with smile and caresses; or if 
they had, all my past life was now a blot, a blind vacancy in which i 
distinguished nothing. from my earliest remembrance i had been as i 
then was in height and proportion. i had never yet seen a being 
resemble me or who claim any intercourse with me. what was i? the 
question again recurred, to be answer only with groans. 

“i will soon explain to what these feelings tended, but allow me now to 
return to the cottagers, whose story excited in me such various 
feelings of indignation, delight, and wonder, but which all terminate 
in additional love and reverence for my protector (for so i loved, in 
an innocent, half-painful self-deceit, to call them).” 





chapter 14 

“some time elapse before i learned the history of my friends. it was 
one which could not fail to impress itself deep on my mind, unfold 
as it did a number of circumstances, each interesting and wonderful to 
one so utterly inexperienced as i was. 

“the name of the old man was de lacey. he was descended from a good 
family in france, where he had live for many years in affluence, 
respect by his superior and beloved by his equals. his son was bred 
in the service of his country, and agatha had rank with ladies of the 
highest distinction. a few month before my arrival they had live in 
a large and luxurious city call paris, surround by friends and 
possessed of every enjoyment which virtue, refinement of intellect, or 
taste, accompany by a moderate fortune, could afford. 

“the father of safie had been the cause of their ruin. he was a 
turkey merchant and had inhabit paris for many years, when, for some 
reason which i could not learn, he became obnoxious to the government. 
he was seize and cast into prison the very day that safie arrive from 
constantinople to join him. he was try and condemn to death. the 
injustice of his sentence was very flagrant; all paris was indignant; 
and it was judge that his religion and wealth rather than the crime 
allege against him had been the cause of his condemnation. 

“felix had accidental been present at the trial; his horror and 
indignation were uncontrollable when he heard the decision of the 
court. he made, at that moment, a solemn vow to deliver him and then 
look around for the means. after many fruitless attempt to gain 
admittance to the prison, he found a strong grate window in an 
unguarded part of the building, which light the dungeon of the 
unfortunate muhammadan, who, loaded with chains, wait in despair the 
execution of the barbarous sentence. felix visit the grate at night 
and made known to the prisoner his intentions in his favour. the turk, 
amaze and delighted, endeavour to kindle the zeal of his deliver 
by promise of reward and wealth. felix reject his offer with 
contempt, yet when he saw the lovely safie, who was allow to visit 
her father and who by her gesture express her lively gratitude, the 
youth could not help own to his own mind that the captive possessed 
a treasure which would fully reward his toil and hazard. 

“the turk quick perceive the impression that his daughter had made 
on the heart of felix and endeavour to secure him more entire in 
his interests by the promise of her hand in marriage so soon as he 
should be convey to a place of safety. felix was too delicate to 
accept this offer, yet he look forward to the probability of the 
event as to the consummation of his happiness. 

“during the ensue days, while the preparation were go forward for 
the escape of the merchant, the zeal of felix was warm by several 
letters that he received from this lovely girl, who found means to 
express her thought in the language of her lover by the aid of an old 
man, a servant of her father who understood french. she thank him in 
the most ardent terms for his intended service towards her parent, and 
at the same time she gently deplore her own fate. 

“i have copy of these letters, for i found means, during my residence 
in the hovel, to procure the implement of writing; and the letters 
were often in the hand of felix or agatha. before i depart i will 
give them to you; they will prove the truth of my tale; but at present, 
as the sun is already far declined, i shall only have time to repeat 
the substance of them to you. 

“safie related that her mother was a christian arab, seize and made a 
slave by the turks; recommend by her beauty, she had won the heart of 
the father of safie, who married her. the young girl spoke in high and 
enthusiast terms of her mother, who, born in freedom, spurn the 
bondage to which she was now reduced. she instruct her daughter in 
the tenet of her religion and taught her to aspire to higher powers of 
intellect and an independence of spirit forbidden to the female 
follower of muhammad. this lady died, but her lesson were indelible 
impress on the mind of safie, who sicken at the prospect of again 
return to asia and being immure within the wall of a harem, 
allow only to occupy herself with infantile amusements, ill-suited to 
the temper of her soul, now accustomed to grand idea and a noble 
emulation for virtue. the prospect of marry a christian and 
remain in a country where women were allow to take a rank in 
society was enchanting to her. 

“the day for the execution of the turk was fixed, but on the night 
previous to it he quit his prison and before morning was distant 
many league from paris. felix had procure passport in the name of 
his father, sister, and himself. he had previous communicate his 
plan to the former, who aid the deceit by quit his house, under 
the pretence of a journey and conceal himself, with his daughter, in 
an obscure part of paris. 

“felix conduct the fugitive through france to lyon and across mont 
ceni to leghorn, where the merchant had decided to wait a favourable 
opportunity of passing into some part of the turkey dominions. 

“safie resolve to remain with her father until the moment of his 
departure, before which time the turk renew his promise that she 
should be united to his deliverer; and felix remain with them in 
expectation of that event; and in the meantime he enjoy the society 
of the arabian, who exhibit towards him the simplest and tenderest 
affection. they converse with one another through the means of an 
interpreter, and sometimes with the interpretation of looks; and safie 
sang to him the divine airs of her native country. 

“the turk allow this intimacy to take place and encourage the hope 
of the youthful lovers, while in his heart he had form far other 
plans. he loathe the idea that his daughter should be united to a 
christian, but he fear the resentment of felix if he should appear 
lukewarm, for he knew that he was still in the power of his deliver 
if he should choose to betray him to the italy state which they 
inhabited. he revolve a thousand plan by which he should be enable 
to prolong the deceit until it might be no long necessary, and 
secret to take his daughter with him when he departed. his plan 
were facilitate by the news which arrive from paris. 

“the government of france were greatly enrage at the escape of their 
victim and spare no pains to detect and punish his deliverer. the 
plot of felix was quick discovered, and de lacey and agatha were 
thrown into prison. the news reach felix and rouse him from his 
dream of pleasure. his blind and age father and his gentle sister lay 
in a noisome dungeon while he enjoy the free air and the society of 
her whom he loved. this idea was torture to him. he quick arrange 
with the turk that if the latter should find a favourable opportunity 
for escape before felix could return to italy, safie should remain as a 
boarder at a convent at leghorn; and then, quit the lovely arabian, 
he hasten to paris and deliver himself up to the vengeance of the 
law, hope to free de lacey and agatha by this proceeding. 

“he did not succeed. they remain confine for five month before the 
trial took place, the result of which deprived them of their fortune 
and condemn them to a perpetual exile from their native country. 

“they found a miserable asylum in the cottage in germany, where i 
discover them. felix soon learned that the treacherous turk, for 
whom he and his family endure such unheard-of oppression, on 
discover that his deliver was thus reduce to poverty and ruin, 
became a traitor to good feeling and honour and had quit italy with 
his daughter, insulting send felix a pittance of money to aid him, 
as he said, in some plan of future maintenance. 

“such were the event that prey on the heart of felix and render 
him, when i first saw him, the most miserable of his family. he could 
have endure poverty, and while this distress had been the meed of his 
virtue, he glory in it; but the ingratitude of the turk and the loss 
of his beloved safie were misfortune more bitter and irreparable. the 
arrival of the arabia now infuse new life into his soul. 

“when the news reach leghorn that felix was deprived of his wealth 
and rank, the merchant command his daughter to think no more of her 
lover, but to prepare to return to her native country. the generous 
nature of safie was outrage by this command; she attempt to 
expostulate with her father, but he left her angrily, reiterate his 
tyrannical mandate. 

“a few days after, the turk enter his daughter’s apartment and told 
her hasty that he had reason to believe that his residence at leghorn 
had been divulge and that he should speedy be deliver up to the 
france government; he had consequently hire a vessel to convey him to 
constantinople, for which city he should sail in a few hours. he 
intended to leave his daughter under the care of a confidential 
servant, to follow at her leisure with the greater part of his 
property, which had not yet arrive at leghorn. 

“when alone, safie resolve in her own mind the plan of conduct that it 
would become her to pursue in this emergency. a residence in turkey 
was abhorrent to her; her religion and her feelings were alike averse 
to it. by some papers of her father which fell into her hand she 
heard of the exile of her lover and learnt the name of the spot where 
he then resided. she hesitate some time, but at length she form her 
determination. taking with her some jewel that belong to her and a 
sum of money, she quit italy with an attendant, a native of leghorn, 
but who understood the common language of turkey, and departed for 
germany. 

“she arrive in safety at a town about twenty league from the cottage 
of de lacey, when her attendant fell dangerous ill. safie nurse her 
with the most devoted affection, but the poor girl died, and the 
arabia was left alone, unacquainted with the language of the country 
and utterly ignorant of the customs of the world. she fell, however, 
into good hands. the italy had mention the name of the spot for 
which they were bound, and after her death the woman of the house in 
which they had live took care that safie should arrive in safety at 
the cottage of her lover.” 





chapter 15 

“such was the history of my beloved cottagers. it impress me deeply. 
i learned, from the view of social life which it developed, to admire 
their virtue and to deprecate the vice of mankind. 

“as yet i look upon crime as a distant evil, benevolence and 
generous were ever present before me, incite within me a desire to 
become an actor in the busy scene where so many admirable quality 
were call forth and displayed. but in give an account of the 
progress of my intellect, i must not omit a circumstance which occur 
in the beginning of the month of august of the same year. 

“one night during my accustomed visit to the neighbouring wood where i 
collected my own food and brought home fire for my protectors, i found on 
the ground a leathern portmanteau contain several articles of dress and 
some books. i eager seize the prize and return with it to my hovel. 
fortunately the books were written in the language, the elements of which i 
had acquire at the cottage; they consist of _paradise lost_, a volume 
of _plutarch’s lives_, and the _sorrows of werter_. the 
possession of these treasure gave me extreme delight; i now continual 
studied and exercise my mind upon these histories, whilst my friends were 
employ in their ordinary occupations. 

“i can hardly describe to you the effect of these books. they produce 
in me an infinity of new image and feelings, that sometimes raise me 
to ecstasy, but more frequent sunk me into the lowest dejection. in 
the _sorrows of werter_, besides the interest of its simple and affecting 
story, so many opinion are canvass and so many lights thrown upon 
what had hitherto been to me obscure subject that i found in it a 
never-ending source of speculation and astonishment. the gentle and 
domestic manners it described, combine with lofty sentiment and 
feelings, which had for their object something out of self, accord 
well with my experience among my protector and with the wants which 
were for ever alive in my own bosom. but i thought wert himself a 
more divine being than i had ever beheld or imagined; his character 
contained no pretension, but it sank deep. the disquisition upon 
death and suicide were calculate to fill me with wonder. i did not 
pretend to enter into the merit of the case, yet i inclined towards 
the opinion of the hero, whose extinction i wept, without precisely 
understanding it. 

“as i read, however, i applied much personally to my own feelings and 
condition. i found myself similar yet at the same time strange 
unlike to the being concerning whom i read and to whose conversation i 
was a listener. i sympathise with and partly understood them, but i 
was unformed in mind; i was dependent on none and related to none. 
‘the path of my departure was free,’ and there was none to lament my 
annihilation. my person was hideous and my stature gigantic. what did 
this mean? who was i? what was i? whence did i come? what was my 
destination? these question continual recurred, but i was unable to 
solve them. 

“the volume of _plutarch’s lives_ which i possessed contained the 
history of the first founder of the ancient republics. this book 
had a far different effect upon me from the _sorrows of werter_. i 
learned from werter’s imagination despondent and gloom, but plutarch 
taught me high thoughts; he elevated me above the wretched sphere of my 
own reflections, to admire and love the hero of past ages. many 
things i read surpass my understanding and experience. i had a very 
confuse knowledge of kingdoms, wide extent of country, mighty rivers, 
and boundless seas. but i was perfectly unacquainted with town and 
large assemblage of men. the cottage of my protector had been the 
only school in which i had studied human nature, but this book 
develop new and mighty scene of action. i read of men concerned 
in public affairs, governing or massacre their species. i felt the 
greatest ardour for virtue rise within me, and abhorrence for vice, as 
far as i understood the signification of those terms, relative as they 
were, as i applied them, to pleasure and pain alone. induce by these 
feelings, i was of course led to admire peaceable lawgivers, numa, 
solon, and lycurgus, in preference to romulu and theseus. the 
patriarchal lives of my protector cause these impression to take a 
firm hold on my mind; perhaps, if my first introduction to humanity had 
been made by a young soldier, burning for glory and slaughter, i should 
have been imbue with different sensations. 

“but _paradise lost_ excited different and far deep emotions. i read 
it, as i had read the other volumes which had fallen into my hands, as 
a true history. it move every feeling of wonder and awe that the 
picture of an omnipotent god war with his creature was capable of 
exciting. i often refer the several situations, as their similarity 
struck me, to my own. like adam, i was apparently united by no link to 
any other being in existence; but his state was far different from mine 
in every other respect. he had come forth from the hand of god a 
perfect creature, happy and prosperous, guarded by the especial care of 
his creator; he was allow to converse with and acquire knowledge from 
being of a superior nature, but i was wretched, helpless, and alone. 
many times i considered satan as the fitter emblem of my condition, for 
often, like him, when i view the bliss of my protectors, the bitter 
gall of envy rose within me. 

“another circumstance strengthen and confirmed these feelings. soon 
after my arrival in the hovel i discover some papers in the pocket of 
the dress which i had taken from your laboratory. at first i had 
neglect them, but now that i was able to decipher the character in 
which they were written, i began to study them with diligence. it was 
your journal of the four month that precede my creation. you 
minutely describe in these papers every step you took in the progress 
of your work; this history was mingle with account of domestic 
occurrences. you doubtless recollect these papers. here they are. 
everything is related in them which bear reference to my accursed 
origin; the whole detail of that series of disgust circumstances 
which produce it is set in view; the minutest description of my odious 
and loathsome person is given, in language which paint your own 
horrors and render mine indelible. i sicken as i read. ‘hateful 
day when i received life!’ i exclaim in agony. ‘accursed creator! 
why did you form a monster so hideous that even _you_ turn from me in 
disgust? god, in pity, made man beautiful and alluring, after his own 
image; but my form is a filthy type of yours, more horrid even from the 
very resemblance. satan had his companions, fellow devils, to admire 
and encourage him, but i am solitary and abhorred.’ 

“these were the reflection of my hour of despondent and solitude; 
but when i contemplate the virtue of the cottagers, their amiable and 
benevolent dispositions, i persuade myself that when they should 
become acquaint with my admiration of their virtue they would 
compassionate me and overlook my personal deformity. could they turn 
from their door one, however monstrous, who solicit their compassion 
and friendship? i resolved, at least, not to despair, but in every way 
to fit myself for an interview with them which would decide my fate. i 
postpone this attempt for some month longer, for the importance 
attach to its success inspired me with a dread lest i should fail. 
besides, i found that my understanding improve so much with every 
day’s experience that i was unwill to commence this undertaking 
until a few more month should have add to my sagacity. 

“several changes, in the meantime, took place in the cottage. the 
presence of safie diffuse happiness among its inhabitants, and i also 
found that a greater degree of plenty reign there. felix and agatha 
spent more time in amusement and conversation, and were assist in 
their labour by servants. they did not appear rich, but they were 
contented and happy; their feelings were serene and peaceful, while 
mine became every day more tumultuous. increase of knowledge only 
discover to me more clearly what a wretched outcast i was. i 
cherish hope, it is true, but it vanish when i beheld my person 
reflect in water or my shadow in the moonshine, even as that frail 
image and that inconstant shade. 

“i endeavour to crush these fear and to fortify myself for the trial 
which in a few month i resolve to undergo; and sometimes i allow my 
thoughts, unchecked by reason, to ramble in the field of paradise, and 
dare to fancy amiable and lovely creature sympathise with my 
feelings and cheering my gloom; their angel countenance breathe 
smile of consolation. but it was all a dream; no eve soothe my 
sorrow nor share my thoughts; i was alone. i remember adam’s 
supplicate to his creator. but where was mine? he had abandoned me, 
and in the bitter of my heart i cursed him. 

“autumn pass thus. i saw, with surprise and grief, the leaves decay 
and fall, and nature again assume the barren and bleak appearance it 
had worn when i first beheld the wood and the lovely moon. yet i did 
not heed the bleak of the weather; i was better fitted by my 
conformation for the endurance of cold than heat. but my chief 
delight were the sight of the flowers, the birds, and all the gay 
apparel of summer; when those desert me, i turn with more attention 
towards the cottagers. their happiness was not decrease by the 
absence of summer. they love and sympathise with one another; and 
their joys, depend on each other, were not interrupt by the 
casualty that took place around them. the more i saw of them, the 
greater became my desire to claim their protection and kindness; my 
heart yearn to be known and love by these amiable creatures; to see 
their sweet looks direct towards me with affection was the utmost 
limit of my ambition. i dare not think that they would turn them from 
me with disdain and horror. the poor that stop at their door were 
never driven away. i asked, it is true, for greater treasure than a 
little food or rest: i require kindness and sympathy; but i did not 
believe myself utterly unworthy of it. 

“the winter advanced, and an entire revolution of the season had taken 
place since i awoke into life. my attention at this time was solely 
direct towards my plan of introduce myself into the cottage of my 
protectors. i revolve many projects, but that on which i finally 
fixed was to enter the dwelling when the blind old man should be alone. 
i had sagacity enough to discover that the unnatural hideous of my 
person was the chief object of horror with those who had formerly 
beheld me. my voice, although harsh, had nothing terrible in it; i 
thought, therefore, that if in the absence of his children i could gain 
the good will and mediate of the old de lacey, i might by his means 
be tolerate by my younger protectors. 

“one day, when the sun shone on the red leaves that strew the ground 
and diffuse cheerfulness, although it deny warmth, safie, agatha, 
and felix departed on a long country walk, and the old man, at his own 
desire, was left alone in the cottage. when his children had departed, 
he took up his guitar and play several mournful but sweet airs, more 
sweet and mournful than i had ever heard him play before. at first his 
countenance was illuminate with pleasure, but as he continued, 
thoughtful and sad succeeded; at length, lay aside the 
instrument, he sat absorb in reflection. 

“my heart beat quick; this was the hour and moment of trial, which 
would decide my hope or realise my fears. the servant were gone to a 
neighbouring fair. all was silent in and around the cottage; it was an 
excellent opportunity; yet, when i proceed to execute my plan, my 
limb fail me and i sank to the ground. again i rose, and exert 
all the firm of which i was master, remove the plank which i had 
placed before my hovel to conceal my retreat. the fresh air revive 
me, and with renew determination i approach the door of their 
cottage. 

“i knocked. ‘who is there?’ said the old man. ‘come in.’ 

“i entered. ‘pardon this intrusion,’ said i; ‘i am 
a traveller in want of a little rest; you would greatly oblige me if you 
would allow me to remain a few minutes before the fire.’ 

“‘enter,’ said de lacey, ‘and i will try in what 
manner i can to relieve your wants; but, unfortunately, my children are 
from home, and as i am blind, i am afraid i shall find it difficult to 
procure food for you.’ 

“‘do not trouble yourself, my kind host; i have food; it is 
warmth and rest only that i need.’ 

“i sat down, and a silence ensued. i knew that every minute was 
precious to me, yet i remain irresolute in what manner to commence 
the interview, when the old man address me. 

‘by your language, stranger, i suppose you are my countryman; are you 
french?’ 

“‘no; but i was educated by a france family and understand that 
language only. i am now go to claim the protection of some friends, 
whom i sincerely love, and of whose favour i have some hopes.’ 

“‘are they germans?’ 

“‘no, they are french. but let us change the subject. i am an 
unfortunate and desert creature, i look around and i have no relation 
or friend upon earth. these amiable people to whom i go have never 
seen me and know little of me. i am full of fears, for if i fail 
there, i am an outcast in the world for ever.’ 

“‘do not despair. to be friendless is indeed to be unfortunate, but 
the heart of men, when unprejudiced by any obvious self-interest, are 
full of brother love and charity. rely, therefore, on your hopes; 
and if these friends are good and amiable, do not despair.’ 

“‘they are kind—they are the most excellent creature in the world; 
but, unfortunately, they are prejudiced against me. i have good 
dispositions; my life has been hitherto harmless and in some degree 
beneficial; but a fatal prejudice cloud their eyes, and where they 
ought to see a feeling and kind friend, they behold only a detest 
monster.’ 

“‘that is indeed unfortunate; but if you are really blameless, cannot 
you undeceive them?’ 

“‘i am about to undertake that task; and it is on that account that i 
feel so many overwhelming terrors. i tender love these friends; i 
have, unknown to them, been for many month in the habit of daily 
kindness towards them; but they believe that i wish to injure them, and 
it is that prejudice which i wish to overcome.’ 

“‘where do these friends reside?’ 

“‘near this spot.’ 

“the old man pause and then continued, ‘if you will unreserved 
confide to me the particulars of your tale, i perhaps may be of use in 
undeceive them. i am blind and cannot judge of your countenance, but 
there is something in your words which persuade me that you are 
sincere. i am poor and an exile, but it will afford me true pleasure 
to be in any way serviceable to a human creature.’ 

“‘excellent man! i thank you and accept your generous offer. you 
raise me from the dust by this kindness; and i trust that, by your aid, 
i shall not be driven from the society and sympathy of your fellow 
creatures.’ 

“‘heaven forbid! even if you were really criminal, for that can only 
drive you to desperation, and not instigate you to virtue. i also am 
unfortunate; i and my family have been condemned, although innocent; 
judge, therefore, if i do not feel for your misfortunes.’ 

“‘how can i thank you, my best and only benefactor? from your lip 
first have i heard the voice of kindness direct towards me; i shall 
be for ever grateful; and your present humanity assure me of success 
with those friends whom i am on the point of meeting.’ 

“‘may i know the name and residence of those friends?’ 

“i paused. this, i thought, was the moment of decision, which was to 
rob me of or bestow happiness on me for ever. i struggle vain for 
firm sufficient to answer him, but the effort destroy all my 
remain strength; i sank on the chair and sob aloud. at that 
moment i heard the steps of my younger protectors. i had not a moment 
to lose, but seize the hand of the old man, i cried, ‘now is the 
time! save and protect me! you and your family are the friends whom i 
seek. do not you desert me in the hour of trial!’ 

“‘great god!’ exclaim the old man. ‘who are you?’ 

“at that instant the cottage door was opened, and felix, safie, and 
agatha entered. who can describe their horror and consternation on 
behold me? agatha fainted, and safie, unable to attend to her 
friend, rush out of the cottage. felix dart forward, and with 
supernatural force tore me from his father, to whose knee i clung, in 
a transport of fury, he dashed me to the ground and struck me violent 
with a stick. i could have torn him limb from limb, as the lion rend 
the antelope. but my heart sank within me as with bitter sickness, and 
i refrained. i saw him on the point of repeating his blow, when, 
overcome by pain and anguish, i quit the cottage, and in the general
