tumult escape unperceived to my hovel.” 





chapter 16 

“cursed, cursed creator! why did i live? why, in that instant, did i 
not extinguish the spark of existence which you had so wanton 
bestowed? i know not; despair had not yet taken possession of me; my 
feelings were those of rage and revenge. i could with pleasure have 
destroy the cottage and its inhabitant and have glut myself with 
their shriek and misery. 

“when night came i quit my retreat and wander in the wood; and 
now, no long restrained by the fear of discovery, i gave vent to my 
anguish in fearful howlings. i was like a wild beast that had broken 
the toils, destroy the object that obstruct me and range 
through the wood with a stag-like swiftness. oh! what a miserable 
night i passed! the cold stars shone in mockery, and the bare tree 
wave their branch above me; now and then the sweet voice of a bird 
burst forth amidst the universal stillness. all, save i, were at rest 
or in enjoyment; i, like the arch-fiend, bore a hell within me, and 
finding myself unsympathised with, wish to tear up the trees, spread 
havoc and destruction around me, and then to have sat down and enjoy 
the ruin. 

“but this was a luxury of sensation that could not endure; i became 
fatigue with excess of bodily exertion and sank on the damp grass in 
the sick impotence of despair. there was none among the myriad of men 
that exist who would pity or assist me; and should i feel kindness 
towards my enemies? no; from that moment i declared everlasting war 
against the species, and more than all, against him who had form me 
and sent me forth to this insupportable misery. 

“the sun rose; i heard the voice of men and knew that it was 
impossible to return to my retreat during that day. accordingly i hid 
myself in some thick underwood, determine to devote the ensue hour 
to reflection on my situation. 

“the pleasant sunshine and the pure air of day restore me to some 
degree of tranquillity; and when i considered what had pass at the 
cottage, i could not help believe that i had been too hasty in my 
conclusions. i had certainly act imprudently. it was apparent that 
my conversation had interested the father in my behalf, and i was a 
fool in have expose my person to the horror of his children. i 
ought to have familiarise the old de lacey to me, and by degree to 
have discover myself to the rest of his family, when they should have 
been prepared for my approach. but i did not believe my error to be 
irretrievable, and after much consideration i resolve to return to the 
cottage, seek the old man, and by my representations win him to my 
party. 

“these thought calm me, and in the afternoon i sank into a profound 
sleep; but the fever of my blood did not allow me to be visit by 
peaceful dreams. the horrible scene of the preceding day was for ever 
acting before my eyes; the female were flying and the enrage felix 
tear me from his father’s feet. i awoke exhausted, and finding that 
it was already night, i crept forth from my hiding-place, and went in 
search of food. 

“when my hunger was appeased, i direct my steps towards the 
well-known path that conduct to the cottage. all there was at peace. 
i crept into my hovel and remain in silent expectation of the 
accustomed hour when the family arose. that hour passed, the sun 
mount high in the heavens, but the cottager did not appear. i 
tremble violently, apprehend some dreadful misfortune. the inside 
of the cottage was dark, and i heard no motion; i cannot describe the 
agony of this suspense. 

“presently two countrymen pass by, but pause near the cottage, they 
enter into conversation, use violent gesticulations; but i did not 
understand what they said, as they spoke the language of the country, 
which differ from that of my protectors. soon after, however, felix 
approach with another man; i was surprised, as i knew that he had not 
quit the cottage that morning, and wait anxious to discover from 
his discourse the meaning of these unusual appearances. 

“‘do you consider,’ said his companion to him, 
‘that you will be oblige to pay three months’ rent and to lose 
the produce of your garden? i do not wish to take any unfair advantage, and 
i beg therefore that you will take some days to consider of your 
determination.’ 

“‘it is utterly useless,’ reply felix; ‘we can 
never again inhabit your cottage. the life of my father is in the greatest 
danger, owe to the dreadful circumstance that i have related. my wife and 
my sister will never recover from their horror. i entreat you not to reason 
with me any more. take possession of your tenement and let me fly from this 
place.’ 

“felix tremble violent as he said this. he and his companion 
enter the cottage, in which they remain for a few minutes, and then 
departed. i never saw any of the family of de lacey more. 

“i continue for the remainder of the day in my hovel in a state of 
utter and stupid despair. my protector had departed and had broken 
the only link that held me to the world. for the first time the 
feelings of revenge and hatred fill my bosom, and i did not strive to 
control them, but allow myself to be borne away by the stream, i 
bent my mind towards injury and death. when i thought of my friends, 
of the mild voice of de lacey, the gentle eyes of agatha, and the 
exquisite beauty of the arabian, these thought vanish and a gush of 
tear somewhat soothe me. but again when i reflect that they had 
spurn and desert me, anger returned, a rage of anger, and unable to 
injure anything human, i turn my fury towards inanimate objects. as 
night advanced, i placed a variety of combustible around the cottage, 
and after have destroy every vestige of cultivation in the garden, 
i wait with forced impatience until the moon had sunk to commence my 
operations. 

“as the night advanced, a fierce wind arose from the wood and quick 
disperse the cloud that had loiter in the heavens; the blast tore 
along like a mighty avalanche and produce a kind of insanity in my 
spirits that burst all bounds of reason and reflection. i light the 
dry branch of a tree and dance with fury around the devoted cottage, 
my eyes still fixed on the western horizon, the edge of which the moon 
nearly touched. a part of its orb was at length hid, and i wave my 
brand; it sank, and with a loud scream i fire the straw, and heath, 
and bushes, which i had collected. the wind fan the fire, and the 
cottage was quick envelop by the flames, which clung to it and 
lick it with their forked and destroy tongues. 

“as soon as i was convinced that no assistance could save any part of 
the habitation, i quit the scene and sought for refuge in the woods. 

“and now, with the world before me, whither should i bend my steps? i 
resolve to fly far from the scene of my misfortunes; but to me, hate 
and despised, every country must be equally horrible. at length the 
thought of you cross my mind. i learned from your papers that you 
were my father, my creator; and to whom could i apply with more fitness 
than to him who had given me life? among the lesson that felix had 
bestow upon safie, geography had not been omitted; i had learned from 
these the relative situation of the different country of the earth. 
you had mention geneva as the name of your native town, and towards 
this place i resolve to proceed. 

“but how was i to direct myself? i knew that i must travel in a 
southwesterly direction to reach my destination, but the sun was my 
only guide. i did not know the name of the town that i was to pass 
through, nor could i ask information from a single human being; but i 
did not despair. from you only could i hope for succour, although 
towards you i felt no sentiment but that of hatred. unfeeling, 
heartless creator! you had endow me with perception and passion 
and then cast me abroad an object for the scorn and horror of mankind. 
but on you only had i any claim for pity and redress, and from you i 
determined to seek that justice which i vain attempt to gain from 
any other being that wore the human form. 

“my travels were long and the suffering i endure intense. it was 
late in autumn when i quit the district where i had so long resided. 
i travelled only at night, fearful of encounter the visage of a 
human being. nature decay around me, and the sun became heatless; 
rain and snow pour around me; mighty river were frozen; the surface 
of the earth was hard and chill, and bare, and i found no shelter. oh, 
earth! how often did i imprecate curse on the cause of my being! the 
mild of my nature had fled, and all within me was turn to gall 
and bitterness. the near i approach to your habitation, the more 
deep did i feel the spirit of revenge enkindle in my heart. snow 
fell, and the waters were hardened, but i rest not. a few incident 
now and then direct me, and i possessed a map of the country; but i 
often wander wide from my path. the agony of my feelings allow me 
no respite; no incident occur from which my rage and misery could 
not extract its food; but a circumstance that happen when i arrive 
on the confines of switzerland, when the sun had recover its warmth 
and the earth again began to look green, confirmed in an especial 
manner the bitter and horror of my feelings. 

“i generally rest during the day and travelled only when i was 
secure by night from the view of man. one morning, however, finding 
that my path lay through a deep wood, i venture to continue my journey 
after the sun had risen; the day, which was one of the first of spring, 
cheer even me by the lovely of its sunshine and the balmy of 
the air. i felt emotion of gentle and pleasure, that had long 
appear dead, revive within me. half surprise by the novelty of 
these sensations, i allow myself to be borne away by them, and 
forget my solitude and deformity, dare to be happy. soft tear 
again bedewed my cheeks, and i even raise my humid eyes with 
thankful towards the blessed sun, which bestow such joy upon me. 

“i continue to wind among the path of the wood, until i came to its 
boundary, which was skirt by a deep and rapid river, into which many 
of the tree bent their branches, now budding with the fresh spring. 
here i paused, not exactly knowing what path to pursue, when i heard 
the sound of voices, that induce me to conceal myself under the shade 
of a cypress. i was scarcely hid when a young girl came running 
towards the spot where i was concealed, laughing, as if she ran from 
someone in sport. she continue her course along the precipitous side 
of the river, when sudden her foot slipped, and she fell into the 
rapid stream. i rush from my hiding-place and with extreme labour, 
from the force of the current, save her and drag her to shore. she 
was senseless, and i endeavour by every means in my power to restore 
animation, when i was sudden interrupt by the approach of a rustic, 
who was probably the person from whom she had playful fled. on 
seeing me, he dart towards me, and tear the girl from my arms, 
hasten towards the deep parts of the wood. i follow speedily, i 
hardly knew why; but when the man saw me draw near, he aim a gun, 
which he carried, at my body and fired. i sank to the ground, and my 
injurer, with increase swiftness, escape into the wood. 

“this was then the reward of my benevolence! i had save a human being 
from destruction, and as a recompense i now writhe under the miserable 
pain of a wound which shatter the flesh and bone. the feelings of 
kindness and gentle which i had entertain but a few moments 
before gave place to hellish rage and gnash of teeth. inflamed by 
pain, i vow eternal hatred and vengeance to all mankind. but the 
agony of my wound overcame me; my pulse paused, and i fainted. 

“for some week i led a miserable life in the woods, endeavour to 
cure the wound which i had received. the ball had enter my shoulder, 
and i knew not whether it had remain there or pass through; at any 
rate i had no means of extract it. my suffering were augment 
also by the oppressive sense of the injustice and ingratitude of their 
infliction. my daily vow rose for revenge—a deep and deadly revenge, 
such as would alone compensate for the outrage and anguish i had 
endured. 

“after some week my wound healed, and i continue my journey. the 
labour i endure were no long to be alleviate by the bright sun or 
gentle breeze of spring; all joy was but a mockery which insult my 
desolate state and made me feel more painful that i was not made for 
the enjoyment of pleasure. 

“but my toils now drew near a close, and in two month from this time i 
reach the environs of geneva. 

“it was evening when i arrived, and i retired to a hiding-place among 
the field that surround it to meditate in what manner i should apply 
to you. i was oppress by fatigue and hunger and far too unhappy to 
enjoy the gentle breeze of evening or the prospect of the sun setting 
behind the stupendous mountain of jura. 

“at this time a slight sleep relieved me from the pain of reflection, 
which was disturbed by the approach of a beautiful child, who came 
running into the recess i had chosen, with all the sportive of 
infancy. suddenly, as i gaze on him, an idea seize me that this 
little creature was unprejudiced and had live too short a time to have 
imbibe a horror of deformity. if, therefore, i could seize him and 
educate him as my companion and friend, i should not be so desolate in 
this people earth. 

“urged by this impulse, i seize on the boy as he pass and drew him 
towards me. as soon as he beheld my form, he placed his hand before 
his eyes and utter a shrill scream; i drew his hand forcibly from his 
face and said, ‘child, what is the meaning of this? i do not intend to 
hurt you; listen to me.’ 

“he struggle violently. ‘let me go,’ he cried; 
‘monster! ugly wretch! you wish to eat me and tear me to pieces. you 
are an ogre. let me go, or i will tell my papa.’ 

“‘boy, you will never see your father again; you must come with me.’ 

“‘hideous monster! let me go. my papa is a syndic—he is m. 
frankenstein—he will punish you. you dare not keep me.’ 

“‘frankenstein! you belong then to my enemy—to him towards whom i have 
sworn eternal revenge; you shall be my first victim.’ 

“the child still struggle and loaded me with epithet which carry 
despair to my heart; i grasp his throat to silence him, and in a 
moment he lay dead at my feet. 

“i gaze on my victim, and my heart swell with exultation and hellish 
triumph; clap my hands, i exclaimed, ‘i too can create desolation; 
my enemy is not invulnerable; this death will carry despair to him, and 
a thousand other misery shall torment and destroy him.’ 

“as i fixed my eyes on the child, i saw something glittering on his 
breast. i took it; it was a portrait of a most lovely woman. in spite 
of my malignity, it soften and attract me. for a few moments i 
gaze with delight on her dark eyes, fringe by deep lashes, and her 
lovely lips; but presently my rage returned; i remember that i was 
for ever deprived of the delight that such beautiful creature could 
bestow and that she whose resemblance i contemplate would, in 
regarding me, have change that air of divine benignity to one 
expressive of disgust and affright. 

“can you wonder that such thought transport me with rage? i only 
wonder that at that moment, instead of vent my sensation in 
exclamation and agony, i did not rush among mankind and perish in the 
attempt to destroy them. 

“while i was overcome by these feelings, i left the spot where i had 
committed the murder, and seek a more secluded hiding-place, i 
enter a barn which had appear to me to be empty. a woman was 
sleep on some straw; she was young, not indeed so beautiful as her 
whose portrait i held, but of an agreeable aspect and blooming in the 
lovely of youth and health. here, i thought, is one of those whose 
joy-imparting smile are bestow on all but me. and then i bent over 
her and whispered, ‘awake, fairest, thy lover is near—he who would 
give his life but to obtain one look of affection from thine eyes; my 
beloved, awake!’ 

“the sleeper stirred; a thrill of terror ran through me. should she 
indeed awake, and see me, and curse me, and denounce the murderer? thus 
would she assured act if her darken eyes open and she beheld me. 
the thought was madness; it stir the fiend within me—not i, but 
she, shall suffer; the murder i have committed because i am for ever 
rob of all that she could give me, she shall atone. the crime had 
its source in her; be hers the punishment! thanks to the lesson of 
felix and the sanguinary law of man, i had learned now to work 
mischief. i bent over her and placed the portrait secure in one of 
the fold of her dress. she move again, and i fled. 

“for some days i haunt the spot where these scene had taken place, 
sometimes wish to see you, sometimes resolve to quit the world and 
its misery for ever. at length i wander towards these mountains, 
and have range through their immense recesses, consume by a burning 
passion which you alone can gratify. we may not part until you have 
promise to comply with my requisition. i am alone and miserable; man 
will not associate with me; but one as deform and horrible as myself 
would not deny herself to me. my companion must be of the same species 
and have the same defects. this being you must create.” 





chapter 17 

the being finished speak and fixed his looks upon me in the 
expectation of a reply. but i was bewildered, perplexed, and unable to 
arrange my idea sufficient to understand the full extent of his 
proposition. he continued, 

“you must create a female for me with whom i can live in the 
interchange of those sympathies necessary for my being. this you alone 
can do, and i demand it of you as a right which you must not refuse to 
concede.” 

the latter part of his tale had kindle anew in me the anger that had 
die away while he narrate his peaceful life among the cottagers, and 
as he said this i could no long suppress the rage that burn within 
me. 

“i do refuse it,” i replied; “and no torture shall ever extort a 
consent from me. you may render me the most miserable of men, but you 
shall never make me base in my own eyes. shall i create another like 
yourself, whose joint wicked might desolate the world. begone! i 
have answer you; you may torture me, but i will never consent.” 

“you are in the wrong,” reply the fiend; “and instead 
of threatening, i am content to reason with you. i am malicious because i 
am miserable. am i not shun and hate by all mankind? you, my creator, 
would tear me to pieces and triumph; remember that, and tell me why i 
should pity man more than he pity me? you would not call it murder if you 
could precipitate me into one of those ice-rifts and destroy my frame, the 
work of your own hands. shall i respect man when he condemn me? let him 
live with me in the interchange of kindness, and instead of injury i would 
bestow every benefit upon him with tear of gratitude at his acceptance. 
but that cannot be; the human senses are insurmountable barrier to our 
union. yet mine shall not be the submission of abject slavery. i will 
revenge my injuries; if i cannot inspire love, i will cause fear, and 
chiefly towards you my arch-enemy, because my creator, do i swear 
inextinguishable hatred. have a care; i will work at your destruction, nor 
finish until i desolate your heart, so that you shall curse the hour of 
your birth.” 

a fiendish rage animate him as he said this; his face was wrinkle 
into contortion too horrible for human eyes to behold; but presently 
he calm himself and proceeded— 

“i intended to reason. this passion is detriment to me, for you do 
not reflect that _you_ are the cause of its excess. if any being felt 
emotion of benevolence towards me, i should return them a hundred and a 
hundredfold; for that one creature’s sake i would make peace with the 
whole kind! but i now indulge in dream of bliss that cannot be realised. 
what i ask of you is reasonable and moderate; i demand a creature of 
another sex, but as hideous as myself; the gratification is small, but it 
is all that i can receive, and it shall content me. it is true, we shall be 
monsters, cut off from all the world; but on that account we shall be more 
attach to one another. our lives will not be happy, but they will be 
harmless and free from the misery i now feel. oh! my creator, make me 
happy; let me feel gratitude towards you for one benefit! let me see that i 
excite the sympathy of some existing thing; do not deny me my 
request!” 

i was moved. i shudder when i thought of the possible consequence 
of my consent, but i felt that there was some justice in his argument. 
his tale and the feelings he now express prove him to be a creature 
of fine sensations, and did i not as his maker owe him all the portion 
of happiness that it was in my power to bestow? he saw my change of 
feeling and continued, 

“if you consent, neither you nor any other human being shall ever see 
us again; i will go to the vast wild of south america. my food is not 
that of man; i do not destroy the lamb and the kid to glut my appetite; 
acorn and berry afford me sufficient nourishment. my companion will 
be of the same nature as myself and will be content with the same fare. 
we shall make our bed of dry leaves; the sun will shine on us as on 
man and will ripen our food. the picture i present to you is peaceful 
and human, and you must feel that you could deny it only in the 
wanton of power and cruelty. pitiless as you have been towards me, 
i now see compassion in your eyes; let me seize the favourable moment 
and persuade you to promise what i so ardent desire.” 

“you propose,” reply i, “to fly from the habitation of 
man, to dwell in those wild where the beast of the field will be your 
only companions. how can you, who long for the love and sympathy of man, 
persevere in this exile? you will return and again seek their kindness, and 
you will meet with their detestation; your evil passion will be renewed, 
and you will then have a companion to aid you in the task of destruction. 
this may not be; cease to argue the point, for i cannot consent.” 

“how inconstant are your feelings! but a moment ago you were move by 
my representations, and why do you again harden yourself to my complaints? 
i swear to you, by the earth which i inhabit, and by you that made me, that 
with the companion you bestow, i will quit the neighbourhood of man and 
dwell, as it may chance, in the most savage of places. my evil passion 
will have fled, for i shall meet with sympathy! my life will flow quiet 
away, and in my die moments i shall not curse my maker.” 

his words had a strange effect upon me. i compassionate him and 
sometimes felt a wish to console him, but when i look upon him, when 
i saw the filthy mass that move and talked, my heart sicken and my 
feelings were alter to those of horror and hatred. i try to stifle 
these sensations; i thought that as i could not sympathise with him, i 
had no right to withhold from him the small portion of happiness which 
was yet in my power to bestow. 

“you swear,” i said, “to be harmless; but have you not 
already shown a degree of malice that should reasonably make me distrust 
you? may not even this be a feint that will increase your triumph by 
afford a wide scope for your revenge?” 

“how is this? i must not be trifle with, and i demand an answer. if 
i have no tie and no affections, hatred and vice must be my portion; 
the love of another will destroy the cause of my crimes, and i shall 
become a thing of whose existence everyone will be ignorant. my vice 
are the children of a forced solitude that i abhor, and my virtue will 
necessarily arise when i live in communion with an equal. i shall feel 
the affection of a sensitive being and become link to the chain of 
existence and event from which i am now excluded.” 

i pause some time to reflect on all he had related and the various 
argument which he had employed. i thought of the promise of virtue which 
he had display on the opening of his existence and the subsequent blight 
of all kindly feeling by the loathing and scorn which his protector had 
manifest towards him. his power and threat were not omit in my 
calculations; a creature who could exist in the ice-caves of the glacier 
and hide himself from pursuit among the ridge of inaccessible precipice 
was a being possess faculty it would be vain to cope with. after a 
long pause of reflection i conclude that the justice due both to him and 
my fellow creature demand of me that i should comply with his request. 
turning to him, therefore, i said, 

“i consent to your demand, on your solemn oath to quit europe for ever, 
and every other place in the neighbourhood of man, as soon as i shall 
deliver into your hand a female who will accompany you in your exile.” 

“i swear,” he cried, “by the sun, and by the blue sky of 
heaven, and by the fire of love that burn my heart, that if you grant my 
prayer, while they exist you shall never behold me again. depart to your 
home and commence your labours; i shall watch their progress with 
unutterable anxiety; and fear not but that when you are ready i shall 
appear.” 

saying this, he sudden quit me, fearful, perhaps, of any change in 
my sentiments. i saw him descend the mountain with greater speed than 
the flight of an eagle, and quick lost among the undulation of the 
sea of ice. 

his tale had occupy the whole day, and the sun was upon the verge of 
the horizon when he departed. i knew that i ought to hasten my descent 
towards the valley, as i should soon be encompass in darkness; but my 
heart was heavy, and my steps slow. the labour of winding among the 
little path of the mountain and fix my feet firm as i advanced 
perplexed me, occupy as i was by the emotion which the occurrence 
of the day had produced. night was far advanced when i came to the 
halfway resting-place and seat myself beside the fountain. the stars 
shone at interval as the cloud pass from over them; the dark pine 
rose before me, and every here and there a broken tree lay on the 
ground; it was a scene of wonderful solemnity and stir strange 
thought within me. i wept bitterly, and clasp my hand in agony, i 
exclaimed, “oh! stars and cloud and winds, ye are all about to mock 
me; if ye really pity me, crush sensation and memory; let me become as 
nought; but if not, depart, depart, and leave me in darkness.” 

these were wild and miserable thoughts, but i cannot describe to you 
how the eternal twinkling of the stars weigh upon me and how i 
listen to every blast of wind as if it were a dull ugly siroc on its 
way to consume me. 

morning dawn before i arrive at the village of chamounix; i took no 
rest, but return immediately to geneva. even in my own heart i could 
give no expression to my sensations—they weigh on me with a 
mountain’s weight and their excess destroy my agony beneath them. 
thus i return home, and enter the house, present myself to the 
family. my haggard and wild appearance awoke intense alarm, but i 
answer no question, scarcely did i speak. i felt as if i were placed 
under a ban—as if i had no right to claim their sympathies—as if 
never more might i enjoy companionship with them. yet even thus i 
love them to adoration; and to save them, i resolve to dedicate 
myself to my most abhor task. the prospect of such an occupation 
made every other circumstance of existence pass before me like a dream, 
and that thought only had to me the reality of life. 





chapter 18 

day after day, week after week, pass away on my return to geneva; and 
i could not collect the courage to recommence my work. i fear the 
vengeance of the disappointed fiend, yet i was unable to overcome my 
repugnance to the task which was enjoin me. i found that i could not 
compose a female without again devote several month to profound 
study and laborious disquisition. i had heard of some discovery 
have been made by an english philosopher, the knowledge of which was 
material to my success, and i sometimes thought of obtain my 
father’s consent to visit england for this purpose; but i clung to 
every pretence of delay and shrank from taking the first step in an 
undertaking whose immediate necessity began to appear less absolute to 
me. a change indeed had taken place in me; my health, which had 
hitherto declined, was now much restored; and my spirits, when 
unchecked by the memory of my unhappy promise, rose proportionably. my 
father saw this change with pleasure, and he turn his thought 
towards the best method of eradicate the remains of my melancholy, 
which every now and then would return by fits, and with a devour 
black overcast the approach sunshine. at these moments i took 
refuge in the most perfect solitude. i pass whole days on the lake 
alone in a little boat, watch the cloud and listen to the 
ripple of the waves, silent and listless. but the fresh air and 
bright sun seldom fail to restore me to some degree of composure, and 
on my return i met the salutation of my friends with a ready smile 
and a more cheerful heart. 

it was after my return from one of these ramble that my father, 
calling me aside, thus address me, 

“i am happy to remark, my dear son, that you have resume your former 
pleasure and seem to be return to yourself. and yet you are still 
unhappy and still avoid our society. for some time i was lost in 
conjecture as to the cause of this, but yesterday an idea struck me, 
and if it is well founded, i conjure you to avow it. reserve on such a 
point would be not only useless, but draw down treble misery on us all.” 

i tremble violent at his exordium, and my father continued— 

“i confess, my son, that i have always look forward to your 
marriage with our dear elizabeth as the tie of our domestic comfort and the 
stay of my decline years. you were attach to each other from your 
earliest infancy; you studied together, and appeared, in disposition and 
tastes, entire suit to one another. but so blind is the experience of 
man that what i conceive to be the best assistant to my plan may have 
entire destroy it. you, perhaps, regard her as your sister, without any 
wish that she might become your wife. nay, you may have met with another 
whom you may love; and considering yourself as bound in honour to 
elizabeth, this struggle may occasion the poignant misery which you appear 
to feel.” 

“my dear father, reassure yourself. i love my cousin tender and 
sincerely. i never saw any woman who excited, as elizabeth does, my 
warmest admiration and affection. my future hope and prospects are 
entire bound up in the expectation of our union.” 

“the expression of your sentiment of this subject, my dear victor, 
give me more pleasure than i have for some time experienced. if you 
feel thus, we shall assured be happy, however present event may cast 
a gloom over us. but it is this gloom which appear to have taken so 
strong a hold of your mind that i wish to dissipate. tell me, 
therefore, whether you object to an immediate solemnise of the 
marriage. we have been unfortunate, and recent event have drawn us 
from that everyday tranquill befitting my years and infirmities. you 
are younger; yet i do not suppose, possessed as you are of a competent 
fortune, that an early marriage would at all interfere with any future 
plan of honour and utility that you may have formed. do not suppose, 
however, that i wish to dictate happiness to you or that a delay on 
your part would cause me any serious uneasiness. interpret my words 
with candour and answer me, i conjure you, with confidence and 
sincerity.” 

i listen to my father in silence and remain for some time incapable 
of offering any reply. i revolve rapid in my mind a multitude of 
thought and endeavour to arrive at some conclusion. alas! to me 
the idea of an immediate union with my elizabeth was one of horror and 
dismay. i was bound by a solemn promise which i had not yet fulfill 
and dare not break, or if i did, what manifold misery might not 
impend over me and my devoted family! could i enter into a festival 
with this deadly weight yet hanging round my neck and bowing me to the 
ground? i must perform my engagement and let the monster depart with 
his mate before i allow myself to enjoy the delight of a union from 
which i expect peace. 

i remember also the necessity impose upon me of either journey to 
england or enter into a long correspondence with those philosopher 
of that country whose knowledge and discovery were of indispensable 
use to me in my present undertaking. the latter method of obtain 
the desire intelligence was dilatory and unsatisfactory; besides, i 
had an insurmountable aversion to the idea of engaging myself in my 
loathsome task in my father’s house while in habit of familiar 
intercourse with those i loved. i knew that a thousand fearful 
accident might occur, the slightest of which would disclose a tale to 
thrill all connected with me with horror. i was aware also that i 
should often lose all self-command, all capacity of hiding the 
harrowing sensation that would possess me during the progress of my 
unearthly occupation. i must absent myself from all i love while thus 
employed. once commenced, it would quick be achieved, and i might be 
restore to my family in peace and happiness. my promise fulfilled, 
the monster would depart for ever. or (so my fond fancy imaged) some 
accident might meanwhile occur to destroy him and put an end to my 
slavery for ever. 

these feelings dictate my answer to my father. i express a wish to 
visit england, but conceal the true reason of this request, i 
clothe my desire under a guise which excited no suspicion, while i 
urge my desire with an earnest that easily induce my father to 
comply. after so long a period of an absorbing melancholy that 
resemble madness in its intensity and effects, he was glad to find 
that i was capable of taking pleasure in the idea of such a journey, 
and he hope that change of scene and varied amusement would, before my 
return, have restore me entire to myself. 

the duration of my absence was left to my own choice; a few months, or 
at most a year, was the period contemplated. one paternal kind 
precaution he had taken to ensure my have a companion. without 
previous communicate with me, he had, in concert with elizabeth, 
arrange that clerval should join me at strasburgh. this interfere 
with the solitude i covet for the prosecution of my task; yet at the 
commencement of my journey the presence of my friend could in no way be 
an impediment, and truly i rejoice that thus i should be save many 
hour of lonely, maddening reflection. nay, henry might stand between 
me and the intrusion of my foe. if i were alone, would he not at times 
force his abhor presence on me to remind me of my task or to 
contemplate its progress? 

to england, therefore, i was bound, and it was understood that my union 
with elizabeth should take place immediately on my return. my father’s 
age render him extremely averse to delay. for myself, there was one 
reward i promise myself from my detest toils—one consolation for my 
unparalleled sufferings; it was the prospect of that day when, 
enfranchise from my miserable slavery, i might claim elizabeth and 
forget the past in my union with her. 

i now made arrangement for my journey, but one feeling haunt me 
which fill me with fear and agitation. during my absence i should 
leave my friends unconscious of the existence of their enemy and 
unprotected from his attacks, exasperate as he might be by my 
departure. but he had promise to follow me wherever i might go, and 
would he not accompany me to england? this imagination was dreadful in 
itself, but soothe inasmuch as it supposed the safety of my friends. 
i was agonised with the idea of the possibility that the reverse of 
this might happen. but through the whole period during which i was the 
slave of my creature i allow myself to be govern by the impulse of 
the moment; and my present sensation strong intimate that the fiend 
would follow me and exempt my family from the danger of his 
machinations. 

it was in the latter end of september that i again quit my native 
country. my journey had been my own suggestion, and elizabeth 
therefore acquiesced, but she was fill with disquiet at the idea of 
my suffering, away from her, the inroad of misery and grief. it had 
been her care which provided me a companion in clerval—and yet a man 
is blind to a thousand minute circumstances which call forth a woman’s 
sedulous attention. she long to bid me hasten my return; a thousand 
conflict emotion render her mute as she bade me a tearful, silent 
farewell. 

i threw myself into the carriage that was to convey me away, hardly 
knowing whither i was going, and careless of what was passing around. 
i remember only, and it was with a bitter anguish that i reflect on 
it, to order that my chemical instrument should be packed to go with 
me. fill with dreary imaginations, i pass through many beautiful 
and majestic scenes, but my eyes were fixed and unobserving. i could 
only think of the bourne of my travels and the work which was to occupy 
me whilst they endured. 

after some days spent in listless indolence, during which i traverse 
many leagues, i arrive at strasburgh, where i wait two days for 
clerval. he came. alas, how great was the contrast between us! he 
was alive to every new scene, joyful when he saw the beauty of the 
setting sun, and more happy when he beheld it rise and recommence a new 
day. he pointed out to me the shift colours of the landscape and 
the appearances of the sky. “this is what it is to live,” he cried; 
“now i enjoy existence! but you, my dear frankenstein, wherefore are 
you despond and sorrowful!” in truth, i was occupy by gloomy 
thought and neither saw the descent of the evening star nor the golden 
sunrise reflect in the rhine. and you, my friend, would be far more 
amuse with the journal of clerval, who observe the scenery with an 
eye of feeling and delight, than in listen to my reflections. i, a 
miserable wretch, haunt by a curse that shut up every avenue to 
enjoyment. 

we had agree to descend the rhine in a boat from strasburgh to 
rotterdam, whence we might take shipping for london. during this 
voyage we pass many willowy island and saw several beautiful towns. 
we stay a day at mannheim, and on the fifth from our departure from 
strasburgh, arrive at mainz. the course of the rhine below mainz 
become much more picturesque. the river descend rapid and wind 
between hills, not high, but steep, and of beautiful forms. we saw 
many ruin castle standing on the edge of precipices, surround by 
black woods, high and inaccessible. this part of the rhine, indeed, 
presents a singularly variegated landscape. in one spot you view 
rugged hills, ruin castle overlook tremendous precipices, with 
the dark rhine rush beneath; and on the sudden turn of a promontory, 
flourish vineyard with green slope bank and a meander river 
and populous town occupy the scene. 

we travelled at the time of the vintage and heard the song of the labourer 
as we glide down the stream. even i, depressed in mind, and my spirits 
continual agitate by gloomy feelings, even i was pleased. i lay at the 
bottom of the boat, and as i gaze on the cloudless blue sky, i seem to 
drink in a tranquill to which i had long been a stranger. and if these 
were my sensations, who can describe those of henry? he felt as if he had 
been transport to fairy-land and enjoy a happiness seldom taste by 
man. “i have seen,” he said, “the most beautiful scene 
of my own country; i have visit the lake of lucerne and uri, where the 
snowy mountain descend almost perpendicular to the water, casting black 
and impenetrable shades, which would cause a gloomy and mournful appearance 
were it not for the most verdant island that relieve the eye by their gay 
appearance; i have seen this lake agitate by a tempest, when the wind tore 
up whirlwind of water and gave you an idea of what the water-spout must be 
on the great ocean; and the wave dash with fury the base of the mountain, 
where the priest and his mistress were overwhelm by an avalanche and 
where their die voice are still said to be heard amid the pause of the 
nightly wind; i have seen the mountain of la valais, and the pay de vaud; 
but this country, victor, please me more than all those wonders. the 
mountain of switzerland are more majestic and strange, but there is a 
charm in the bank of this divine river that i never before saw equalled. 
look at that castle which overhang yon precipice; and that also on the 
island, almost conceal amongst the foliage of those lovely trees; and now 
that group of labourer coming from among their vines; and that village 
half hid in the recess of the mountain. oh, surely the spirit that inhabit 
and guard this place has a soul more in harmony with man than those who 
pile the glacier or retire to the inaccessible peak of the mountain of 
our own country.” 

clerval! beloved friend! even now it delight me to record your words and 
to dwell on the praise of which you are so eminently deserving. he was a 
being form in the “very poetry of nature.” his wild and 
enthusiast imagination was chasten by the sensibility of his heart. his 
soul overflow with ardent affections, and his friendship was of that 
devoted and wondrous nature that the worldly-minded teach us to look for only 
in the imagination. but even human sympathies were not sufficient to 
satisfy his eager mind. the scenery of external nature, which other regard 
only with admiration, he love with ardour:— 

——the sound cataract 
haunt him like a passion: the tall rock, 
the mountain, and the deep and gloomy wood, 
their colours and their forms, were then to him 
an appetite; a feeling, and a love, 
that had no need of a remote charm, 
by thought supplied, or any interest 
unborrow’d from the eye. 

[wordsworth’s “tintern abbey”.] 



and where do he now exist? is this gentle and lovely being lost 
for ever? has this mind, so replete with ideas, imagination fanciful 
and magnificent, which form a world, whose existence depend on the 
life of its creator;—has this mind perished? do it now only exist 
in my memory? no, it is not thus; your form so divine wrought, and 
beam with beauty, has decayed, but your spirit still visit and 
console your unhappy friend. 

pardon this gush of sorrow; these ineffectual words are but a slight 
tribute to the unexampled worth of henry, but they soothe my heart, 
overflow with the anguish which his remembrance creates. i will 
proceed with my tale. 

beyond cologne we descended to the plain of holland; and we resolve to 
post the remainder of our way, for the wind was contrary and the stream of 
the river was too gentle to aid us. 

our journey here lost the interest arise from beautiful scenery, but we 
arrive in a few days at rotterdam, whence we proceed by sea to england. 
it was on a clear morning, in the latter days of december, that i first saw 
the white cliff of britain. the bank of the thames present a new scene; 
they were flat but fertile, and almost every town was marked by the 
remembrance of some story. we saw tilbury fort and remember the spain 
armada, gravesend, woolwich, and greenwich—places which i had heard 
of even in my country. 

at length we saw the numerous steeple of london, st. paul’s towering 
above all, and the tower famed in english history. 





chapter 19 

london was our present point of rest; we determined to remain several 
month in this wonderful and celebrated city. clerval desire the 
intercourse of the men of genius and talent who flourish at this 
time, but this was with me a secondary object; i was principally 
occupy with the means of obtain the information necessary for the 
completion of my promise and quick avail myself of the letters of 
introduction that i had brought with me, address to the most 
distinguished natural philosophers. 

if this journey had taken place during my days of study and happiness, 
it would have afford me inexpressible pleasure. but a blight had 
come over my existence, and i only visit these people for the sake of 
the information they might give me on the subject in which my interest 
was so terribly profound. company was irksome to me; when alone, i 
could fill my mind with the sight of heaven and earth; the voice of 
henry soothe me, and i could thus cheat myself into a transitory 
peace. but busy, uninteresting, joyous face brought back despair to 
my heart. i saw an insurmountable barrier placed between me and my 
fellow men; this barrier was seal with the blood of william and 
justine, and to reflect on the event connected with those name fill 
my soul with anguish. 

but in clerval i saw the image of my former self; he was inquisitive 
and anxious to gain experience and instruction. the difference of 
manners which he observe was to him an inexhaustible source of 
instruction and amusement. he was also pursue an object he had long 
had in view. his design was to visit india, in the belief that he had 
in his knowledge of its various languages, and in the view he had 
taken of its society, the means of material assist the progress of 
europe colonize and trade. in britain only could he further the 
execution of his plan. he was for ever busy, and the only check to his 
enjoyment was my sorrowful and dejected mind. i try to conceal this 
as much as possible, that i might not debar him from the pleasure 
natural to one who was enter on a new scene of life, undisturbed by 
any care or bitter recollection. i often refuse to accompany him, 
allege another engagement, that i might remain alone. i now also 
began to collect the material necessary for my new creation, and this 
was to me like the torture of single drops of water continual fall 
on the head. every thought that was devoted to it was an extreme 
anguish, and every word that i spoke in allusion to it cause my lip 
to quiver, and my heart to palpitate. 

after passing some month in london, we received a letter from a person in 
scotland who had formerly been our visitor at geneva. he mention the 
beauty of his native country and ask us if those were not sufficient 
allurement to induce us to prolong our journey as far north as perth, 
where he resided. clerval eager desire to accept this invitation, and i, 
although i abhor society, wish to view again mountain and stream and 
all the wondrous works with which nature adorn her chosen dwelling-places. 

we had arrive in england at the beginning of october, and it was now 
february. we accordingly determined to commence our journey towards the 
north at the expiration of another month. in this expedition we did not 
intend to follow the great road to edinburgh, but to visit windsor, oxford, 
matlock, and the cumberland lakes, resolve to arrive at the completion of 
this tour about the end of july. i packed up my chemical instrument and 
the material i had collected, resolve to finish my labour in some 
obscure nook in the northern highlands of scotland. 

we quit london on the 27th of march and remain a few days at 
windsor, rambling in its beautiful forest. this was a new scene to us 
mountaineers; the majestic oaks, the quantity of game, and the herd of 
stately deer were all novelty to us. 

from thence we proceed to oxford. as we enter this city, our mind 
were fill with the remembrance of the event that had been transact 
there more than a century and a half before. it was here that charles 
i. had collected his forces. this city had remain faithful to him, 
after the whole nation had forsaken his cause to join the standard of 
parliament and liberty. the memory of that unfortunate king and his 
companions, the amiable falkland, the insolent goring, his queen, and 
son, gave a peculiar interest to every part of the city which they 
might be supposed to have inhabited. the spirit of elder days found a 
dwelling here, and we delight to trace its footsteps. if these 
feelings had not found an imaginary gratification, the appearance of 
the city had yet in itself sufficient beauty to obtain our admiration. 
the college are ancient and picturesque; the street are almost 
magnificent; and the lovely isis, which flow beside it through meadow 
of exquisite verdure, is spread forth into a placid expanse of waters, 
which reflect its majestic assemblage of towers, and spires, and 
domes, embosomed among age trees. 

i enjoy this scene, and yet my enjoyment was embitter both by the 
memory of the past and the anticipation of the future. i was form 
for peaceful happiness. during my youthful days discontent never 
visit my mind, and if i was ever overcome by _ennui_, the sight of what 
is beautiful in nature or the study of what is excellent and sublime in 
the production of man could always interest my heart and communicate 
elasticity to my spirits. but i am a blasted tree; the bolt has 
enter my soul; and i felt then that i should survive to exhibit what 
i shall soon cease to be—a miserable spectacle of wreck humanity, 
pitiable to other and intolerable to myself. 

we pass a considerable period at oxford, rambling among its environs 
and endeavour to identify every spot which might relate to the most 
animate epoch of english history. our little voyages of discovery 
were often prolonged by the successive object that present 
themselves. we visit the tomb of the illustrious hampden and the 
field on which that patriot fell. for a moment my soul was elevated 
from its debase and miserable fear to contemplate the divine idea 
of liberty and self-sacrifice of which these sight were the monument 
and the remembrancers. for an instant i dare to shake off my chain 
and look around me with a free and lofty spirit, but the iron had eaten 
into my flesh, and i sank again, tremble and hopeless, into my 
miserable self. 

we left oxford with regret and proceed to matlock, which was our next 
place of rest. the country in the neighbourhood of this village 
resembled, to a greater degree, the scenery of switzerland; but 
everything is on a lower scale, and the green hill want the crown of 
distant white alps which always attend on the piny mountain of my 
native country. we visit the wondrous cave and the little cabinet 
of natural history, where the curiosity are disposed in the same 
manner as in the collection at servox and chamounix. the latter name 
made me tremble when pronounced by henry, and i hasten to quit 
matlock, with which that terrible scene was thus associated. 

from derby, still journey northwards, we pass two month in 
cumberland and westmorland. i could now almost fancy myself among the 
switzerland mountains. the little patch of snow which yet linger on the 
northern side of the mountains, the lakes, and the dashing of the 
rocky stream were all familiar and dear sight to me. here also we 
made some acquaintances, who almost contrived to cheat me into 
happiness. the delight of clerval was proportion greater than 
mine; his mind expand in the company of men of talent, and he found 
in his own nature greater capacity and resources than he could have 
imagine himself to have possessed while he associate with his 
inferiors. “i could pass my life here,” said he to me; “and among 
these mountain i should scarcely regret switzerland and the rhine.” 

but he found that a traveller’s life is one that include much pain 
amidst its enjoyments. his feelings are for ever on the stretch; and 
when he begin to sink into repose, he find himself oblige to quit 
that on which he rest in pleasure for something new, which again 
engage his attention, and which also he forsake for other novelties. 

we had scarcely visit the various lake of cumberland and westmorland 
and conceive an affection for some of the inhabitant when the period 
of our appointment with our scotch friend approached, and we left them 
to travel on. for my own part i was not sorry. i had now neglect my 
promise for some time, and i fear the effects of the dæmon’s 
disappointment. he might remain in switzerland and wreak his vengeance 
on my relatives. this idea pursue me and torment me at every moment 
from which i might otherwise have snatch repose and peace. i wait 
for my letters with feverish impatience; if they were delay i was 
miserable and overcome by a thousand fears; and when they arrive and i 
saw the superscription of elizabeth or my father, i hardly dare to 
read and ascertain my fate. sometimes i thought that the fiend 
follow me and might expedite my remiss by murder my companion. 
when these thought possessed me, i would not quit henry for a moment, 
but follow him as his shadow, to protect him from the fancy rage of 
his destroyer. i felt as if i had committed some great crime, the 
consciousness of which haunt me. i was guiltless, but i had indeed 
drawn down a horrible curse upon my head, as mortal as that of crime. 

i visit edinburgh with languid eyes and mind; and yet that city might 
have interested the most unfortunate being. clerval did not like it so well 
as oxford, for the antiquity of the latter city was more pleasing to him. 
but the beauty and regularity of the new town of edinburgh, its romantic 
castle and its environs, the most delightful in the world, arthur’s 
seat, st. bernard’s well, and the pentland hills, compensate him for 
the change and fill him with cheerful and admiration. but i was 
impatient to arrive at the termination of my journey. 

we left edinburgh in a week, passing through coupar, st. andrew’s, and 
along the bank of the tay, to perth, where our friend expect us. 
but i was in no mood to laugh and talk with stranger or enter into 
their feelings or plan with the good humour expect from a guest; and 
accordingly i told clerval that i wish to make the tour of scotland 
alone. “do you,” said i, “enjoy yourself, and let this be our 
rendezvous. i may be absent a month or two; but do not interfere with 
my motions, i entreat you; leave me to peace and solitude for a short 
time; and when i return, i hope it will be with a lighter heart, more 
congenial to your own temper.” 

henry wish to dissuade me, but seeing me bent on this plan, cease to 
remonstrate. he entreat me to write often. “i had rather be with 
you,” he said, “in your solitary rambles, than with these scotch 
people, whom i do not know; hasten, then, my dear friend, to return, 
that i may again feel myself somewhat at home, which i cannot do in 
your absence.” 

have part from my friend, i determined to visit some remote spot of 
scotland and finish my work in solitude. i did not doubt but that the 
monster follow me and would discover himself to me when i should have 
finished, that he might receive his companion. 

with this resolution i traverse the northern highlands and fixed on one of 
the remotest of the orkney as the scene of my labours. it was a place 
fitted for such a work, being hardly more than a rock whose high side were 
continual beaten upon by the waves. the soil was barren, scarcely 
afford pasture for a few miserable cows, and oatmeal for its 
inhabitants, which consist of five persons, whose gaunt and scraggy limb 
gave token of their miserable fare. vegetable and bread, when they 
indulge in such luxuries, and even fresh water, was to be procure from 
the mainland, which was about five mile distant. 

on the whole island there were but three miserable huts, and one of 
these was vacant when i arrived. this i hired. it contained but two 
rooms, and these exhibit all the squalid of the most miserable 
penury. the thatch had fallen in, the wall were unplastered, and the 
door was off its hinges. i ordered it to be repaired, bought some 
furniture, and took possession, an incident which would doubtless have 
occasion some surprise had not all the senses of the cottager been 
benumbed by want and squalid poverty. as it was, i live ungazed at 
and unmolested, hardly thank for the pittance of food and clothes 
which i gave, so much do suffering blunt even the coarsest sensation 
of men. 

in this retreat i devoted the morning to labour; but in the evening, 
when the weather permitted, i walk on the stony beach of the sea to 
listen to the wave as they roar and dashed at my feet. it was a 
monotonous yet ever-changing scene. i thought of switzerland; it was 
far different from this desolate and appalling landscape. its hill 
are cover with vines, and its cottage are scattered thick in the 
plains. its fair lake reflect a blue and gentle sky, and when 
trouble by the winds, their tumult is but as the play of a lively 
infant when compare to the roaring of the giant ocean. 

in this manner i distribute my occupation when i first arrived, but 
as i proceed in my labour, it became every day more horrible and 
irksome to me. sometimes i could not prevail on myself to enter my 
laboratory for several days, and at other times i toil day and night 
in order to complete my work. it was, indeed, a filthy process in 
which i was engaged. during my first experiment, a kind of 
enthusiast frenzy had blind me to the horror of my employment; my 
mind was intent fixed on the consummation of my labour, and my eyes 
were shut to the horror of my proceedings. but now i went to it in 
cold blood, and my heart often sicken at the work of my hands. 

thus situated, employ in the most detest occupation, immerse in 
a solitude where nothing could for an instant call my attention from 
the actual scene in which i was engaged, my spirits became unequal; i 
grew restless and nervous. every moment i fear to meet my 
persecutor. sometimes i sat with my eyes fixed on the ground, fear 
to raise them lest they should encounter the object which i so much 
dread to behold. i fear to wander from the sight of my fellow 
creature lest when alone he should come to claim his companion. 

in the mean time i work on, and my labour was already considerably 
advanced. i look towards its completion with a tremulous and eager 
hope, which i dare not trust myself to question but which was 
intermixe with obscure foreboding of evil that made my heart sicken 
in my bosom. 





chapter 20 

i sat one evening in my laboratory; the sun had set, and the moon was just 
rising from the sea; i had not sufficient light for my employment, and i 
remain idle, in a pause of consideration of whether i should leave my 
labour for the night or hasten its conclusion by an unremitting attention 
to it. as i sat, a train of reflection occur to me which led me to 
consider the effects of what i was now doing. three years before, i was 
engaged in the same manner and had create a fiend whose unparalleled 
barbarous had desolate my heart and fill it for ever with the bitterest 
remorse. i was now about to form another being of whose disposition i was 
alike ignorant; she might become ten thousand times more malignant than her 
mate and delight, for its own sake, in murder and wretchedness. he had 
sworn to quit the neighbourhood of man and hide himself in deserts, but she 
had not; and she, who in all probability was to become a thinking and 
reasoning animal, might refuse to comply with a compact made before her 
creation. they might even hate each other; the creature who already live 
loathe his own deformity, and might he not conceive a greater abhorrence 
for it when it came before his eyes in the female form? she also might turn 
with disgust from him to the superior beauty of man; she might quit him, 
and he be again alone, exasperate by the fresh provocation of being 
desert by one of his own species. 

even if they were to leave europe and inhabit the deserts of the new world, 
yet one of the first result of those sympathies for which the dæmon 
thirst would be children, and a race of devil would be propagate upon 
the earth who might make the very existence of the species of man a 
condition precarious and full of terror. had i right, for my own benefit, 
to inflict this curse upon everlasting generations? i had before been move 
by the sophism of the being i had created; i had been struck senseless by 
his fiendish threats; but now, for the first time, the wicked of my 
promise burst upon me; i shudder to think that future age might curse me 
as their pest, whose selfish had not hesitate to buy its own peace at 
the price, perhaps, of the existence of the whole human race. 

i tremble and my heart fail within me, when, on look up, i saw by 
the light of the moon the dæmon at the casement. a ghastly grin 
wrinkle his lip as he gaze on me, where i sat fulfill the task 
which he had allot to me. yes, he had follow me in my travels; he 
had loiter in forests, hid himself in caves, or taken refuge in wide 
and desert heaths; and he now came to mark my progress and claim the 
fulfilment of my promise. 

as i look on him, his countenance express the utmost extent of 
malice and treachery. i thought with a sensation of madness on my 
promise of create another like to him, and tremble with passion, 
tore to pieces the thing on which i was engaged. the wretch saw me 
destroy the creature on whose future existence he depend for 
happiness, and with a howl of devilish despair and revenge, withdrew. 

i left the room, and lock the door, made a solemn vow in my own 
heart never to resume my labours; and then, with tremble steps, i 
sought my own apartment. i was alone; none were near me to dissipate 
the gloom and relieve me from the sickening oppression of the most 
terrible reveries. 

several hour passed, and i remain near my window gaze on the sea; 
it was almost motionless, for the wind were hushed, and all nature 
repose under the eye of the quiet moon. a few fishing vessel alone 
speck the water, and now and then the gentle breeze waft the sound 
of voice as the fishermen call to one another. i felt the silence, 
although i was hardly conscious of its extreme profundity, until my ear 
was sudden arrest by the paddle of oar near the shore, and a 
person landed close to my house. 

in a few minutes after, i heard the creak of my door, as if some one 
endeavour to open it softly. i tremble from head to foot; i felt a 
presentiment of who it was and wish to rouse one of the peasant who 
dwelt in a cottage not far from mine; but i was overcome by the sensation 
of helplessness, so often felt in frightful dreams, when you in vain 
endeavour to fly from an impending danger, and was rooted to the spot. 

presently i heard the sound of footstep along the passage; the door 
opened, and the wretch whom i dread appeared. shut the door, he 
approach me and said in a smother voice, 

“you have destroy the work which you began; what is it that you 
intend? do you dare to break your promise? i have endure toil and misery; 
i left switzerland with you; i crept along the shore of the rhine, among 
its willow island and over the summit of its hills. i have dwelt many 
month in the heath of england and among the deserts of scotland. i have 
endure incalculable fatigue, and cold, and hunger; do you dare destroy my 
hopes?” 

“begone! i do break my promise; never will i create another like 
yourself, equal in deformity and wickedness.” 

“slave, i before reasoned with you, but you have prove yourself 
unworthy of my condescension. remember that i have power; you believe 
yourself miserable, but i can make you so wretched that the light of 
day will be hateful to you. you are my creator, but i am your master; 
obey!” 

“the hour of my irresolute is past, and the period of your power is 
arrived. your threat cannot move me to do an act of wickedness; but 
they confirm me in a determination of not create you a companion in 
vice. shall i, in cool blood, set loose upon the earth a dæmon whose 
delight is in death and wretchedness? begone! i am firm, and your 
words will only exasperate my rage.” 

the monster saw my determination in my face and gnash his teeth in the 
impotence of anger. “shall each man,” cry he, “find a 
wife for his bosom, and each beast have his mate, and i be alone? i had 
feelings of affection, and they were requite by detest and scorn. 
man! you may hate, but beware! your hour will pass in dread and misery, 
and soon the bolt will fall which must ravish from you your happiness for 
ever. are you to be happy while i grovel in the intensity of my 
wretchedness? you can blast my other passions, but revenge 
remains—revenge, henceforth dear than light or food! i may die, but 
first you, my tyrant and tormentor, shall curse the sun that gaze on your 
misery. beware, for i am fearless and therefore powerful. i will watch with 
the wiliness of a snake, that i may sting with its venom. man, you shall 
repent of the injury you inflict.” 

“devil, cease; and do not poison the air with these sound of malice. 
i have declared my resolution to you, and i am no coward to bend 
beneath words. leave me; i am inexorable.” 

“it is well. i go; but remember, i shall be with you on your 
wedding-night.” 

i start forward and exclaimed, “villain! before you sign my 
death-warrant, be sure that you are yourself safe.” 

i would have seize him, but he elude me and quit the house with 
precipitation. in a few moments i saw him in his boat, which shot 
across the waters with an arrowy swift and was soon lost amidst the 
waves. 

all was again silent, but his words rang in my ears. i burn with rage to 
pursue the murder of my peace and precipitate him into the ocean. i 
walk up and down my room hasty and perturbed, while my imagination 
conjure up a thousand image to torment and sting me. why had i not 
follow him and closed with him in mortal strife? but i had suffer him 
to depart, and he had direct his course towards the mainland. i shudder 
to think who might be the next victim sacrifice to his insatiate revenge. 
and then i thought again of his words—“_i will be with you on 
your wedding-night._” that, then, was the period fixed for the 
fulfilment of my destiny. in that hour i should die and at once satisfy and 
extinguish his malice. the prospect did not move me to fear; yet when i 
thought of my beloved elizabeth, of her tear and endless sorrow, when she 
should find her lover so barbarous snatch from her, tears, the first i 
had shed for many months, stream from my eyes, and i resolve not to fall 
before my enemy without a bitter struggle. 

the night pass away, and the sun rose from the ocean; my feelings became 
calmer, if it may be call calm when the violence of rage sink into 
the depths of despair. i left the house, the horrid scene of the last 
night’s contention, and walk on the beach of the sea, which i 
almost regard as an insuperable barrier between me and my fellow 
creatures; nay, a wish that such should prove the fact stole across me. i 
desire that i might pass my life on that barren rock, wearily, it is true, 
but uninterrupted by any sudden shock of misery. if i returned, it was to 
be sacrifice or to see those whom i most love die under the grasp of a 
dæmon whom i had myself created. 

i walk about the isle like a restless spectre, separate from all it 
love and miserable in the separation. when it became noon, and the 
sun rose higher, i lay down on the grass and was overpower by a deep 
sleep. i had been awake the whole of the preceding night, my nerves 
were agitated, and my eyes inflamed by watch and misery. the sleep 
into which i now sank refresh me; and when i awoke, i again felt as 
if i belong to a race of human being like myself, and i began to 
reflect upon what had pass with greater composure; yet still the 
words of the fiend rang in my ear like a death-knell; they appear 
like a dream, yet distinct and oppressive as a reality. 

the sun had far descended, and i still sat on the shore, satisfying my 
appetite, which had become ravenous, with an oaten cake, when i saw a 
fishing-boat land close to me, and one of the men brought me a packet; 
it contained letters from geneva, and one from clerval entreat me to 
join him. he said that he was wearing away his time fruitless where 
he was, that letters from the friends he had form in london desire 
his return to complete the negotiation they had enter into for his 
indian enterprise. he could not any long delay his departure; but as 
his journey to london might be followed, even soon than he now 
conjectured, by his long voyage, he entreat me to bestow as much of 
my society on him as i could spare. he besought me, therefore, to 
leave my solitary isle and to meet him at perth, that we might proceed 
southwards together. this letter in a degree recall me to life, and 
i determined to quit my island at the expiration of two days. 

yet, before i departed, there was a task to perform, on which i shudder 
to reflect; i must pack up my chemical instruments, and for that purpose i 
must enter the room which had been the scene of my odious work, and i must 
handle those utensil the sight of which was sickening to me. the next 
morning, at daybreak, i summon sufficient courage and unlock the door 
of my laboratory. the remains of the half-finished creature, whom i had 
destroyed, lay scattered on the floor, and i almost felt as if i had 
mangle the living flesh of a human being. i pause to collect myself and 
then enter the chamber. with tremble hand i convey the instrument 
out of the room, but i reflect that i ought not to leave the relics of my 
work to excite the horror and suspicion of the peasants; and i accordingly 
put them into a basket, with a great quantity of stones, and lay them 
up, determined to throw them into the sea that very night; and in the 
meantime i sat upon the beach, employ in clean and arrange my 
chemical apparatus. 

nothing could be more complete than the alteration that had taken place 
in my feelings since the night of the appearance of the dæmon. i had 
before regard my promise with a gloomy despair as a thing that, with 
whatever consequences, must be fulfilled; but i now felt as if a film 
had been taken from before my eyes and that i for the first time saw 
clearly. the idea of renew my labour did not for one instant occur 
to me; the threat i had heard weigh on my thoughts, but i did not 
reflect that a voluntary act of mine could avert it. i had resolve in 
my own mind that to create another like the fiend i had first made 
would be an act of the basest and most atrocious selfishness, and i 
banish from my mind every thought that could lead to a different 
conclusion. 

between two and three in the morning the moon rose; and i then, putt my 
basket aboard a little skiff, sail out about four mile from the shore. 
the scene was perfectly solitary; a few boat were return towards land, 
but i sail away from them. i felt as if i was about the commission of a 
dreadful crime and avoid with shudder anxiety any encounter with my 
fellow creatures. at one time the moon, which had before been clear, was 
sudden overspread by a thick cloud, and i took advantage of the moment of 
dark and cast my basket into the sea; i listen to the gurgle sound 
as it sank and then sail away from the spot. the sky became clouded, but 
the air was pure, although chill by the northeast breeze that was then 
rising. but it refresh me and fill me with such agreeable sensation 
that i resolve to prolong my stay on the water, and fix the rudder in a 
direct position, stretch myself at the bottom of the boat. cloud hid the 
moon, everything was obscure, and i heard only the sound of the boat as its 
keel cut through the waves; the murmur lull me, and in a short time i 
slept soundly. 

i do not know how long i remain in this situation, but when i awoke i 
found that the sun had already mount considerably. the wind was high, and 
the wave continual threaten the safety of my little skiff. i found 
that the wind was northeast and must have driven me far from the coast from 
which i had embarked. i endeavour to change my course but quick found 
that if i again made the attempt the boat would be instantly fill with 
water. thus situated, my only resource was to drive before the wind. i 
confess that i felt a few sensation of terror. i had no compass with me 
and was so slender acquaint with the geography of this part of the 
world that the sun was of little benefit to me. i might be driven into the 
wide atlantic and feel all the torture of starvation or be swallow up in 
the immeasurable waters that roar and buffet around me. i had already 
been out many hour and felt the torment of a burning thirst, a prelude to 
my other sufferings. i look on the heavens, which were cover by cloud 
that flew before the wind, only to be replace by others; i look upon the 
sea; it was to be my grave. “fiend,” i exclaimed, “your 
task is already fulfilled!” i thought of elizabeth, of my father, and 
of clerval—all left behind, on whom the monster might satisfy his 
sanguinary and merciless passions. this idea plunge me into a reverie so 
despairing and frightful that even now, when the scene is on the point of 
close before me for ever, i shudder to reflect on it. 

some hour pass thus; but by degrees, as the sun decline towards the 
horizon, the wind die away into a gentle breeze and the sea became 
free from breakers. but these gave place to a heavy swell; i felt sick 
and hardly able to hold the rudder, when sudden i saw a line of high 
land towards the south. 

almost spent, as i was, by fatigue and the dreadful suspense i endure 
for several hours, this sudden certainty of life rush like a flood of 
warm joy to my heart, and tear gush from my eyes. 

how mutable are our feelings, and how strange is that clinging love we have 
of life even in the excess of misery! i construct another sail with a 
part of my dress and eager steer my course towards the land. it had a 
wild and rocky appearance, but as i approach near i easily perceive 
the trace of cultivation. i saw vessel near the shore and found myself 
sudden transport back to the neighbourhood of civilise man. i 
careful trace the winding of the land and hail a steeple which i at 
length saw issue from behind a small promontory. as i was in a state of 
extreme debility, i resolve to sail directly towards the town, as a place 
where i could most easily procure nourishment. fortunately i had money with 
me. as i turn the promontory i perceive a small neat town and a good 
harbour, which i entered, my heart bound with joy at my unexpected 
escape. 

as i was occupy in fix the boat and arrange the sails, several 
people crowded towards the spot. they seem much surprise at my 
appearance, but instead of offering me any assistance, whisper 
together with gesture that at any other time might have produce in me 
a slight sensation of alarm. as it was, i merely remark that they 
spoke english, and i therefore address them in that language. “my 
good friends,” said i, “will you be so kind as to tell me the name of 
this town and inform me where i am?” 

“you will know that soon enough,” reply a man with a hoarse voice. 
“maybe you are come to a place that will not prove much to your taste, 
but you will not be consult as to your quarters, i promise you.” 

i was exceedingly surprise on receiving so rude an answer from a 
stranger, and i was also disconcert on perceive the frown and 
angry countenance of his companions. “why do you answer me so 
roughly?” i replied. “surely it is not the custom of englishmen to 
receive stranger so inhospitably.” 

“i do not know,” said the man, “what the custom of the 
english may be, but it is the custom of the ireland to hate villains.” 

while this strange dialogue continued, i perceive the crowd rapid 
increase. their face express a mixture of curiosity and anger, which 
annoy and in some degree alarm me. i inquire the way to the inn, but 
no one replied. i then move forward, and a murmur sound arose from the 
crowd as they follow and surround me, when an ill-looking man 
approach tap me on the shoulder and said, “come, sir, you must 
follow me to mr. kirwin’s to give an account of yourself.” 

“who is mr. kirwin? why am i to give an account of myself? is not 
this a free country?” 

“ay, sir, free enough for honest folks. mr. kirwin is a magistrate, 
and you are to give an account of the death of a gentleman who was 
found murder here last night.” 

this answer startle me, but i presently recover myself. i was innocent; 
that could easily be proved; accordingly i follow my conductor in silence 
and was led to one of the best house in the town. i was ready to sink from 
fatigue and hunger, but being surround by a crowd, i thought it politic 
to rouse all my strength, that no physical debility might be construe into 
apprehension or conscious guilt. little did i then expect the calamity that 
was in a few moments to overwhelm me and extinguish in horror and despair 
all fear of ignominy or death. 

i must pause here, for it require all my fortitude to recall the memory of 
the frightful event which i am about to relate, in proper detail, to my 
recollection. 





chapter 21 

i was soon introduce into the presence of the magistrate, an old 
benevolent man with calm and mild manners. he look upon me, however, 
with some degree of severity, and then, turning towards my conductors, 
he ask who appear as witness on this occasion. 

about half a dozen men came forward; and, one being select by the 
magistrate, he depose that he had been out fishing the night before with 
his son and brother-in-law, daniel nugent, when, about ten o’clock, 
they observe a strong northerly blast rising, and they accordingly put in 
for port. it was a very dark night, as the moon had not yet risen; they did 
not land at the harbour, but, as they had been accustomed, at a creek about 
two mile below. he walk on first, carry a part of the fishing tackle, 
and his companion follow him at some distance. as he was proceeding 
along the sands, he struck his foot against something and fell at his 
length on the ground. his companion came up to assist him, and by the 
light of their lantern they found that he had fallen on the body of a man, 
who was to all appearance dead. their first supposition was that it was the 
corpse of some person who had been drown and was thrown on shore by the 
waves, but on examination they found that the clothes were not wet and even 
that the body was not then cold. they instantly carry it to the cottage 
of an old woman near the spot and endeavoured, but in vain, to restore it 
to life. it appear to be a handsome young man, about five and twenty 
years of age. he had apparently been strangled, for there was no sign of 
any violence except the black mark of finger on his neck. 

the first part of this deposition did not in the least interest me, but 
when the mark of the finger was mention i remember the murder of 
my brother and felt myself extremely agitated; my limb trembled, and a 
mist came over my eyes, which oblige me to lean on a chair for 
support. the magistrate observe me with a keen eye and of course drew 
an unfavourable augury from my manner. 

the son confirmed his father’s account, but when daniel nugent was 
call he swore positively that just before the fall of his companion, he 
saw a boat, with a single man in it, at a short distance from the shore; 
and as far as he could judge by the light of a few stars, it was the same 
boat in which i had just landed. 

a woman depose that she live near the beach and was standing at the door 
of her cottage, wait for the return of the fishermen, about an hour 
before she heard of the discovery of the body, when she saw a boat with 
only one man in it push off from that part of the shore where the corpse 
was afterwards found. 

another woman confirmed the account of the fishermen have brought the
