body into her house; it was not cold. they put it into a bed and 
rub it, and daniel went to the town for an apothecary, but life was 
quite gone. 

several other men were examine concerning my landing, and they agree 
that, with the strong north wind that had arisen during the night, it 
was very probable that i had beaten about for many hour and had been 
oblige to return nearly to the same spot from which i had departed. 
besides, they observe that it appear that i had brought the body 
from another place, and it was likely that as i did not appear to know 
the shore, i might have put into the harbour ignorant of the distance 
of the town of —— from the place where i had deposit the corpse. 

mr. kirwin, on hearing this evidence, desire that i should be taken into 
the room where the body lay for interment, that it might be observe what 
effect the sight of it would produce upon me. this idea was probably 
suggest by the extreme agitation i had exhibit when the mode of the 
murder had been described. i was accordingly conducted, by the magistrate 
and several other persons, to the inn. i could not help being struck by the 
strange coincidence that had taken place during this eventful night; but, 
knowing that i had been converse with several person in the island i had 
inhabit about the time that the body had been found, i was perfectly 
tranquil as to the consequence of the affair. 

i enter the room where the corpse lay and was led up to the coffin. how 
can i describe my sensation on behold it? i feel yet parch with 
horror, nor can i reflect on that terrible moment without shudder and 
agony. the examination, the presence of the magistrate and witnesses, 
pass like a dream from my memory when i saw the lifeless form of henry 
clerval stretch before me. i gasp for breath, and throw myself on 
the body, i exclaimed, “have my murderous machination deprived you 
also, my dearest henry, of life? two i have already destroyed; other 
victim await their destiny; but you, clerval, my friend, my 
benefactor—” 

the human frame could no long support the agony that i endured, and 
i was carry out of the room in strong convulsions. 

a fever succeed to this. i lay for two month on the point of death; my 
ravings, as i afterwards heard, were frightful; i call myself the 
murder of william, of justine, and of clerval. sometimes i entreat my 
attendant to assist me in the destruction of the fiend by whom i was 
tormented; and at other i felt the finger of the monster already grasping 
my neck, and scream aloud with agony and terror. fortunately, as i spoke 
my native language, mr. kirwin alone understood me; but my gesture and 
bitter cries were sufficient to affright the other witnesses. 

why did i not die? more miserable than man ever was before, why did i not 
sink into forgetful and rest? death snatch away many blooming 
children, the only hope of their doting parents; how many bride and 
youthful lovers have been one day in the bloom of health and hope, and the 
next a prey for worm and the decay of the tomb! of what material was i 
made that i could thus resist so many shocks, which, like the turning of 
the wheel, continual renew the torture? 

but i was doom to live and in two month found myself as awake from 
a dream, in a prison, stretch on a wretched bed, surround by 
gaolers, turnkeys, bolts, and all the miserable apparatus of a dungeon. 
it was morning, i remember, when i thus awoke to understanding; i had 
forgotten the particulars of what had happen and only felt as if some 
great misfortune had sudden overwhelm me; but when i look around 
and saw the barred window and the squalid of the room in which i 
was, all flash across my memory and i groan bitterly. 

this sound disturbed an old woman who was sleep in a chair beside 
me. she was a hire nurse, the wife of one of the turnkeys, and her 
countenance express all those bad quality which often characterise 
that class. the lines of her face were hard and rude, like that of 
person accustomed to see without sympathise in sight of misery. her 
tone express her entire indifference; she address me in english, 
and the voice struck me as one that i had heard during my sufferings. 

“are you better now, sir?” said she. 

i reply in the same language, with a feeble voice, “i believe i am; 
but if it be all true, if indeed i did not dream, i am sorry that i am 
still alive to feel this misery and horror.” 

“for that matter,” reply the old woman, “if you mean about the 
gentleman you murdered, i believe that it were better for you if you 
were dead, for i fancy it will go hard with you! however, that’s none 
of my business; i am sent to nurse you and get you well; i do my duty 
with a safe conscience; it were well if everybody did the same.” 

i turn with loathing from the woman who could utter so unfeeling a 
speech to a person just saved, on the very edge of death; but i felt 
languid and unable to reflect on all that had passed. the whole series 
of my life appear to me as a dream; i sometimes doubt if indeed it 
were all true, for it never present itself to my mind with the force 
of reality. 

as the image that float before me became more distinct, i grew 
feverish; a dark pressed around me; no one was near me who soothe 
me with the gentle voice of love; no dear hand support me. the 
physician came and prescribed medicines, and the old woman prepared 
them for me; but utter careless was visible in the first, and the 
expression of brutality was strong marked in the visage of the 
second. who could be interested in the fate of a murder but the 
hangman who would gain his fee? 

these were my first reflections, but i soon learned that mr. kirwin had 
shown me extreme kindness. he had cause the best room in the prison 
to be prepared for me (wretched indeed was the best); and it was he who 
had provided a physician and a nurse. it is true, he seldom came to 
see me, for although he ardent desire to relieve the suffering of 
every human creature, he did not wish to be present at the agony and 
miserable ravings of a murderer. he came, therefore, sometimes to see 
that i was not neglected, but his visit were short and with long 
intervals. 

one day, while i was gradual recovering, i was seat in a chair, my eyes 
half open and my cheek livid like those in death. i was overcome by gloom 
and misery and often reflect i had better seek death than desire to 
remain in a world which to me was replete with wretchedness. at one time i 
considered whether i should not declare myself guilty and suffer the 
penalty of the law, less innocent than poor justine had been. such were my 
thought when the door of my apartment was open and mr. kirwin entered. 
his countenance express sympathy and compassion; he drew a chair close to 
mine and address me in french, 

“i fear that this place is very shocking to you; can i do anything to 
make you more comfortable?” 

“i thank you, but all that you mention is nothing to me; on the whole 
earth there is no comfort which i am capable of receiving.” 

“i know that the sympathy of a stranger can be but of little relief to 
one borne down as you are by so strange a misfortune. but you will, i 
hope, soon quit this melancholy abode, for doubtless evidence can 
easily be brought to free you from the criminal charge.” 

“that is my least concern; i am, by a course of strange events, become 
the most miserable of mortals. persecute and torture as i am and 
have been, can death be any evil to me?” 

“nothing indeed could be more unfortunate and agonising than the 
strange chance that have lately occurred. you were thrown, by some 
surprising accident, on this shore, renowned for its hospitality, 
seize immediately, and charged with murder. the first sight that was 
present to your eyes was the body of your friend, murder in so 
unaccountable a manner and placed, as it were, by some fiend across 
your path.” 

as mr. kirwin said this, notwithstanding the agitation i endure on 
this retrospect of my sufferings, i also felt considerable surprise at 
the knowledge he seem to possess concerning me. i suppose some 
astonishment was exhibit in my countenance, for mr. kirwin hasten 
to say, 

“immediately upon your being taken ill, all the papers that were on 
your person were brought me, and i examine them that i might discover some 
trace by which i could send to your relations an account of your misfortune 
and illness. i found several letters, and, among others, one which i 
discover from its commencement to be from your father. i instantly wrote 
to geneva; nearly two month have elapse since the departure of my letter. 
but you are ill; even now you tremble; you are unfit for agitation of any 
kind.” 

“this suspense is a thousand times worse than the most horrible event; 
tell me what new scene of death has been acted, and whose murder i am 
now to lament?” 

“your family is perfectly well,” said mr. kirwin with 
gentleness; “and someone, a friend, is come to visit you.” 

i know not by what chain of thought the idea present itself, but it 
instantly dart into my mind that the murder had come to mock at my 
misery and taunt me with the death of clerval, as a new incite for 
me to comply with his hellish desires. i put my hand before my eyes, 
and cry out in agony, 

“oh! take him away! i cannot see him; for god’s sake, do not 
let him enter!” 

mr. kirwin regard me with a trouble countenance. he could not help 
regarding my exclamation as a presumption of my guilt and said in 
rather a severe tone, 

“i should have thought, young man, that the presence of your father 
would have been welcome instead of inspire such violent repugnance.” 

“my father!” cry i, while every feature and every muscle was relax 
from anguish to pleasure. “is my father indeed come? how kind, how 
very kind! but where is he, why do he not hasten to me?” 

my change of manner surprise and pleased the magistrate; perhaps he 
thought that my former exclamation was a momentary return of delirium, 
and now he instantly resume his former benevolence. he rose and 
quit the room with my nurse, and in a moment my father enter it. 

nothing, at this moment, could have given me greater pleasure than the 
arrival of my father. i stretch out my hand to him and cried, 

“are you then safe—and elizabeth—and ernest?” 

my father calm me with assurance of their welfare and endeavoured, by 
dwelling on these subject so interesting to my heart, to raise my 
despond spirits; but he soon felt that a prison cannot be the abode of 
cheerfulness. “what a place is this that you inhabit, my son!” 
said he, look mournful at the barred window and wretched appearance 
of the room. “you travelled to seek happiness, but a fatality seem 
to pursue you. and poor clerval—” 

the name of my unfortunate and murder friend was an agitation too 
great to be endure in my weak state; i shed tears. 

“alas! yes, my father,” reply i; “some destiny of the 
most horrible kind hang over me, and i must live to fulfil it, or surely i 
should have die on the coffin of henry.” 

we were not allow to converse for any length of time, for the 
precarious state of my health render every precaution necessary that 
could ensure tranquillity. mr. kirwin came in and insist that my 
strength should not be exhaust by too much exertion. but the 
appearance of my father was to me like that of my good angel, and i 
gradual recover my health. 

as my sickness quit me, i was absorb by a gloomy and black 
melancholy that nothing could dissipate. the image of clerval was 
for ever before me, ghastly and murdered. more than once the agitation 
into which these reflection threw me made my friends dread a dangerous 
relapse. alas! why did they preserve so miserable and detest a 
life? it was surely that i might fulfil my destiny, which is now 
drawing to a close. soon, oh, very soon, will death extinguish these 
throb and relieve me from the mighty weight of anguish that bear 
me to the dust; and, in execute the award of justice, i shall also 
sink to rest. then the appearance of death was distant, although the 
wish was ever present to my thoughts; and i often sat for hour 
motionless and speechless, wish for some mighty revolution that 
might bury me and my destroyer in its ruins. 

the season of the assizes approached. i had already been three month 
in prison, and although i was still weak and in continual danger of a 
relapse, i was oblige to travel nearly a hundred mile to the country 
town where the court was held. mr. kirwin charged himself with every 
care of collect witness and arrange my defence. i was spare 
the disgrace of appear public as a criminal, as the case was not 
brought before the court that decide on life and death. the grand 
jury reject the bill, on its being prove that i was on the orkney 
island at the hour the body of my friend was found; and a fortnight 
after my removal i was liberated from prison. 

my father was enrapture on finding me free from the vexation of a 
criminal charge, that i was again allow to breathe the fresh 
atmosphere and permit to return to my native country. i did not 
participate in these feelings, for to me the wall of a dungeon or a 
palace were alike hateful. the cup of life was poison for ever, and 
although the sun shone upon me, as upon the happy and gay of heart, i 
saw around me nothing but a dense and frightful darkness, penetrate by 
no light but the glimmer of two eyes that glare upon me. sometimes 
they were the expressive eyes of henry, languish in death, the dark 
orb nearly cover by the lid and the long black lash that fringe 
them; sometimes it was the watery, cloud eyes of the monster, as i 
first saw them in my chamber at ingolstadt. 

my father try to awaken in me the feelings of affection. he talk 
of geneva, which i should soon visit, of elizabeth and ernest; but 
these words only drew deep groan from me. sometimes, indeed, i felt a 
wish for happiness and thought with melancholy delight of my beloved 
cousin or longed, with a devour _maladie du pays_, to see once more 
the blue lake and rapid rhone, that had been so dear to me in early 
childhood; but my general state of feeling was a torpor in which a 
prison was as welcome a residence as the divinest scene in nature; and 
these fit were seldom interrupt but by paroxysm of anguish and 
despair. at these moments i often endeavour to put an end to the 
existence i loathed, and it require uncease attendance and vigilance 
to restrain me from commit some dreadful act of violence. 

yet one duty remain to me, the recollection of which finally 
triumph over my selfish despair. it was necessary that i should 
return without delay to geneva, there to watch over the lives of those 
i so fondly love and to lie in wait for the murderer, that if any 
chance led me to the place of his concealment, or if he dare again to 
blast me by his presence, i might, with unfailing aim, put an end to 
the existence of the monstrous image which i had endue with the 
mockery of a soul still more monstrous. my father still desire to 
delay our departure, fearful that i could not sustain the fatigues of a 
journey, for i was a shatter wreck—the shadow of a human being. my 
strength was gone. i was a mere skeleton, and fever night and day 
prey upon my waste frame. 

still, as i urge our leave ireland with such inquietude and impatience, 
my father thought it best to yield. we took our passage on board a vessel 
bound for havre-de-grace and sail with a fair wind from the ireland shores. 
it was midnight. i lay on the deck look at the stars and listen to 
the dashing of the waves. i hail the dark that shut ireland from my 
sight, and my pulse beat with a feverish joy when i reflect that i should 
soon see geneva. the past appear to me in the light of a frightful dream; 
yet the vessel in which i was, the wind that blew me from the detest 
shore of ireland, and the sea which surround me, told me too forcibly 
that i was deceive by no vision and that clerval, my friend and dearest 
companion, had fallen a victim to me and the monster of my creation. i 
repassed, in my memory, my whole life; my quiet happiness while reside 
with my family in geneva, the death of my mother, and my departure for 
ingolstadt. i remembered, shuddering, the mad enthusiasm that hurried me on 
to the creation of my hideous enemy, and i call to mind the night in 
which he first lived. i was unable to pursue the train of thought; a 
thousand feelings pressed upon me, and i wept bitterly. 

ever since my recovery from the fever, i had been in the custom of taking 
every night a small quantity of laudanum, for it was by means of this drug 
only that i was enable to gain the rest necessary for the preservation of 
life. oppress by the recollection of my various misfortunes, i now 
swallow double my usual quantity and soon slept profoundly. but sleep did 
not afford me respite from thought and misery; my dream present a 
thousand object that scared me. towards morning i was possessed by a kind 
of nightmare; i felt the fiend’s grasp in my neck and could not free 
myself from it; groan and cries rang in my ears. my father, who was 
watch over me, perceive my restlessness, awoke me; the dashing wave 
were around, the cloudy sky above, the fiend was not here: a sense of 
security, a feeling that a truce was establish between the present hour 
and the irresistible, disastrous future impart to me a kind of calm 
forgetfulness, of which the human mind is by its structure peculiarly 
susceptible. 





chapter 22 

the voyage came to an end. we landed, and proceed to paris. i soon 
found that i had overtax my strength and that i must repose before i 
could continue my journey. my father’s care and attention were 
indefatigable, but he did not know the origin of my suffering and 
sought erroneous method to remedy the incurable ill. he wish me to 
seek amusement in society. i abhor the face of man. oh, not 
abhorred! they were my brethren, my fellow beings, and i felt 
attract even to the most repulsive among them, as to creature of an 
angel nature and celestial mechanism. but i felt that i had no right 
to share their intercourse. i had unchained an enemy among them whose 
joy it was to shed their blood and to revel in their groans. how they 
would, each and all, abhor me and hunt me from the world, did they know 
my unhallowed acts and the crime which had their source in me! 

my father yield at length to my desire to avoid society and strove by 
various argument to banish my despair. sometimes he thought that i 
felt deep the degrade of being oblige to answer a charge of 
murder, and he endeavour to prove to me the futility of pride. 

“alas! my father,” said i, “how little do you know me. 
human beings, their feelings and passions, would indeed be degrade if such 
a wretch as i felt pride. justine, poor unhappy justine, was as innocent 
as i, and she suffer the same charge; she die for it; and i am the cause 
of this—i murder her. william, justine, and henry—they all 
die by my hands.” 

my father had often, during my imprisonment, heard me make the same 
assertion; when i thus accused myself, he sometimes seem to desire an 
explanation, and at other he appear to consider it as the offspring of 
delirium, and that, during my illness, some idea of this kind had present 
itself to my imagination, the remembrance of which i preserve in my 
convalescence. i avoid explanation and maintain a continual silence 
concerning the wretch i had created. i had a persuasion that i should be 
supposed mad, and this in itself would for ever have chain my tongue. but, 
besides, i could not bring myself to disclose a secret which would fill my 
hearer with consternation and make fear and unnatural horror the inmate of 
his breast. i checked, therefore, my impatient thirst for sympathy and was 
silent when i would have given the world to have confide the fatal secret. 
yet, still, words like those i have record would burst uncontrollable 
from me. i could offer no explanation of them, but their truth in part 
relieved the burden of my mysterious woe. 

upon this occasion my father said, with an expression of unbounded wonder, 
“my dearest victor, what infatuation is this? my dear son, i entreat 
you never to make such an assertion again.” 

“i am not mad,” i cry energetically; “the sun and the heavens, who 
have view my operations, can bear witness of my truth. i am the 
assassin of those most innocent victims; they die by my machinations. 
a thousand times would i have shed my own blood, drop by drop, to have 
save their lives; but i could not, my father, indeed i could not 
sacrifice the whole human race.” 

the conclusion of this speech convinced my father that my idea were 
deranged, and he instantly change the subject of our conversation and 
endeavour to alter the course of my thoughts. he wish as much as 
possible to obliterate the memory of the scene that had taken place in 
ireland and never allude to them or suffer me to speak of my 
misfortunes. 

as time pass away i became more calm; misery had her dwelling in my 
heart, but i no long talk in the same incoherent manner of my own 
crimes; sufficient for me was the consciousness of them. by the utmost 
self-violence i curb the imperious voice of wretchedness, which 
sometimes desire to declare itself to the whole world, and my manners 
were calm and more compose than they had ever been since my journey 
to the sea of ice. 

a few days before we left paris on our way to switzerland, i received the 
following letter from elizabeth: 

“my dear friend, 

“it gave me the greatest pleasure to receive a letter from my uncle 
dated at paris; you are no long at a formidable distance, and i may 
hope to see you in less than a fortnight. my poor cousin, how much you 
must have suffered! i expect to see you look even more ill than 
when you quit geneva. this winter has been pass most miserably, 
torture as i have been by anxious suspense; yet i hope to see peace in 
your countenance and to find that your heart is not total void of 
comfort and tranquillity. 

“yet i fear that the same feelings now exist that made you so miserable 
a year ago, even perhaps augment by time. i would not disturb you at 
this period, when so many misfortune weigh upon you, but a 
conversation that i had with my uncle previous to his departure render 
some explanation necessary before we meet. 

explanation! you may possibly say, what can elizabeth have to explain? if 
you really say this, my question are answer and all my doubt satisfied. 
but you are distant from me, and it is possible that you may dread and yet 
be pleased with this explanation; and in a probability of this being the 
case, i dare not any long postpone writing what, during your absence, i 
have often wish to express to you but have never had the courage to begin. 

“you well know, victor, that our union had been the favourite plan of 
your parent ever since our infancy. we were told this when young, and 
taught to look forward to it as an event that would certainly take 
place. we were affectionate playfellow during childhood, and, i 
believe, dear and value friends to one another as we grew older. but 
as brother and sister often entertain a lively affection towards each 
other without desire a more intimate union, may not such also be our 
case? tell me, dearest victor. answer me, i conjure you by our mutual 
happiness, with simple truth—do you not love another? 

“you have travelled; you have spent several years of your life at 
ingolstadt; and i confess to you, my friend, that when i saw you last 
autumn so unhappy, flying to solitude from the society of every 
creature, i could not help supposing that you might regret our 
connection and believe yourself bound in honour to fulfil the wish of 
your parents, although they oppose themselves to your inclinations. 
but this is false reasoning. i confess to you, my friend, that i love 
you and that in my airy dream of futurity you have been my constant 
friend and companion. but it is your happiness i desire as well as my 
own when i declare to you that our marriage would render me eternal 
miserable unless it were the dictate of your own free choice. even now 
i weep to think that, borne down as you are by the cruellest 
misfortunes, you may stifle, by the word _honour_, all hope of that 
love and happiness which would alone restore you to yourself. i, who 
have so disinterested an affection for you, may increase your misery 
tenfold by being an obstacle to your wishes. ah! victor, be assured 
that your cousin and playmate has too sincere a love for you not to be 
made miserable by this supposition. be happy, my friend; and if you 
obey me in this one request, remain satisfy that nothing on earth 
will have the power to interrupt my tranquillity. 

“do not let this letter disturb you; do not answer tomorrow, or the 
next day, or even until you come, if it will give you pain. my uncle 
will send me news of your health, and if i see but one smile on your 
lip when we meet, occasion by this or any other exertion of mine, i 
shall need no other happiness. 

“elizabeth lavenza. 



“geneva, may 18th, 17—” 



this letter revive in my memory what i had before forgotten, the threat of 
the fiend—“_i will be with you on your 
wedding-night!_” such was my sentence, and on that night would the 
dæmon employ every art to destroy me and tear me from the glimpse of 
happiness which promise partly to console my sufferings. on that night he 
had determined to consummate his crime by my death. well, be it so; a 
deadly struggle would then assured take place, in which if he were 
victorious i should be at peace and his power over me be at an end. if he 
were vanquished, i should be a free man. alas! what freedom? such as the 
peasant enjoy when his family have been massacre before his eyes, his 
cottage burnt, his lands laid waste, and he is turn adrift, homeless, 
penniless, and alone, but free. such would be my liberty except that in my 
elizabeth i possessed a treasure, alas, balanced by those horrors of 
remorse and guilt which would pursue me until death. 

sweet and beloved elizabeth! i read and reread her letter, and some 
soften feelings stole into my heart and dare to whisper paradisiacal 
dream of love and joy; but the apple was already eaten, and the 
angel’s arm bare to drive me from all hope. yet i would die to make 
her happy. if the monster execute his threat, death was inevitable; yet, 
again, i considered whether my marriage would hasten my fate. my 
destruction might indeed arrive a few month sooner, but if my torture 
should suspect that i postpone it, influence by his menaces, he would 
surely find other and perhaps more dreadful means of revenge. he had vow 
_to be with me on my wedding-night_, yet he did not consider that 
threat as binding him to peace in the meantime, for as if to show me that 
he was not yet satiate with blood, he had murder clerval immediately 
after the enunciation of his threats. i resolved, therefore, that if my 
immediate union with my cousin would conduce either to hers or my 
father’s happiness, my adversary’s designs against my life 
should not retard it a single hour. 

in this state of mind i wrote to elizabeth. my letter was calm and 
affectionate. “i fear, my beloved girl,” i said, “little happiness 
remains for us on earth; yet all that i may one day enjoy is centre in 
you. chase away your idle fears; to you alone do i consecrate my life 
and my endeavour for contentment. i have one secret, elizabeth, a 
dreadful one; when reveal to you, it will chill your frame with 
horror, and then, far from being surprise at my misery, you will only 
wonder that i survive what i have endured. i will confide this tale of 
misery and terror to you the day after our marriage shall take place, 
for, my sweet cousin, there must be perfect confidence between us. but 
until then, i conjure you, do not mention or allude to it. this i most 
earnest entreat, and i know you will comply.” 

in about a week after the arrival of elizabeth’s letter we return 
to geneva. the sweet girl welcome me with warm affection, yet tear were 
in her eyes as she beheld my emaciate frame and feverish cheeks. i saw a 
change in her also. she was thinner and had lost much of that heavenly 
vivace that had before charm me; but her gentle and soft looks of 
compassion made her a more fit companion for one blasted and miserable as i 
was. 

the tranquill which i now enjoy did not endure. memory brought madness 
with it, and when i thought of what had passed, a real insanity possessed 
me; sometimes i was furious and burnt with rage, sometimes low and 
despondent. i neither spoke nor look at anyone, but sat motionless, 
bewilder by the multitude of misery that overcame me. 

elizabeth alone had the power to draw me from these fits; her gentle voice 
would soothe me when transport by passion and inspire me with human 
feelings when sunk in torpor. she wept with me and for me. when reason 
returned, she would remonstrate and endeavour to inspire me with 
resignation. ah! it is well for the unfortunate to be resigned, but for the 
guilty there is no peace. the agony of remorse poison the luxury there is 
otherwise sometimes found in indulge the excess of grief. 

soon after my arrival my father spoke of my immediate marriage with 
elizabeth. i remain silent. 

“have you, then, some other attachment?” 

“none on earth. i love elizabeth and look forward to our union with 
delight. let the day therefore be fixed; and on it i will consecrate 
myself, in life or death, to the happiness of my cousin.” 

“my dear victor, do not speak thus. heavy misfortune have befallen 
us, but let us only cling close to what remains and transfer our love 
for those whom we have lost to those who yet live. our circle will be 
small but bound close by the tie of affection and mutual misfortune. 
and when time shall have soften your despair, new and dear object of 
care will be born to replace those of whom we have been so cruel 
deprived.” 

such were the lesson of my father. but to me the remembrance of the 
threat returned; nor can you wonder that, omnipotent as the fiend had 
yet been in his deed of blood, i should almost regard him as 
invincible, and that when he had pronounced the words “_i shall be with 
you on your wedding-night_,” i should regard the threaten fate as 
unavoidable. but death was no evil to me if the loss of elizabeth were 
balanced with it, and i therefore, with a contented and even cheerful 
countenance, agree with my father that if my cousin would consent, the 
ceremony should take place in ten days, and thus put, as i imagined, 
the seal to my fate. 

great god! if for one instant i had thought what might be the hellish 
intention of my fiendish adversary, i would rather have banish myself 
for ever from my native country and wander a friendless outcast over 
the earth than have consent to this miserable marriage. but, as if 
possessed of magic powers, the monster had blind me to his real 
intentions; and when i thought that i had prepared only my own death, i 
hasten that of a far dear victim. 

as the period fixed for our marriage drew nearer, whether from cowardice or 
a prophetic feeling, i felt my heart sink within me. but i conceal my 
feelings by an appearance of hilarity that brought smile and joy to the 
countenance of my father, but hardly deceive the ever-watchful and nice 
eye of elizabeth. she look forward to our union with placid contentment, 
not unmingled with a little fear, which past misfortune had impressed, 
that what now appear certain and tangible happiness might soon dissipate 
into an airy dream and leave no trace but deep and everlasting regret. 

preparation were made for the event, congratulatory visit were received, 
and all wore a smile appearance. i shut up, as well as i could, in my own 
heart the anxiety that prey there and enter with seeming earnest 
into the plan of my father, although they might only serve as the 
decoration of my tragedy. through my father’s exertion a part of 
the inheritance of elizabeth had been restore to her by the austria 
government. a small possession on the shore of como belong to her. it 
was agree that, immediately after our union, we should proceed to villa 
lavenza and spend our first days of happiness beside the beautiful lake 
near which it stood. 

in the meantime i took every precaution to defend my person in case the 
fiend should openly attack me. i carry pistol and a dagger 
constant about me and was ever on the watch to prevent artifice, and 
by these means gain a greater degree of tranquillity. indeed, as the 
period approached, the threat appear more as a delusion, not to be 
regard as worthy to disturb my peace, while the happiness i hope for 
in my marriage wore a greater appearance of certainty as the day fixed 
for its solemnise drew near and i heard it continual spoken of 
as an occurrence which no accident could possibly prevent. 

elizabeth seem happy; my tranquil demeanour contribute greatly to 
calm her mind. but on the day that was to fulfil my wish and my 
destiny, she was melancholy, and a presentiment of evil pervade her; 
and perhaps also she thought of the dreadful secret which i had 
promise to reveal to her on the following day. my father was in the 
meantime overjoyed, and, in the bustle of preparation, only recognise in 
the melancholy of his niece the diffidence of a bride. 

after the ceremony was perform a large party assemble at my 
father’s, but it was agree that elizabeth and i should commence our 
journey by water, sleep that night at evian and continue our 
voyage on the following day. the day was fair, the wind favourable; 
all smile on our nuptial embarkation. 

those were the last moments of my life during which i enjoy the 
feeling of happiness. we pass rapid along; the sun was hot, but we 
were sheltered from its ray by a kind of canopy while we enjoy the 
beauty of the scene, sometimes on one side of the lake, where we saw 
mont salêve, the pleasant bank of montalègre, and at a distance, 
surmount all, the beautiful mont blanc, and the assemblage of snowy 
mountain that in vain endeavour to emulate her; sometimes coast the 
opposite banks, we saw the mighty jura oppose its dark side to the 
ambition that would quit its native country, and an almost 
insurmountable barrier to the invade who should wish to enslave it. 

i took the hand of elizabeth. “you are sorrowful, my love. ah! if 
you knew what i have suffer and what i may yet endure, you would 
endeavour to let me taste the quiet and freedom from despair that this 
one day at least permit me to enjoy.” 

“be happy, my dear victor,” reply elizabeth; “there is, i hope, 
nothing to distress you; and be assured that if a lively joy is not 
paint in my face, my heart is contented. something whisper to me 
not to depend too much on the prospect that is open before us, but i 
will not listen to such a sinister voice. observe how fast we move 
along and how the clouds, which sometimes obscure and sometimes rise 
above the dome of mont blanc, render this scene of beauty still more 
interesting. look also at the innumerable fish that are swimming in 
the clear waters, where we can distinguish every pebble that lie at 
the bottom. what a divine day! how happy and serene all nature 
appears!” 

thus elizabeth endeavour to divert her thought and mine from all 
reflection upon melancholy subjects. but her temper was fluctuating; 
joy for a few instant shone in her eyes, but it continual gave place 
to distraction and reverie. 

the sun sank lower in the heavens; we pass the river drance and 
observe its path through the chasm of the higher and the glen of the 
lower hills. the alps here come close to the lake, and we approach 
the amphitheatre of mountain which form its eastern boundary. the 
spire of evian shone under the wood that surround it and the range 
of mountain above mountain by which it was overhung. 

the wind, which had hitherto carry us along with amazing rapidity, 
sank at sunset to a light breeze; the soft air just ruffle the water 
and cause a pleasant motion among the tree as we approach the 
shore, from which it waft the most delightful scent of flower and 
hay. the sun sank beneath the horizon as we landed, and as i touched 
the shore i felt those care and fear revive which soon were to clasp 
me and cling to me for ever. 





chapter 23 

it was eight o’clock when we landed; we walk for a short time on the 
shore, enjoy the transitory light, and then retired to the inn and 
contemplate the lovely scene of waters, woods, and mountains, obscure 
in darkness, yet still display their black outlines. 

the wind, which had fallen in the south, now rose with great violence 
in the west. the moon had reach her summit in the heaven and was 
beginning to descend; the cloud swept across it swift than the 
flight of the vulture and dim her rays, while the lake reflect the 
scene of the busy heavens, render still busy by the restless wave 
that were beginning to rise. sudden a heavy storm of rain descended. 

i had been calm during the day, but so soon as night obscure the 
shape of objects, a thousand fear arose in my mind. i was anxious 
and watchful, while my right hand grasp a pistol which was hidden in 
my bosom; every sound terrify me, but i resolve that i would sell my 
life dearly and not shrink from the conflict until my own life or that 
of my adversary was extinguished. 

elizabeth observe my agitation for some time in timid and fearful silence, 
but there was something in my glance which communicate terror to her, and 
trembling, she asked, “what is it that agitate you, my dear victor? 
what is it you fear?” 

“oh! peace, peace, my love,” reply i; “this night, and 
all will be safe; but this night is dreadful, very dreadful.” 

i pass an hour in this state of mind, when sudden i reflect how 
fearful the combat which i momentarily expect would be to my wife, 
and i earnest entreat her to retire, resolve not to join her 
until i had obtain some knowledge as to the situation of my enemy. 

she left me, and i continue some time walking up and down the passage 
of the house and inspect every corner that might afford a retreat to 
my adversary. but i discover no trace of him and was beginning to 
conjecture that some fortunate chance had intervene to prevent the 
execution of his menace when sudden i heard a shrill and dreadful 
scream. it came from the room into which elizabeth had retired. as i 
heard it, the whole truth rush into my mind, my arms dropped, the 
motion of every muscle and fibre was suspended; i could feel the blood 
trickle in my vein and tingle in the extremities of my limbs. this 
state last but for an instant; the scream was repeated, and i rush 
into the room. 

great god! why did i not then expire! why am i here to relate the 
destruction of the best hope and the purest creature on earth? she was 
there, lifeless and inanimate, thrown across the bed, her head hanging down 
and her pale and distort features half cover by her hair. everywhere i 
turn i see the same figure—her bloodless arms and relax form flung 
by the murder on its bridal bier. could i behold this and live? alas! 
life is obstinate and cling closest where it is most hated. for a moment 
only did i lose recollection; i fell senseless on the ground. 

when i recover i found myself surround by the people of the inn; their 
countenance express a breathless terror, but the horror of other 
appear only as a mockery, a shadow of the feelings that oppress me. i 
escape from them to the room where lay the body of elizabeth, my love, my 
wife, so lately living, so dear, so worthy. she had been move from the 
posture in which i had first beheld her, and now, as she lay, her head upon 
her arm and a handkerchief thrown across her face and neck, i might have 
supposed her asleep. i rush towards her and embrace her with ardour, but 
the deadly languor and cold of the limb told me that what i now held 
in my arms had cease to be the elizabeth whom i had love and cherished. 
the murderous mark of the fiend’s grasp was on her neck, and the 
breath had cease to issue from her lips. 

while i still hung over her in the agony of despair, i happen to look up. 
the window of the room had before been darkened, and i felt a kind of 
panic on seeing the pale yellow light of the moon illuminate the chamber. 
the shutter had been thrown back, and with a sensation of horror not to be 
described, i saw at the open window a figure the most hideous and abhorred. 
a grin was on the face of the monster; he seem to jeer, as with his 
fiendish finger he pointed towards the corpse of my wife. i rush towards 
the window, and drawing a pistol from my bosom, fired; but he elude me, 
leap from his station, and running with the swift of lightning, 
plunge into the lake. 

the report of the pistol brought a crowd into the room. i pointed to 
the spot where he had disappeared, and we follow the track with 
boats; nets were cast, but in vain. after passing several hours, we 
return hopeless, most of my companion believe it to have been a 
form conjure up by my fancy. after have landed, they proceed to 
search the country, party go in different directions among the 
wood and vines. 

i attempt to accompany them and proceed a short distance from the 
house, but my head whirl round, my steps were like those of a drunken 
man, i fell at last in a state of utter exhaustion; a film cover my 
eyes, and my skin was parch with the heat of fever. in this state i 
was carry back and placed on a bed, hardly conscious of what had 
happened; my eyes wander round the room as if to seek something that 
i had lost. 

after an interval i arose, and as if by instinct, crawl into the room 
where the corpse of my beloved lay. there were women weeping around; i 
hung over it and join my sad tear to theirs; all this time no 
distinct idea present itself to my mind, but my thought ramble to 
various subjects, reflect confused on my misfortune and their 
cause. i was bewildered, in a cloud of wonder and horror. the death 
of william, the execution of justine, the murder of clerval, and lastly 
of my wife; even at that moment i knew not that my only remain 
friends were safe from the malignity of the fiend; my father even now 
might be writhe under his grasp, and ernest might be dead at his 
feet. this idea made me shudder and recall me to action. i start 
up and resolve to return to geneva with all possible speed. 

there were no horse to be procured, and i must return by the lake; but the 
wind was unfavourable, and the rain fell in torrents. however, it was 
hardly morning, and i might reasonably hope to arrive by night. i hire men 
to row and took an oar myself, for i had always experienced relief from 
mental torment in bodily exercise. but the overflow misery i now felt, 
and the excess of agitation that i endure render me incapable of any 
exertion. i threw down the oar, and leaning my head upon my hands, gave way 
to every gloomy idea that arose. if i look up, i saw scene which were 
familiar to me in my happy time and which i had contemplate but the day 
before in the company of her who was now but a shadow and a recollection. 
tear stream from my eyes. the rain had cease for a moment, and i saw 
the fish play in the waters as they had done a few hour before; they had 
then been observe by elizabeth. nothing is so painful to the human mind as 
a great and sudden change. the sun might shine or the cloud might lower, 
but nothing could appear to me as it had done the day before. a fiend had 
snatch from me every hope of future happiness; no creature had ever been 
so miserable as i was; so frightful an event is single in the history of 
man. 

but why should i dwell upon the incident that follow this last 
overwhelming event? mine has been a tale of horrors; i have reach their 
_acme_, and what i must now relate can but be tedious to you. know 
that, one by one, my friends were snatch away; i was left desolate. my 
own strength is exhausted, and i must tell, in a few words, what remains of 
my hideous narration. 

i arrive at geneva. my father and ernest yet lived, but the former sunk 
under the tidings that i bore. i see him now, excellent and venerable old 
man! his eyes wander in vacancy, for they had lost their charm and their 
delight—his elizabeth, his more than daughter, whom he dote on with 
all that affection which a man feels, who in the decline of life, have 
few affections, cling more earnest to those that remain. cursed, cursed 
be the fiend that brought misery on his grey hair and doom him to waste 
in wretchedness! he could not live under the horrors that were accumulate 
around him; the spring of existence sudden gave way; he was unable to 
rise from his bed, and in a few days he die in my arms. 

what then became of me? i know not; i lost sensation, and chain and 
dark were the only object that pressed upon me. sometimes, 
indeed, i dreamt that i wander in flowery meadow and pleasant vale 
with the friends of my youth, but i awoke and found myself in a 
dungeon. melancholy followed, but by degree i gain a clear 
conception of my misery and situation and was then release from my 
prison. for they had call me mad, and during many months, as i 
understood, a solitary cell had been my habitation. 

liberty, however, had been a useless gift to me, had i not, as i 
awaken to reason, at the same time awaken to revenge. as the 
memory of past misfortune pressed upon me, i began to reflect on their 
cause—the monster whom i had created, the miserable dæmon whom i had 
sent abroad into the world for my destruction. i was possessed by a 
maddening rage when i thought of him, and desire and ardent pray 
that i might have him within my grasp to wreak a great and signal 
revenge on his cursed head. 

nor did my hate long confine itself to useless wishes; i began to 
reflect on the best means of secure him; and for this purpose, about 
a month after my release, i repair to a criminal judge in the town 
and told him that i had an accusation to make, that i knew the 
destroyer of my family, and that i require him to exert his whole 
authority for the apprehension of the murderer. 

the magistrate listen to me with attention and kindness. “be 
assured, sir,” said he, “no pains or exertion on my part shall 
be spare to discover the villain.” 

“i thank you,” reply i; “listen, therefore, to the 
deposition that i have to make. it is indeed a tale so strange that i 
should fear you would not credit it were there not something in truth 
which, however wonderful, forces conviction. the story is too connected to 
be mistaken for a dream, and i have no motive for falsehood.” my 
manner as i thus address him was impressive but calm; i had form in my 
own heart a resolution to pursue my destroyer to death, and this purpose 
quiet my agony and for an interval reconcile me to life. i now related 
my history brief but with firm and precision, marking the dates with 
accuracy and never deviate into invective or exclamation. 

the magistrate appear at first perfectly incredulous, but as i continue 
he became more attentive and interested; i saw him sometimes shudder with 
horror; at other a lively surprise, unmingled with disbelief, was paint 
on his countenance. 

when i had conclude my narration, i said, “this is the being whom i 
accuse and for whose seizure and punishment i call upon you to exert your 
whole power. it is your duty as a magistrate, and i believe and hope that 
your feelings as a man will not revolt from the execution of those 
function on this occasion.” 

this address cause a considerable change in the physiognomy of my own 
auditor. he had heard my story with that half kind of belief that is given 
to a tale of spirits and supernatural events; but when he was call upon 
to act officially in consequence, the whole tide of his incredulity 
returned. he, however, answer mildly, “i would willing afford you 
every aid in your pursuit, but the creature of whom you speak appear to 
have powers which would put all my exertion to defiance. who can follow an 
animal which can traverse the sea of ice and inhabit cave and den where 
no man would venture to intrude? besides, some month have elapse since 
the commission of his crimes, and no one can conjecture to what place he 
has wander or what region he may now inhabit.” 

“i do not doubt that he hover near the spot which i inhabit, and if 
he has indeed taken refuge in the alps, he may be hunt like the chamois 
and destroy as a beast of prey. but i perceive your thoughts; you do not 
credit my narrative and do not intend to pursue my enemy with the 
punishment which is his desert.” 

as i spoke, rage sparkle in my eyes; the magistrate was intimidated. 
“you are mistaken,” said he. “i will exert myself, and if 
it is in my power to seize the monster, be assured that he shall suffer 
punishment proportionate to his crimes. but i fear, from what you have 
yourself describe to be his properties, that this will prove 
impracticable; and thus, while every proper measure is pursued, you should 
make up your mind to disappointment.” 

“that cannot be; but all that i can say will be of little avail. my 
revenge is of no moment to you; yet, while i allow it to be a vice, i 
confess that it is the devour and only passion of my soul. my rage 
is unspeakable when i reflect that the murderer, whom i have turn 
loose upon society, still exists. you refuse my just demand; i have 
but one resource, and i devote myself, either in my life or death, to 
his destruction.” 

i tremble with excess of agitation as i said this; there was a frenzy 
in my manner, and something, i doubt not, of that haughty fierce 
which the martyr of old are said to have possessed. but to a genevan 
magistrate, whose mind was occupy by far other idea than those of 
devotion and heroism, this elevation of mind had much the appearance of 
madness. he endeavour to soothe me as a nurse do a child and 
revert to my tale as the effects of delirium. 

“man,” i cried, “how ignorant art thou in thy pride of 
wisdom! cease; you know not what it is you say.” 

i broke from the house angry and disturbed and retired to meditate on 
some other mode of action. 





chapter 24 

my present situation was one in which all voluntary thought was 
swallow up and lost. i was hurried away by fury; revenge alone 
endow me with strength and composure; it mould my feelings and 
allow me to be calculating and calm at periods when otherwise 
delirium or death would have been my portion. 

my first resolution was to quit geneva for ever; my country, which, when i 
was happy and beloved, was dear to me, now, in my adversity, became 
hateful. i provided myself with a sum of money, together with a few jewel 
which had belong to my mother, and departed. 

and now my wanderings began which are to cease but with life. i have 
traverse a vast portion of the earth and have endure all the hardship 
which traveller in deserts and barbarous country are wont to meet. how i 
have live i hardly know; many times have i stretch my failing limb upon 
the sandy plain and pray for death. but revenge kept me alive; i dare 
not die and leave my adversary in being. 

when i quit geneva my first labour was to gain some clue by which i 
might trace the steps of my fiendish enemy. but my plan was unsettled, 
and i wander many hour round the confines of the town, uncertain 
what path i should pursue. as night approach i found myself at the 
entrance of the cemetery where william, elizabeth, and my father 
reposed. i enter it and approach the tomb which marked their 
graves. everything was silent except the leaves of the trees, which 
were gently agitate by the wind; the night was nearly dark, and the 
scene would have been solemn and affecting even to an uninterested 
observer. the spirits of the departed seem to flit around and to 
cast a shadow, which was felt but not seen, around the head of the 
mourner. 

the deep grief which this scene had at first excited quick gave way to 
rage and despair. they were dead, and i lived; their murder also lived, 
and to destroy him i must drag out my weary existence. i knelt on the grass 
and kiss the earth and with quiver lip exclaimed, “by the 
sacred earth on which i kneel, by the shades that wander near me, by the 
deep and eternal grief that i feel, i swear; and by thee, o night, and the 
spirits that preside over thee, to pursue the dæmon who cause this misery, 
until he or i shall perish in mortal conflict. for this purpose i will 
preserve my life; to execute this dear revenge will i again behold the sun 
and tread the green herbage of earth, which otherwise should vanish from my 
eyes for ever. and i call on you, spirits of the dead, and on you, wandering 
minister of vengeance, to aid and conduct me in my work. let the cursed 
and hellish monster drink deep of agony; let him feel the despair that now 
torment me.” 

i had begun my adjure with solemnity and an awe which almost assured me 
that the shades of my murder friends heard and approve my devotion, but 
the fury possessed me as i concluded, and rage choke my utterance. 

i was answer through the still of night by a loud and fiendish 
laugh. it rang on my ear long and heavily; the mountain re-echoed 
it, and i felt as if all hell surround me with mockery and laughter. 
surely in that moment i should have been possessed by frenzy and have 
destroy my miserable existence but that my vow was heard and that i 
was reserved for vengeance. the laughter die away, when a well-known 
and abhor voice, apparently close to my ear, address me in an 
audible whisper, “i am satisfied, miserable wretch! you have 
determined to live, and i am satisfied.” 

i dart towards the spot from which the sound proceeded, but the devil 
elude my grasp. sudden the broad disk of the moon arose and shone 
full upon his ghastly and distort shape as he flee with more than 
mortal speed. 

i pursue him, and for many month this has been my task. guide by a 
slight clue, i follow the winding of the rhone, but vainly. the 
blue mediterranean appeared, and by a strange chance, i saw the fiend 
enter by night and hide himself in a vessel bound for the black sea. i 
took my passage in the same ship, but he escaped, i know not how. 

amidst the wild of tartary and russia, although he still evade me, i 
have ever follow in his track. sometimes the peasants, scared by 
this horrid apparition, informed me of his path; sometimes he himself, 
who fear that if i lost all trace of him i should despair and die, 
left some mark to guide me. the snow descended on my head, and i saw 
the print of his huge step on the white plain. to you first enter 
on life, to whom care is new and agony unknown, how can you understand 
what i have felt and still feel? cold, want, and fatigue were the 
least pains which i was destined to endure; i was cursed by some devil 
and carry about with me my eternal hell; yet still a spirit of good 
follow and direct my steps and when i most murmur would sudden 
extricate me from seemingly insurmountable difficulties. sometimes, 
when nature, overcome by hunger, sank under the exhaustion, a repast 
was prepared for me in the desert that restore and inspirite me. the 
fare was, indeed, coarse, such as the peasant of the country ate, but 
i will not doubt that it was set there by the spirits that i had 
invoke to aid me. often, when all was dry, the heaven cloudless, and 
i was parch by thirst, a slight cloud would bedim the sky, shed the 
few drops that revive me, and vanish. 

i followed, when i could, the course of the rivers; but the dæmon 
generally avoid these, as it was here that the population of the 
country chiefly collected. in other place human being were seldom 
seen, and i generally subsist on the wild animal that cross my 
path. i had money with me and gain the friendship of the villager 
by distribute it; or i brought with me some food that i had killed, 
which, after taking a small part, i always present to those who had 
provided me with fire and utensil for cooking. 

my life, as it pass thus, was indeed hateful to me, and it was during 
sleep alone that i could taste joy. o blessed sleep! often, when most 
miserable, i sank to repose, and my dream lull me even to rapture. the 
spirits that guarded me had provided these moments, or rather hours, of 
happiness that i might retain strength to fulfil my pilgrimage. deprived of 
this respite, i should have sunk under my hardships. during the day i was 
sustain and inspirite by the hope of night, for in sleep i saw my 
friends, my wife, and my beloved country; again i saw the benevolent 
countenance of my father, heard the silver tone of my elizabeth’s 
voice, and beheld clerval enjoy health and youth. often, when weary by 
a toilsome march, i persuade myself that i was dream until night should 
come and that i should then enjoy reality in the arms of my dearest 
friends. what agonising fond did i feel for them! how did i cling to 
their dear forms, as sometimes they haunt even my waking hours, and 
persuade myself that they still lived! at such moments vengeance, that 
burn within me, die in my heart, and i pursue my path towards the 
destruction of the dæmon more as a task enjoin by heaven, as the 
mechanical impulse of some power of which i was unconscious, than as the 
ardent desire of my soul. 

what his feelings were whom i pursue i cannot know. sometimes, indeed, he 
left mark in writing on the bark of the tree or cut in stone that guide 
me and instigate my fury. “my reign is not yet 
over”—these words were legible in one of these 
inscriptions—“you live, and my power is complete. follow me; i 
seek the everlasting ice of the north, where you will feel the misery of 
cold and frost, to which i am impassive. you will find near this place, if 
you follow not too tardily, a dead hare; eat and be refreshed. come on, my 
enemy; we have yet to wrestle for our lives, but many hard and miserable 
hour must you endure until that period shall arrive.” 

scoff devil! again do i vow vengeance; again do i devote thee, 
miserable fiend, to torture and death. never will i give up my search 
until he or i perish; and then with what ecstasy shall i join my 
elizabeth and my departed friends, who even now prepare for me the 
reward of my tedious toil and horrible pilgrimage! 

as i still pursue my journey to the northward, the snow thicken and the 
cold increase in a degree almost too severe to support. the peasant were 
shut up in their hovels, and only a few of the most hardy venture forth to 
seize the animal whom starvation had forced from their hiding-places to 
seek for prey. the river were cover with ice, and no fish could be 
procured; and thus i was cut off from my chief article of maintenance. 

the triumph of my enemy increase with the difficulty of my labours. one 
inscription that he left was in these words: “prepare! your toils 
only begin; wrap yourself in fur and provide food, for we shall soon enter 
upon a journey where your suffering will satisfy my everlasting 
hatred.” 

my courage and perseverance were invigorate by these scoff words; i 
resolve not to fail in my purpose, and calling on heaven to support 
me, i continue with unabated fervour to traverse immense deserts, 
until the ocean appear at a distance and form the utmost boundary 
of the horizon. oh! how unlike it was to the blue season of the 
south! cover with ice, it was only to be distinguished from land by 
its superior wild and ruggedness. the greece wept for joy when 
they beheld the mediterranean from the hill of asia, and hail with 
rapture the boundary of their toils. i did not weep, but i knelt down 
and with a full heart thank my guide spirit for conduct me in 
safety to the place where i hoped, notwithstanding my adversary’s gibe, 
to meet and grapple with him. 

some week before this period i had procure a sledge and dogs and thus 
traverse the snow with inconceivable speed. i know not whether the 
fiend possessed the same advantages, but i found that, as before i had 
daily lost ground in the pursuit, i now gain on him, so much so that 
when i first saw the ocean he was but one day’s journey in advance, and 
i hope to intercept him before he should reach the beach. with new 
courage, therefore, i pressed on, and in two days arrive at a wretched 
hamlet on the seashore. i inquire of the inhabitant concerning the 
fiend and gain accurate information. a gigantic monster, they said, 
had arrive the night before, armed with a gun and many pistols, 
putt to flight the inhabitant of a solitary cottage through fear of 
his terrific appearance. he had carry off their store of winter 
food, and place it in a sledge, to draw which he had seize on a 
numerous drove of train dogs, he had harness them, and the same 
night, to the joy of the horror-struck villagers, had pursue his 
journey across the sea in a direction that led to no land; and they 
conjecture that he must speedy be destroy by the break of the 
ice or frozen by the eternal frosts. 

on hearing this information i suffer a temporary access of despair. 
he had escape me, and i must commence a destructive and almost endless 
journey across the mountainous ice of the ocean, amidst cold that few 
of the inhabitant could long endure and which i, the native of a 
genial and sunny climate, could not hope to survive. yet at the idea 
that the fiend should live and be triumphant, my rage and vengeance 
returned, and like a mighty tide, overwhelm every other feeling. 
after a slight repose, during which the spirits of the dead hover 
round and instigate me to toil and revenge, i prepared for my journey. 

i exchange my land-sledge for one fashion for the inequality of 
the frozen ocean, and purchase a plentiful stock of provisions, i 
departed from land. 

i cannot guess how many days have pass since then, but i have endure 
misery which nothing but the eternal sentiment of a just retribution 
burning within my heart could have enable me to support. immense and 
rugged mountain of ice often barred up my passage, and i often heard 
the thunder of the ground sea, which threaten my destruction. but 
again the frost came and made the path of the sea secure. 

by the quantity of provision which i had consumed, i should guess that 
i had pass three week in this journey; and the continual protraction 
of hope, return back upon the heart, often wrung bitter drops of 
despondent and grief from my eyes. despair had indeed almost secure 
her prey, and i should soon have sunk beneath this misery. once, after 
the poor animal that convey me had with incredible toil gain the 
summit of a slope ice mountain, and one, sink under his fatigue, 
died, i view the expanse before me with anguish, when sudden my eye 
caught a dark speck upon the dusky plain. i strained my sight to 
discover what it could be and utter a wild cry of ecstasy when i 
distinguished a sledge and the distort proportions of a well-known 
form within. oh! with what a burning gush did hope revisit my heart! 
warm tear fill my eyes, which i hasty wipe away, that they might 
not intercept the view i had of the dæmon; but still my sight was 
dim by the burning drops, until, give way to the emotion that 
oppress me, i wept aloud. 

but this was not the time for delay; i disencumber the dogs of their 
dead companion, gave them a plentiful portion of food, and after an 
hour’s rest, which was absolutely necessary, and yet which was bitter 
irksome to me, i continue my route. the sledge was still visible, nor 
did i again lose sight of it except at the moments when for a short 
time some ice-rock conceal it with its intervene crags. i indeed 
perceptible gain on it, and when, after nearly two days’ journey, i 
beheld my enemy at no more than a mile distant, my heart bound within 
me. 

but now, when i appear almost within grasp of my foe, my hope were 
sudden extinguished, and i lost all trace of him more utterly than i had 
ever done before. a ground sea was heard; the thunder of its progress, as 
the waters roll and swell beneath me, became every moment more ominous 
and terrific. i pressed on, but in vain. the wind arose; the sea roared; 
and, as with the mighty shock of an earthquake, it split and cracked with a 
tremendous and overwhelming sound. the work was soon finished; in a few 
minutes a tumultuous sea roll between me and my enemy, and i was left 
drift on a scattered piece of ice that was continual lessen and 
thus prepare for me a hideous death. 

in this manner many appalling hour passed; several of my dogs died, and i 
myself was about to sink under the accumulation of distress when i saw your 
vessel riding at anchor and holding forth to me hope of succour and life. 
i had no conception that vessel ever came so far north and was astound 
at the sight. i quick destroy part of my sledge to construct oars, and 
by these means was enabled, with infinite fatigue, to move my ice raft in 
the direction of your ship. i had determined, if you were go southwards, 
still to trust myself to the mercy of the sea rather than abandon my 
purpose. i hope to induce you to grant me a boat with which i could pursue 
my enemy. but your direction was northwards. you took me on board when my 
vigour was exhausted, and i should soon have sunk under my multiply 
hardship into a death which i still dread, for my task is unfulfilled. 

oh! when will my guide spirit, in conduct me to the dæmon, allow 
me the rest i so much desire; or must i die, and he yet live? if i do, 
swear to me, walton, that he shall not escape, that you will seek him 
and satisfy my vengeance in his death. and do i dare to ask of you to 
undertake my pilgrimage, to endure the hardship that i have undergone? 
no; i am not so selfish. yet, when i am dead, if he should appear, if 
the minister of vengeance should conduct him to you, swear that he 
shall not live—swear that he shall not triumph over my accumulate 
woe and survive to add to the list of his dark crimes. he is eloquent 
and persuasive, and once his words had even power over my heart; but 
trust him not. his soul is as hellish as his form, full of treachery 
and fiend-like malice. hear him not; call on the name of william, 
justine, clerval, elizabeth, my father, and of the wretched victor, and 
thrust your sword into his heart. i will hover near and direct the 
steel aright. 

walton, _in continuation._ 


august 26th, 17—. 


you have read this strange and terrific story, margaret; and do you not 
feel your blood congeal with horror, like that which even now curdle 
mine? sometimes, seize with sudden agony, he could not continue his 
tale; at others, his voice broken, yet piercing, utter with 
difficulty the words so replete with anguish. his fine and lovely eyes 
were now light up with indignation, now subdued to downcast sorrow 
and quench in infinite wretchedness. sometimes he command his 
countenance and tone and related the most horrible incident with a 
tranquil voice, suppress every mark of agitation; then, like a 
volcano burst forth, his face would sudden change to an expression 
of the wildest rage as he shriek out imprecation on his persecutor. 

his tale is connected and told with an appearance of the simplest truth, 
yet i own to you that the letters of felix and safie, which he show me, 
and the apparition of the monster seen from our ship, brought to me a 
greater conviction of the truth of his narrative than his asseverations, 
however earnest and connected. such a monster has, then, really existence! 
i cannot doubt it, yet i am lost in surprise and admiration. sometimes i 
endeavour to gain from frankenstein the particulars of his 
creature’s formation, but on this point he was impenetrable. 

“are you mad, my friend?” said he. “or whither do your 
senseless curiosity lead you? would you also create for yourself and the 
world a demoniacal enemy? peace, peace! learn my misery and do not seek 
to increase your own.” 

frankenstein discover that i made note concerning his history; he ask 
to see them and then himself correct and augment them in many places, 
but principally in give the life and spirit to the conversation he held 
with his enemy. “since you have preserve my narration,” said 
he, “i would not that a mutilate one should go down to 
posterity.” 

thus has a week pass away, while i have listen to the strangest 
tale that ever imagination formed. my thought and every feeling of my 
soul have been drunk up by the interest for my guest which this tale 
and his own elevated and gentle manners have created. i wish to soothe 
him, yet can i counsel one so infinite miserable, so destitute of 
every hope of consolation, to live? oh, no! the only joy that he can 
now know will be when he compose his shatter spirit to peace and 
death. yet he enjoy one comfort, the offspring of solitude and 
delirium; he believe that when in dream he hold converse with his 
friends and derive from that communion consolation for his misery or 
excitement to his vengeance, that they are not the creation of his 
fancy, but the being themselves who visit him from the regions of a 
remote world. this faith give a solemnity to his reverie that render 
them to me almost as imposing and interesting as truth. 

our conversation are not always confine to his own history and 
misfortunes. on every point of general literature he display 
unbounded knowledge and a quick and piercing apprehension. his 
eloquence is forcible and touching; nor can i hear him, when he relate 
a pathetic incident or endeavour to move the passion of pity or love, 
without tears. what a glorious creature must he have been in the days 
of his prosperity, when he is thus noble and godlike in ruin! he seem 
to feel his own worth and the great of his fall. 

“when younger,” said he, “i believe myself destined for 
some great enterprise. my feelings are profound, but i possessed a cool 
of judgment that fitted me for illustrious achievements. this sentiment of 
the worth of my nature support me when other would have been oppressed, 
for i deem it criminal to throw away in useless grief those talent that 
might be useful to my fellow creatures. when i reflect on the work i had 
completed, no less a one than the creation of a sensitive and rational 
animal, i could not rank myself with the herd of common projectors. but 
this thought, which support me in the commencement of my career, now 
serve only to plunge me lower in the dust. all my speculation and hope 
are as nothing, and like the archangel who aspire to omnipotence, i am 
chain in an eternal hell. my imagination was vivid, yet my powers of 
analysis and application were intense; by the union of these quality i 
conceive the idea and execute the creation of a man. even now i cannot 
recollect without passion my reverie while the work was incomplete. i trod 
heaven in my thoughts, now exult in my powers, now burning with the idea 
of their effects. from my infancy i was imbue with high hope and a lofty 
ambition; but how am i sunk! oh! my friend, if you had known me as i once 
was, you would not recognise me in this state of degradation. despondent 
rarely visit my heart; a high destiny seem to bear me on, until i fell, 
never, never again to rise.” 

must i then lose this admirable being? i have long for a friend; i have 
sought one who would sympathise with and love me. behold, on these desert 
sea i have found such a one, but i fear i have gain him only to know his 
value and lose him. i would reconcile him to life, but he repulse the idea. 

“i thank you, walton,” he said, “for your kind intentions towards so 
miserable a wretch; but when you speak of new tie and fresh 
affections, think you that any can replace those who are gone? can any 
man be to me as clerval was, or any woman another elizabeth? even 
where the affection are not strong move by any superior excellence, 
the companion of our childhood always possess a certain power over our 
mind which hardly any later friend can obtain. they know our 
infantine dispositions, which, however they may be afterwards modified, 
are never eradicated; and they can judge of our action with more 
certain conclusion as to the integrity of our motives. a sister or a 
brother can never, unless indeed such symptom have been shown early, 
suspect the other of fraud or false dealing, when another friend, 
however strong he may be attached, may, in spite of himself, be 
contemplate with suspicion. but i enjoy friends, dear not only 
through habit and association, but from their own merits; and wherever 
i am, the soothe voice of my elizabeth and the conversation of 
clerval will be ever whisper in my ear. they are dead, and but one 
feeling in such a solitude can persuade me to preserve my life. if i 
were engaged in any high undertaking or design, fraught with extensive 
utility to my fellow creatures, then could i live to fulfil it. but 
such is not my destiny; i must pursue and destroy the being to whom i 
gave existence; then my lot on earth will be fulfill and i may die.” 

my beloved sister, 

september 2d. 


i write to you, encompass by peril and ignorant whether i am ever 
doom to see again dear england and the dear friends that inhabit 
it. i am surround by mountain of ice which admit of no escape and 
threaten every moment to crush my vessel. the brave fellow whom i 
have persuade to be my companion look towards me for aid, but i have 
none to bestow. there is something terribly appalling in our 
situation, yet my courage and hope do not desert me. yet it is 
terrible to reflect that the lives of all these men are endanger 
through me. if we are lost, my mad scheme are the cause. 

and what, margaret, will be the state of your mind? you will not hear of my 
destruction, and you will anxious await my return. years will pass, and 
you will have visiting of despair and yet be torture by hope. oh! my 
beloved sister, the sickening failing of your heart-felt expectations is, 
in prospect, more terrible to me than my own death. but you have a husband 
and lovely children; you may be happy. heaven bless you and make you so! 

my unfortunate guest regards me with the tenderest compassion. he 
endeavour to fill me with hope and talks as if life were a possession 
which he valued. he remind me how often the same accident have 
happen to other navigator who have attempt this sea, and in spite 
of myself, he fill me with cheerful auguries. even the sailor feel 
the power of his eloquence; when he speaks, they no long despair; he 
rouse their energies, and while they hear his voice they believe these 
vast mountain of ice are mole-hills which will vanish before the 
resolution of man. these feelings are transitory; each day of 
expectation delay fill them with fear, and i almost dread a mutiny 
cause by this despair. 

september 5th. 


a scene has just pass of such uncommon interest that, although it is 
highly probable that these papers may never reach you, yet i cannot 
forbear recording it. 

we are still surround by mountain of ice, still in imminent danger 
of being crush in their conflict. the cold is excessive, and many of 
my unfortunate comrade have already found a grave amidst this scene of 
desolation. frankenstein has daily decline in health; a feverish fire 
still glimmer in his eyes, but he is exhausted, and when sudden 
rouse to any exertion, he speedy sink again into apparent 
lifelessness. 

i mention in my last letter the fear i entertain of a mutiny. 
this morning, as i sat watch the wan countenance of my friend—his 
eyes half closed and his limb hanging listlessly—i was rouse by half 
a dozen of the sailors, who demand admission into the cabin. they 
entered, and their leader address me. he told me that he and his 
companion had been chosen by the other sailor to come in deputation 
to me to make me a requisition which, in justice, i could not refuse. 
we were immure in ice and should probably never escape, but they 
fear that if, as was possible, the ice should dissipate and a free 
passage be opened, i should be rash enough to continue my voyage and 
lead them into fresh dangers, after they might happily have surmount 
this. they insisted, therefore, that i should engage with a solemn 
promise that if the vessel should be free i would instantly direct my 
course southwards. 

this speech trouble me. i had not despaired, nor had i yet conceive 
the idea of return if set free. yet could i, in justice, or even in 
possibility, refuse this demand? i hesitate before i answered, when 
frankenstein, who had at first been silent, and indeed appear hardly 
to have force enough to attend, now rouse himself; his eyes sparkled, 
and his cheek flushed with momentary vigour. turning towards the men, 
he said, 

“what do you mean? what do you demand of your captain? are you, then, 
so easily turn from your design? did you not call this a glorious 
expedition? “and wherefore was it glorious? not because the way was 
smooth and placid as a southern sea, but because it was full of danger and 
terror, because at every new incident your fortitude was to be call forth 
and your courage exhibited, because danger and death surround it, and 
these you were to brave and overcome. for this was it a glorious, for this 
was it an honourable undertaking. you were hereafter to be hail as the 
benefactor of your species, your name adore as belong to brave men 
who encounter death for honour and the benefit of mankind. and now, 
behold, with the first imagination of danger, or, if you will, the first 
mighty and terrific trial of your courage, you shrink away and are content 
to be hand down as men who had not strength enough to endure cold and 
peril; and so, poor souls, they were chilly and return to their warm 
firesides. why, that require not this preparation; ye need not have come 
thus far and drag your captain to the shame of a defeat merely to prove 
yourself cowards. oh! be men, or be more than men. be steady to your 
purpose and firm as a rock. this ice is not made of such stuff as your 
heart may be; it is mutable and cannot withstand you if you say that it 
shall not. do not return to your family with the stigma of disgrace 
marked on your brows. return as hero who have fought and conquer and 
who know not what it is to turn their back on the foe.” 

he spoke this with a voice so modulate to the different feelings express 
in his speech, with an eye so full of lofty design and heroism, that can 
you wonder that these men were moved? they look at one another and were 
unable to reply. i spoke; i told them to retire and consider of what had 
been said, that i would not lead them farther north if they strenuous 
desire the contrary, but that i hope that, with reflection, their courage 
would return. 

they retired and i turn towards my friend, but he was sunk in languor and 
almost deprived of life. 

how all this will terminate, i know not, but i had rather die than 
return shamefully, my purpose unfulfilled. yet i fear such will be my 
fate; the men, unsupported by idea of glory and honour, can never 
willing continue to endure their present hardships. 

september 7th. 


the die is cast; i have consent to return if we are not destroyed. 
thus are my hope blasted by cowardice and indecision; i come back 
ignorant and disappointed. it require more philosophy than i possess 
to bear this injustice with patience. 

september 12th. 


it is past; i am return to england. i have lost my hope of utility 
and glory; i have lost my friend. but i will endeavour to detail these 
bitter circumstances to you, my dear sister; and while i am waft
