towards england and towards you, i will not despond. 

september 9th, the ice began to move, and roaring like thunder were heard 
at a distance as the island split and cracked in every direction. we were 
in the most imminent peril, but as we could only remain passive, my chief 
attention was occupy by my unfortunate guest whose illness increase in 
such a degree that he was entire confine to his bed. the ice cracked 
behind us and was driven with force towards the north; a breeze sprang from 
the west, and on the 11th the passage towards the south became perfectly 
free. when the sailor saw this and that their return to their native 
country was apparently assured, a shout of tumultuous joy broke from them, 
loud and long-continued. frankenstein, who was dozing, awoke and ask the 
cause of the tumult. “they shout,” i said, “because they 
will soon return to england.” 

“do you, then, really return?” 

“alas! yes; i cannot withstand their demands. i cannot lead them 
unwilling to danger, and i must return.” 

“do so, if you will; but i will not. you may give up your purpose, but 
mine is assign to me by heaven, and i dare not. i am weak, but 
surely the spirits who assist my vengeance will endow me with 
sufficient strength.” saying this, he endeavour to spring from the 
bed, but the exertion was too great for him; he fell back and fainted. 

it was long before he was restored, and i often thought that life was 
entire extinct. at length he open his eyes; he breathe with 
difficulty and was unable to speak. the surgeon gave him a compose 
draught and ordered us to leave him undisturbed. in the meantime he 
told me that my friend had certainly not many hour to live. 

his sentence was pronounced, and i could only grieve and be patient. i sat 
by his bed, watch him; his eyes were closed, and i thought he slept; but 
presently he call to me in a feeble voice, and bidding me come near, 
said, “alas! the strength i rely on is gone; i feel that i shall 
soon die, and he, my enemy and persecutor, may still be in being. think 
not, walton, that in the last moments of my existence i feel that burning 
hatred and ardent desire of revenge i once expressed; but i feel myself 
justified in desire the death of my adversary. during these last days i 
have been occupy in examine my past conduct; nor do i find it blamable. 
in a fit of enthusiast madness i create a rational creature and was 
bound towards him to assure, as far as was in my power, his happiness and 
well-being. this was my duty, but there was another still paramount to 
that. my duty towards the being of my own species had greater claim to 
my attention because they included a greater proportion of happiness or 
misery. urge by this view, i refused, and i did right in refusing, to 
create a companion for the first creature. he show unparalleled malignity 
and selfish in evil; he destroy my friends; he devoted to destruction 
being who possessed exquisite sensations, happiness, and wisdom; nor do i 
know where this thirst for vengeance may end. miserable himself that he may 
render no other wretched, he ought to die. the task of his destruction was 
mine, but i have failed. when actuate by selfish and vicious motives, i 
ask you to undertake my unfinished work, and i renew this request now, 
when i am only induce by reason and virtue. 

“yet i cannot ask you to renounce your country and friends to fulfil 
this task; and now that you are return to england, you will have 
little chance of meeting with him. but the consideration of these 
points, and the well balance of what you may esteem your duties, i 
leave to you; my judgment and idea are already disturbed by the near 
approach of death. i dare not ask you to do what i think right, for i 
may still be misle by passion. 

“that he should live to be an instrument of mischief disturb me; in 
other respects, this hour, when i momentarily expect my release, is the 
only happy one which i have enjoy for several years. the form of 
the beloved dead flit before me, and i hasten to their arms. farewell, 
walton! seek happiness in tranquill and avoid ambition, even if it 
be only the apparently innocent one of distinguish yourself in 
science and discoveries. yet why do i say this? i have myself been 
blasted in these hopes, yet another may succeed.” 

his voice became faint as he spoke, and at length, exhaust by his 
effort, he sank into silence. about half an hour afterwards he 
attempt again to speak but was unable; he pressed my hand feebly, and 
his eyes closed for ever, while the irradiate of a gentle smile pass 
away from his lips. 

margaret, what comment can i make on the untimely extinction of this 
glorious spirit? what can i say that will enable you to understand the 
depth of my sorrow? all that i should express would be inadequate and 
feeble. my tear flow; my mind is overshadow by a cloud of 
disappointment. but i journey towards england, and i may there find 
consolation. 

i am interrupted. what do these sound portend? it is midnight; the 
breeze blow fairly, and the watch on deck scarcely stir. again there 
is a sound as of a human voice, but hoarser; it come from the cabin 
where the remains of frankenstein still lie. i must arise and examine. 
good night, my sister. 

great god! what a scene has just taken place! i am yet dizzy with the 
remembrance of it. i hardly know whether i shall have the power to detail 
it; yet the tale which i have record would be incomplete without this 
final and wonderful catastrophe. 

i enter the cabin where lay the remains of my ill-fated and admirable 
friend. over him hung a form which i cannot find words to 
describe—gigantic in stature, yet uncouth and distort in its 
proportions. as he hung over the coffin, his face was conceal by long 
locks of ragged hair; but one vast hand was extended, in colour and 
apparent texture like that of a mummy. when he heard the sound of my 
approach, he cease to utter exclamation of grief and horror and sprung 
towards the window. never did i behold a vision so horrible as his face, of 
such loathsome yet appalling hideousness. i shut my eyes involuntary and 
endeavour to recollect what were my duty with regard to this destroyer. 
i call on him to stay. 

he paused, look on me with wonder, and again turning towards the 
lifeless form of his creator, he seem to forget my presence, and 
every feature and gesture seem instigate by the wildest rage of some 
uncontrollable passion. 

“that is also my victim!” he exclaimed. “in his murder my 
crime are consummated; the miserable series of my being is wound to its 
close! oh, frankenstein! generous and self-devoted being! what do it 
avail that i now ask thee to pardon me? i, who irretrievable destroy thee 
by destroy all thou lovedst. alas! he is cold, he cannot answer 
me.” 

his voice seem suffocated, and my first impulses, which had suggest to 
me the duty of obey the die request of my friend in destroy his 
enemy, were now suspend by a mixture of curiosity and compassion. i 
approach this tremendous being; i dare not again raise my eyes to his 
face, there was something so scare and unearthly in his ugliness. i 
attempt to speak, but the words die away on my lips. the monster 
continue to utter wild and incoherent self-reproaches. at length i 
gather resolution to address him in a pause of the tempest of his passion. 

“your repentance,” i said, “is now superfluous. if you 
had listen to the voice of conscience and heed the sting of remorse 
before you had urge your diabolical vengeance to this extremity, 
frankenstein would yet have lived.” 

“and do you dream?” said the dæmon. “do you think that i was then 
dead to agony and remorse? he,” he continued, point to the corpse, 
“he suffer not in the consummation of the deed. oh! not the 
ten-thousandth portion of the anguish that was mine during the 
lingering detail of its execution. a frightful selfish hurried me 
on, while my heart was poison with remorse. think you that the 
groan of clerval were music to my ears? my heart was fashion to be 
susceptible of love and sympathy, and when wrench by misery to vice 
and hatred, it did not endure the violence of the change without 
torture such as you cannot even imagine. 

“after the murder of clerval i return to switzerland, heart-broken 
and overcome. i pity frankenstein; my pity amount to horror; i 
abhor myself. but when i discover that he, the author at once of 
my existence and of its unspeakable torments, dare to hope for 
happiness, that while he accumulate wretched and despair upon me 
he sought his own enjoyment in feelings and passion from the 
indulgence of which i was for ever barred, then impotent envy and bitter 
indignation fill me with an insatiable thirst for vengeance. i 
recollect my threat and resolve that it should be accomplished. i 
knew that i was prepare for myself a deadly torture, but i was the 
slave, not the master, of an impulse which i detest yet could not 
disobey. yet when she died! nay, then i was not miserable. i had 
cast off all feeling, subdued all anguish, to riot in the excess of my 
despair. evil thenceforth became my good. urge thus far, i had no 
choice but to adapt my nature to an element which i had willing 
chosen. the completion of my demoniacal design became an insatiable 
passion. and now it is ended; there is my last victim!” 

i was at first touched by the expression of his misery; yet, when i call 
to mind what frankenstein had said of his powers of eloquence and 
persuasion, and when i again cast my eyes on the lifeless form of my 
friend, indignation was rekindle within me. “wretch!” i said. 
“it is well that you come here to whine over the desolate that you 
have made. you throw a torch into a pile of buildings, and when they are 
consumed, you sit among the ruins and lament the fall. hypocrite fiend! 
if he whom you mourn still lived, still would he be the object, again would 
he become the prey, of your accursed vengeance. it is not pity that you 
feel; you lament only because the victim of your malignity is withdrawn 
from your power.” 

“oh, it is not thus—not thus,” interrupt the being. 
“yet such must be the impression convey to you by what appear to 
be the purport of my actions. yet i seek not a fellow feeling in my misery. 
no sympathy may i ever find. when i first sought it, it was the love of 
virtue, the feelings of happiness and affection with which my whole being 
overflowed, that i wish to be participated. but now that virtue has 
become to me a shadow, and that happiness and affection are turn into 
bitter and loathing despair, in what should i seek for sympathy? i am 
content to suffer alone while my suffering shall endure; when i die, i am 
well satisfy that abhorrence and opprobrium should load my memory. once 
my fancy was soothe with dream of virtue, of fame, and of enjoyment. once 
i false hope to meet with being who, pardon my outward form, would 
love me for the excellent quality which i was capable of unfolding. i was 
nourish with high thought of honour and devotion. but now crime has 
degrade me beneath the meanest animal. no guilt, no mischief, no 
malignity, no misery, can be found comparable to mine. when i run over the 
frightful catalogue of my sins, i cannot believe that i am the same 
creature whose thought were once fill with sublime and transcendent 
vision of the beauty and the majesty of goodness. but it is even so; the 
fallen angel become a malignant devil. yet even that enemy of god and man 
had friends and associate in his desolation; i am alone. 

“you, who call frankenstein your friend, seem to have a knowledge of my 
crime and his misfortunes. but in the detail which he gave you of them 
he could not sum up the hour and month of misery which i endure 
waste in impotent passions. for while i destroy his hopes, i did 
not satisfy my own desires. they were for ever ardent and craving; still 
i desire love and fellowship, and i was still spurned. was there no 
injustice in this? am i to be thought the only criminal, when all 
humankind sin against me? why do you not hate felix, who drove his 
friend from his door with contumely? why do you not execrate the rustic 
who sought to destroy the saviour of his child? nay, these are virtuous 
and immaculate beings! i, the miserable and the abandoned, am an 
abortion, to be spurn at, and kicked, and trample on. even now my 
blood boil at the recollection of this injustice. 

“but it is true that i am a wretch. i have murder the lovely and 
the helpless; i have strangle the innocent as they slept and grasp to 
death his throat who never injure me or any other living thing. i have 
devoted my creator, the select specimen of all that is worthy of love and 
admiration among men, to misery; i have pursue him even to that 
irremediable ruin. there he lies, white and cold in death. you hate me, but 
your abhorrence cannot equal that with which i regard myself. i look on the 
hand which execute the deed; i think on the heart in which the 
imagination of it was conceive and long for the moment when these hand 
will meet my eyes, when that imagination will haunt my thought no more. 

“fear not that i shall be the instrument of future mischief. my work 
is nearly complete. neither yours nor any man’s death is need to 
consummate the series of my being and accomplish that which must be done, 
but it require my own. do not think that i shall be slow to perform this 
sacrifice. i shall quit your vessel on the ice raft which brought me 
thither and shall seek the most northern extremity of the globe; i shall 
collect my funeral pile and consume to ashes this miserable frame, that its 
remains may afford no light to any curious and unhallowed wretch who would 
create such another as i have been. i shall die. i shall no long feel the 
agony which now consume me or be the prey of feelings unsatisfied, yet 
unquenched. he is dead who call me into being; and when i shall be no 
more, the very remembrance of us both will speedy vanish. i shall no 
long see the sun or stars or feel the wind play on my cheeks. light, 
feeling, and sense will pass away; and in this condition must i find my 
happiness. some years ago, when the image which this world afford first 
open upon me, when i felt the cheering warmth of summer and heard the 
rustling of the leaves and the warble of the birds, and these were all to 
me, i should have wept to die; now it is my only consolation. pollute by 
crime and torn by the bitterest remorse, where can i find rest but in 
death? 

“farewell! i leave you, and in you the last of humankind whom these 
eyes will ever behold. farewell, frankenstein! if thou wert yet alive 
and yet cherish a desire of revenge against me, it would be better 
satiate in my life than in my destruction. but it was not so; thou 
didst seek my extinction, that i might not cause greater wretchedness; 
and if yet, in some mode unknown to me, thou hadst not cease to think 
and feel, thou wouldst not desire against me a vengeance greater than 
that which i feel. blasted as thou wert, my agony was still superior to 
thine, for the bitter sting of remorse will not cease to rankle in my 
wound until death shall close them for ever. 

“but soon,” he cry with sad and solemn enthusiasm, “i 
shall die, and what i now feel be no long felt. soon these burning 
misery will be extinct. i shall ascend my funeral pile triumphant and 
exult in the agony of the torture flames. the light of that conflagration 
will fade away; my ashes will be swept into the sea by the winds. my spirit 
will sleep in peace, or if it thinks, it will not surely think thus. 
farewell.” 

he sprang from the cabin-window as he said this, upon the ice raft 
which lay close to the vessel. he was soon borne away by the wave and 
lost in dark and distance. 




end of the project gutenberg ebook of frankenstein, by 
mary wollstonecraft (godwin) shelley
